CHAPTER VII

UPANAYANA

This word literally means ’leading or taking near’. But the important question is ’near what’? It appears that it originally meant ’taking near the {{ÄcÄrya|ÄcÄrya}}’ (for instruction); it may have also meant ‘introducing the novice to the stage of student-hood’. Some of the {{gá¹›hyasÅ«tras|gá¹›hyasÅ«tras}} bring out this sense clearly e. g. the {{Hir. gá¹›.|Hiraá¹‡yakeÅ›i-gá¹›.}} I. 5. 2 1 says “The teacher then makes the boy utter ‘I have come unto {{brahmacarya|brahmacarya}}. Lead me near (initiate me into it). Let me be a student,- impelled by the god {{Savitr|Savitá¹›}}’”. The {{MÄnava|MÄnava}} and {{KÄá¹­haka gá¹›.|KÄá¹­haka-gá¹›.}} (41.1) also use the word {{upÄyana|upÄyana}} for {{upanayana|upanayana}} and {{Ä€dityadarÅ›ana|Ä€dityadarÅ›ana}} on {{KÄá¹­haka gá¹›.|KÄá¹­haka-gá¹›.}} (41. 1) says that {{upÄnaya|upÄnaya}}, {{upanayana|upanayana}}, {{mauÃ±jÄ«bandhana|mauÃ±jÄ«bandhana}}, {{baá¹­ukaraá¹‡a|baá¹­ukaraá¹‡a}}, {{vratabandha|vratabandha}} are synonyms.

  • à¤…à¤¥à¥ˆà¤¨à¤®à¥ {{à¤…à¤­à¤¿à¤§à¥à¤¯à¤¾à¤¹à¤¾à¤°à¤¯à¤¤à¤¿|à¤…à¤­à¤¿à¤µà¥à¤¯à¤¾à¤¹à¤¾à¤°à¤¯à¤¤à¤¿}} à¥¤ à¤¬à¥à¤°à¤¹à¥à¤®à¤šà¤°à¥à¤¯à¤®à¥ à¤†à¤—à¤¾à¤®à¥, à¤‰à¤ª à¤®à¤¾ à¤¨à¤¯à¤¸à¥à¤µ, à¤¬à¥à¤°à¤¹à¥à¤®à¤šà¤¾à¤°à¥€ à¤­à¤µà¤¾à¤¨à¤¿ à¤¦à¥‡à¤µà¥‡à¤¨ à¤¸à¤µà¤¿à¤¤à¥à¤°à¤¾ à¤ªà¥à¤°à¤¸à¥‚à¤¤à¤ƒ à¥¤ â€” {{à¤¹à¤¿à¤°à¤£à¥à¤¯. à¤—à¥ƒ.|à¤¹à¤¿à¤°à¤£à¥à¤¯à¤•à¥‡à¤¶à¤¿-à¤—à¥ƒ.}} I.5.2 (S.B.E. vol 30 p. 150); à¤¬à¥à¤°à¤¹à¥à¤®à¤šà¤°à¥à¤¯à¤®à¥ à¤†à¤—à¤¾à¤®à¥ à¤‡à¤¤à¤¿ à¤µà¤¾à¤šà¤¯à¤¤à¤¿, à¤¬à¥à¤°à¤¹à¥à¤®à¤šà¤¾à¤°à¥€ à¤…à¤¸à¤¾à¤¨à¤¿ à¤‡à¤¤à¤¿ à¤š à¥¤ â€” {{à¤ªà¤¾à¤°.à¤—à¥ƒ.|à¤ªà¤¾à¤°à¤¸à¥à¤•à¤°-à¤—à¥ƒ.}} II.2; {{à¤—à¥‹à¤­à¤¿à¤²à¤—à¥ƒà¤¹à¥à¤¯|à¤—à¥‹à¤­à¤¿à¤²-à¤—à¥ƒà¤¹à¥à¤¯}} II.10.21 (S.B.E. vol. 30 p. 65). The phrases à¤¬à¥à¤°à¤¹à¥à¤®à¤šà¤°à¥à¤¯à¤®à¥ à¤†à¤—à¤¾à¤®à¥ and à¤¬à¥à¤°à¤¹à¥à¤®à¤šà¤¾à¤°à¥€ à¤…à¤¸à¤¾à¤¨à¤¿ occur in the {{à¤¶à¤¤à¤ªà¤¥|à¤¶à¤¤à¤ªà¤¥-à¤¬à¥à¤°à¤¾à¤¹à¥à¤®à¤£}} XI.5.4.1; vide {{à¤†à¤ª.à¤®.à¤ªà¤¾.|à¤†à¤ªà¤¸à¥à¤¤à¤®à¥à¤¬-à¤®à¤¨à¥à¤¤à¥à¤°-à¤ªà¤¾à¤ }} II.3.26 for à¤¬à¥à¤°à¤¹à¥à¤®à¤šà¤°à¥à¤¯… (or if fire is not available) to the sun, to the moon, to the wind or into waters. This hymn thus brings out most of the characteristic features of the {{brahmacÄri|brahmacÄrÄ«}} and of upanayana ( viz. deerskin, {{mekhalÄ|mekhalÄ}}, offering of {{samidh|samidh}}, begging and a life of hard work and restraint). From the reference to the beard and from the words ’this man ({{ayam puruá¹£aá¸¥|ayam puruá¹£aá¸¥}})’ occurring in the Atharvaveda VIII. 1. 1 and elsewhere it appears probable that upanayana was performed rather later in those ancient days than in the days of the sÅ«tras.

In the {{Tai. Br.|Tai. Br.}} III.15 10. 11 there is the story of {{BharadvÄja|BharadvÄja}} who remained a {{brahmacÄrÄ«|brahmacÄrÄ«}} for three parts of his life ( i. e. till 75 ) and to whom Indra said that in all that long period of brahmacarya he had mastered only an insignificant portion ( three handfuls out of three mountains) of the Vedas, which were endless in extent. The story of {{NÄbhÄnediá¹£á¹­ha|NÄbhÄnediá¹£á¹­ha}}, son of {{Manu|Manu}}, who was excluded from ancestral property at a partition made by his brothers, narrated in the {{Ait. Br.|Ait. Br.}} shows16 that he was a {{brahmacÄri|brahmacÄrÄ«}} staying with a teacher away from his father’s place. The {{Åšat. Br.|Åšat. Br.}} XI. 5. 4, contains many and almost complete details about the life of {{brahmacÄrins|brahmacÄrins}} which bear a very close similarity to those taught in the gá¹›hya sÅ«tras. A brief summary is set out below. The boy says ‘I have come unto brahmacarya’ and ’let me be a {{brahmacÄri|brahmacÄrÄ«}}’. Then the teacher asks him ’ what is your name{{’|?}}’; then the teacher takes him near ({{upanayati|upanayati}}); the teacher takes hold of the boy’s hand with the words ’ you are the {{brahmacÄrÄ«|brahmacÄrÄ«}} of Indra; Agni is your teacher, I am your teacher, N. N. (addressing the boy by his name)’. Then he consigns the boy to (the care of ) the elements. The teacher instructs him ’ drink water, do work (in the teacher’s house ), put a fuel stick (on the fire), do not sleep (by day). He repeats the {{SÄvitrÄ«|SÄvitrÄ«}} (mantra sacred to Savitá¹›). Formerly it was repeated a year (after the boy came as a {{brahmacÄrÄ«|brahmacÄrÄ«}}), then at the end of six months, 24 days, 12 days, 3 days; but one should repeat to the {{brÄhmaá¹‡a|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} boy the verse at once ( on the very day of upanayana ); the teacher repeats it to him first each {{pÄda|pÄda}} separately, then the half and then the whole. Being a {{brahmacÄri|brahmacÄrÄ«}} one should not eat honey.2

A few words about the origin and development of this most important {{saá¹skÄra|saá¹skÄra}} would not be out of place here. Comparison with the ancient Zoroastrian scriptures (vide S. B. E. vol. V. pp. 285-290 about the sacred girdle and shirt) and the modern practices among the Parsis of India tend to show that {{Upanayana|Upanayana}} goes back to an Indo-Iranian origin. But that subject is outside the scope of this work. Confining ourselves to Indian Literature, we find that already in the {{á¹šg.|á¹šgveda}} X. 109.5 the word ‘{{brahmacÄri|brahmacÄrÄ«}}’ occurs ‘oh gods! he ({{Bá¹›haspati|Bá¹›haspati}}), all pervading one, moves as a {{brahmacÄrÄ«|brahmacÄrÄ«}} pervading all (sacrifices); he is only one part of the gods (i. e. of sacrifices); {{Bá¹›haspati|Bá¹›haspati}} secured by that (i. e. by his service to the gods) a wife (me who am named) {{JuhÅ«|JuhÅ«}}, who was (formerly) taken by {{Soma|Soma}}’.17 The word ‘{{upanayana|upanayana}}’18 can be derived and explained in two ways: (1) taking (the boy) near the {{ÄcÄrya|ÄcÄrya}}, (2) that rite by which the boy is taken to the {{ÄcÄrya|ÄcÄrya}}. The first sense appears to have been the original one and when an extensive ritual came to be associated with {{upanayana|upanayana}} the second came to be the sense of the word. Such an ancient work as the {{Ä€p. Dh. S.|Ä€p.Dh.S.}} I.1.1.19 says that {{upanayana|upanayana}} is a {{saá¹skÄra|saá¹skÄra}} (purificatory rite) laid down by revelation for him who seeks learning (i. e. it accepts the second explanation) or this sÅ«tra may mean ‘it is a {{saá¹skÄra|saá¹skÄra}} brought about by imparting the {{Å›ruti|Å›ruti}} (viz. {{GÄyatrÄ«|GÄyatrÄ«}} mantra) to him who seeks learning’. This would mean that {{upanayana|upanayana}} principally is {{gÄyatryupadeÅ›a|gÄyatryupadeÅ›a}} ({{tho|the}} imparting of the sacred {{GÄyatri|GÄyatrÄ«}} mantra). This appears to be {{suggestod|suggested}} by the Vedic passage quoted above (p.154 f. n. 356) ‘he created the {{brÄhmaá¹‡a|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} with {{GÄyatrÄ«|GÄyatrÄ«}}, the {{ká¹£atriya|ká¹£atriya}} with {{Triá¹£á¹­ubh|Triá¹£á¹­ubh}}, the {{vaiÅ›ya|vaiÅ›ya}} with {{JagatÄ«|JagatÄ«}}’, and by {{KÄtyÄyana Åšrauta|KÄtyÄyana-Å›rautasÅ«tra}} ‘one should initiate the {{brÄhmaá¹‡a|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} with {{GÄyatrÄ«|GÄyatrÄ«}}’. {{JaiminË†|Jaimini}}19 also (in VI. 1.35) propounds the view that {{upanayana|upanayana}} is a {{saá¹skÄra|saá¹skÄra}} and has a seen result viz. thereby the boy is brought near the teacher for learning {{VedaË†|Veda}}.

The reference to {{Soma|Soma}} recalls {{á¹šg.|á¹šgveda}} X.85.45 ‘à¤¸à¥‹à¤®à¥‹ {{à¤¦à¤¦à¤¦à¥à¤°à¥à¤¨à¥à¤§à¤°à¥à¤µà¤¾à¤¯|à¤¦à¤¦à¤¦à¥ à¤—à¤¨à¥à¤§à¤°à¥à¤µà¤¾à¤¯}}.’ Every girl was supposed to have been under the protection of {{Soma|Soma}}, {{Gandharva|Gandharva}} and {{Agni|Agni}} before her marriage with a human bridegroom.

  • à¤¸à¤‚à¤¸à¥à¤•à¤¾à¤°à¤¸à¥à¤¯ à¤¤à¤¦à¤°à¥à¤¥à¤¤à¥à¤µà¤¾à¤¦à¥ à¤µà¤¿à¤¦à¥à¤¯à¤¾à¤¯à¤¾à¤‚ {{à¤ªà¥à¤°à¤·à¤¶à¥à¤°à¥à¤¤à¤¿:|à¤ªà¥à¤°à¥à¤·à¤¶à¥à¤°à¥à¤¤à¤¿à¤ƒ}} à¥¤ â€” à¤œà¥ˆà¤®à¤¿à¤¨à¤¿ VI.1.35; ‘à¤µà¤¿à¤¦à¥à¤¯à¤¾à¤¯à¤¾à¤®à¥ à¤à¤µ à¤à¤·à¤¾ à¤¶à¥à¤°à¥à¤¤à¤¿à¤ƒ (à¤µà¤¸à¤¨à¥à¤¤à¥‡ à¤¬à¥à¤°à¤¾à¤¹à¥à¤®à¤£à¤®à¥ à¤‰à¤ªà¤¨à¤¯à¥€à¤¤) à¥¤ à¤‰à¤ªà¤¨à¤¯à¤¨à¤¸à¥à¤¯ à¤¸à¤‚à¤¸à¥à¤•à¤¾à¤°à¤¸à¥à¤¯ à¤¤à¤¦à¤°à¥à¤¥à¤¤à¥à¤µà¤¾à¤¤à¥ à¥¤ à¤µà¤¿à¤¦à¥à¤¯à¤¾-à¤…à¤°à¥à¤¥à¤®à¥ {{à¤‰à¤ªà¤¾à¤¨à¥à¤¯à¤¾à¤¯à¤¸à¥à¤¯|à¤‰à¤ªà¤¾à¤§à¥à¤¯à¤¾à¤¯à¤¸à¥à¤¯}} à¤¸à¤®à¥€à¤ªà¤‚ à¤¨à¥€à¤¯à¤¤à¥‡, à¤¨ à¤…à¤¦à¥ƒà¤·à¥à¤Ÿ-à¤…à¤°à¥à¤¥à¤‚, à¤¨-à¤…à¤ªà¤¿ à¤•à¤Ÿà¤‚ à¤•à¥à¤¡à¥à¤¯à¤‚ à¤µà¤¾ à¤•à¤°à¥à¤¤à¥à¤®à¥ à¥¤ à¤¦à¥ƒà¤·à¥à¤Ÿ-à¤…à¤°à¥à¤¥à¤®à¥ à¤à¤µ à¤¸à¤¾ à¤à¤·à¤¾ à¤µà¤¿à¤¦à¥à¤¯à¤¾à¤¯à¤¾à¤‚ à¤ªà¥à¤°à¥à¤·-à¤¶à¥à¤°à¥à¤¤à¤¿à¤ƒ à¥¤ à¤•à¤¥à¤®à¥ à¤…à¤µà¤—à¤®à¥à¤¯à¤¤à¥‡ ? à¥¤ à¤¸à¤¦à¥à¤¯à¥‹ à¤¹ à¤µà¤¾ {{à¤¤à¥à¤µà¤¾à¤µ|à¤¤à¤¾à¤µà¤¦à¥}} à¤¬à¥à¤°à¤¾à¤¹à¥à¤®à¤£à¤¾à¤¯ à¤…à¤¨à¥à¤¬à¥à¤°à¥‚à¤¯à¤¾à¤¦à¥ à¤†à¤—à¥à¤¨à¥‡à¤¯à¥‹ à¤µà¥ˆ à¤¬à¥à¤°à¤¾à¤¹à¥à¤®à¤£à¤ƒ à¥¤ …… à¤¤à¤‚ à¤µà¥ˆ à¤ªà¤šà¥à¤›à¥‹à¤½à¤¨à¥à¤µà¤¾à¤¹ ……à¤…à¤¥ à¤…à¤°à¥à¤§à¤°à¥à¤šà¤¶à¥‹à¤½à¤¨à¥à¤µà¤¾à¤¹…..à¤…à¤¥ à¤•à¥ƒà¤¤à¥à¤¸à¥à¤¨à¤¾à¤®à¥ à¥¤ à¤¤à¤¦à¥ à¤†à¤¹à¥à¤ƒ à¥¤ à¤¨ à¤¬à¥à¤°à¤¹à¥à¤®à¤šà¤¾à¤°à¥€ à¤¸à¤¨à¥ à¤®à¤§à¥à¤µà¤¶à¥à¤¨à¥€à¤¯à¤¾à¤¤à¥ ……à¥¤ à¤¶à¤¤à¤ªà¤¥ XI. 5.4.1-17.

The word ‘{{antevÄsÄ«|antevÄsÄ«}}’ (lit. one who dwells near a teacher ) {{ooours|occurs}} in the {{Åšaá¹­. Br.|Åšat. Br.}} V.1.5.17 and in the {{Tai. Up.|Tai. Up.}} I. 11. The {{Åšaá¹­. Br.|Åšat. Br.}} (XI. 3. 3. 2) says20 ‘he who takes to brahmacarya indeed takes upon himself a sacrificial session of long duration.’ It further says (XI. 3. 3. 3-6 ) that the boy when entering upon {{studentbood|studenthood}} approaches giving a fourth part of himself to Agni, Death, the teacher and himself and that by the offering of {{samidh|samidh}} ( to fire), by begging {{an’|and}} by doing work in the teacher’s house respectively he secures freedom from the action of the first three. It also says that after one finishes studenthood and takes the ceremonial bath one should not beg. Vide Gopatha Br. (ed. by {{Gastra|Gaastra}}) 2, 3 and Baud. Dh. S. I. 2. 53. The {{Åšat. Br.|Åšat. Br.}} (III. 6. 2. 15 ) further says ’therefore {{brahmachÄrins|brahmachÄrins}} protect the teacher, his house and his cattle, with the idea that otherwise he might be taken away from them’.21

Janamejaya {{PÄriká¹£ita|PÄriká¹£ita}} asks the {{haá¹sas|haá¹sas}} ( who were the {{Ä€havanÄ«ya|Ä€havanÄ«ya}} and {{Daká¹£iá¹‡Ä|Daká¹£i à¤†à¤šà¤¾à¤°à¥à¤¯-à¤•à¤°à¤£à¤®à¥ à¤à¤¤à¤¦à¥ à¤…à¤µà¤—à¤®à¥à¤¯à¤¤à¥‡ à¥¤ à¤•à¥à¤¤à¤ƒ? à¤†à¤¤à¥à¤®à¤¨à¥‡à¤ªà¤¦-à¤¦à¤°à¥à¤¶à¤¨à¤¾à¤¤à¥ à¥¤’ â€” à¤¶à¤¬à¤°à¤ƒ à¥¤

{{á¹šg.|á¹šgveda}} III. 8. 422 is a verse that clearly indicates that some of the characteristics of {{upanayana|upanayana}} described in the {{gá¹›hya sÅ«tras|gá¹›hyasÅ«tras}} were well known even then. There the sacrificial post ({{yÅ«pa|yÅ«pa}}) is praised as a young person (i. e. in the language of the later classical rhetoricians there is the first kind of the figure {{AtiÅ›ayokti|AtiÅ›ayokti}}) ‘here comes the youth, well dressed and encircled (the boy by his {{mekhalÄ|mekhalÄ}} and the post by its {{raÅ›anÄ|raÅ›anÄ}}); he, when born, attains eminence; wise sages, full of devotion to the gods in their hearts and entertaining happy thoughts, raise him up’. Here in ‘{{unnayanti|unnayanti}}’ we have the same root that we have in {{upanayana|upanayana}}. This mantra is employed in the {{upanayana|upanayana}} by several {{gá¹›hya sÅ«tras|gá¹›hyasÅ«tras}}. e.g. {{Ä€Å›v.|Ä€Å›v.}} I. 20. 8 (who employs it for making the boy circumambulate) and {{PÄr.|PÄr.}} II. 2. (prescribes that the mantra is to be recited when tying the girdle round the boy’s waist). In the {{Tai. S.|Tai.S.}} VI.23 3. 10.5 we have the famous passage referring to the three debts where the words ‘{{brahmacÄrÄ«|brahmacÄrÄ«}}’ and ‘{{brahmacarya|brahmacarya}}’ occur. ‘Every {{brÄhmaá¹‡a|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} when born is indebted in three debts viz. in {{brahmacarya|brahmacarya}} to the sages, in sacrifice to the gods and in offspring to the manes; he indeed becomes free from debts who has a son, who sacrifices and who dwells (with the teacher) as a {{brahmacÄrÄ«|brahmacÄrÄ«}}.’

  • à¤¯à¥à¤µà¤¾ à¤¸à¥à¤µà¤¾à¤¸à¤¾à¤ƒ à¤ªà¤°à¤¿à¤µà¥€à¤¤ à¤†à¤—à¤¾à¤¤à¥, à¤¸ à¤‰ à¤¶à¥à¤°à¥‡à¤¯à¤¾à¤¨à¥ à¤­à¤µà¤¤à¤¿ à¤œà¤¾à¤¯à¤®à¤¾à¤¨à¤ƒ à¥¤ à¤¤à¤‚ à¤§à¥€à¤°à¤¾à¤¸à¤ƒ à¤•à¤µà¤¯ à¤‰à¤¨à¥à¤¨à¤¯à¤¨à¥à¤¤à¤¿ à¤¸à¥à¤µà¤¾à¤§à¥à¤¯à¥‹ à¤®à¤¨à¤¸à¤¾ à¤¦à¥‡à¤µà¤¯à¤¨à¥à¤¤à¤ƒ à¥¥ â€” {{à¤‹.|á¹‡Ä}} fires ) ‘what is holy{{’|?}}’ and the latter reply ‘It is brahmacarya’(vide Gopatha Br. 2.5). Gopatha (2.5) further says ’the period of studenthood for the mastery of all Vedas is 48 years,which, being distributed among the Vedas in four parts, makes brahmacarya last for 12 years, which is the lowest limit; one should practise brahmacarya according to one’s ability before taking the ceremonial bath.’3 The same work says that the {{brahmacÄri|brahmacÄrÄ«}} should fetch samidhs every day for worshipping fire and beg and that if he does not do so continuously for seven days he has to undergo upanayana again (2. 6) and that the lady of the house should daily give alms to a {{brahmacÄrin|brahmacÄrin}} with the idea that he may not deprive her of her wealth, of merit due to {{iá¹£á¹­Äpurta|iá¹£á¹­Äpurta}}. It also says that a {{brahmacÄri|brahmacÄrÄ«}} should not sleep on a cot, should not engage in singing and dancing, should not roam about, should not spit about nor go to a cemetery.4

The Moon and Jupiter must be astrologically strong with reference to the boy’s horoscope. The rule about Jupiter probably arose from the fact that Jupiter was supposed to rule over knowledge and happiness and as upanayana was meant to be the entrance for Veda-study, Jupiter’s benevolent aspect was thought to be necessary. Jupiter when in the 2nd, 5th, 7th, 9th, 11th zodiacal sign from the sign of birth (calculation to be made inclusive of the sign of birth ) is auspicious, when in the 1st, 3rd, 6th, 1Ändogya}} (VIII. 11. 3) speaks of {{Indra’s|Indra’s}} {{brahmacarya|brahmacarya}} for 101 years and {{ChÄndogya|ChÄndogya}} II. 23. 1. speaks of {{brahmacarya|brahmacarya}} for life.

We shall now turn to {{upanayana|upanayana}} as described0th place from the sign of birth it is auspicious after the performance of a propitiatory homa and when it is in the 4th, 8th, 12th place from birth, it is inauspicious. The moon is supposed to be malefic when she is in the 2nd, 4th, 5th, 8 in the {{sÅ«tras|sÅ«tras}} and {{smá¹›tis|smá¹›tis}}. The following matters fall to be treated under {{Upanayana|Upanayana}}: The proper age for {{upanayana|upanayana}}, the auspicious seasons for it; the skin, the garments, the girdle and the staff for the {{brahmacÄrins|brahmacÄrins}} of different {{vará¹‡Äs|vará¹‡as}}; the {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}}; the preliminaries of {{upanayana|upanayana}}, such as {{homa|homa}}, taking of curds by the boy, {{aÃ±jalipÅ«raá¹‡a|aÃ±jalipÅ«raá¹‡a}}, {{aÅ›mÄrohana|aÅ›mÄrohaá¹‡a}}; the principal rites of {{upanayana|upanayana}} viz. takingth, 9th or 12th place from the sign of birth. Four hours from sunrise is the best for upanayana, from that time to noon is middling and afternoon is prohibited. Some said that a person’s upanayana should not be performed in the month in which he was born, others restricted the prohibition to the {{paká¹£a|paká¹£a}} ( the half) of the month in which he was born. There are other rules about the Lagna (the rising zodiacal sign at the time of upanayana ) {{whioh|which}} are not set out here. There are other prohibited astrological conjunctions like {{VyatÄ«pÄta|VyatÄ«pÄta}}, {{Vaidhá¹›ti|V of the student’s hand by the teacher, touching the chest of the student expressive of acceptance as pupil, handing the boy to {{Savitá¹›|Savitá¹›}} and other gods ({{paridÄna|paridÄna}}), instruction in the duties of the student (such as putting fuel on fire, begging &c.), instruction in the famous {{SÄvitrÄ«|SÄaidhá¹›ti}} &c. For detailed rules on these, works like the {{Saá¹. Pr.|Saá¹. Pr.}} (pp. 355-385), {{Nirá¹‡ayasindhu|Nirá¹‡ayasindhu}} and {{Dharmasindhu|Dharmasindhu}} may be consulted.

  • à¤†à¤ªà¥‚à¤°à¥à¤¯à¤®à¤¾à¤£-à¤ªà¤•à¥à¤·à¥‡ à¤ªà¥à¤£à¥à¤¯à¥‡ à¤¨à¤•à¥à¤·à¤¤à¥à¤°à¥‡, à¤µà¤¿à¤¶à¥‡à¤·à¥‡à¤£ à¤ªà¥à¤‚-à¤¨à¤¾à¤®à¤§à¥‡à¤¯à¥‡ à¥¤ â€” {{à¤­à¤¾à¤°à¤¦à¥à¤µà¤¾à¤œ|BhÄradvÄjaá¹£atriya}}, of {{badara|badara}} or {{udumbara|udumbara}} wood for a {{vaiÅ›ya|vaiÅ›ya}}; while some teachers say that the staff should be made of a tree (which is used in sacrifices ) without reference to any {{vará¹‡a|vará¹‡a}}. {{Gaut|Gaut}}. (I. 21 ) and Baud. Dh. S. (II. 5. 17 ) say that the staff of {{palÄÅ›a|palÄÅ›a}} or {{bilva|bilva}} should be used for {{brÄhmaá¹‡a|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} and {{Gaut|Gaut}}. (I. 22-23 ) says that {{aÅ›vattha|aÅ›vattha}} and {{pÄ«lu|pÄ«lu}} wood staff should be used respectively for {{ká¹£atriya|ká¹£atriya}} and {{vaiÅ›ya|vaiÅ›ya}} or}} I. 1. For {{à¤ªà¥à¤‚à¤¨à¤¾à¤®à¤§à¥‡à¤¯à¤¨à¤•à¥à¤·à¤¤à¥à¤°|puá¹-nÄmadheya-naká¹£atra}} see note 512.

Later works introduced very intricate rules about the proper months, tithis, days and times for {{upanayana|upanayana}}. It is neither possible nor very necessary to go into these astrological details. But a few words must be said as in modern times {{upanayana|upanayana}} is performed only in accordance with these rules. {{Vá¹›ddhagÄrgya|Vá¹›ddhagÄrgya}}34 laid down that six months from {{MÄgha|MÄgha}} were the proper months for {{upanayana|upanayana}}, while others say that five months from {{MÄgha|MÄgha}} are the proper ones. Then 1st, 4th, 7th, 8th, 9th, 13th, 14 of any sacrificial tree for all {{vará¹‡as|vará¹‡as}}. Baud. gá¹›. prescribes {{nyagrodha|nyagrodha}} or {{rauhitaka|rauhitaka}} for {{ká¹£atriya|ká¹£atriya}}, and {{badara|badara}} or {{udumbara|udumbara}} for {{vaiÅ›ya|vaiÅ›ya}}. {{PÄr. gá¹›.|PÄr. gá¹›.}} (II. 5 ) recommends a staff of {{palÄÅ›a|palÄÅ›a}}, {{bilva|bilva}} and {{udumbara|udumbara}} respectively for the three {{vará¹‡as|vará¹‡as}} (or any of these for all ). {{KÄá¹­haka|KÄá¹­haka}} gá¹›. (41.22)recommends {{palÄÅ›a|palÄÅ›a}}, {{aÅ›vattha|aÅ›vattha}} and {{nyagrodth, 15th i. e. (full moon and new moon) tithis are generally avoided (though there are some counter {{exoeptions|exceptions}} which are not set out here). It is stated that {{upanayana|upanayana}} should not be performed when Venus is so near the sun that it cannot be seen, when the sun is in the first degree of any zodiacal sign, on {{anadhyaya|anadhyÄya}} days and on {{galagraha|galagraha}}35 (the tithis specified above). Jupiter, Venus, Mars36 and Mercury are respectively the presiding deities of the {{á¹šgveda|á¹šgveda}} and the other Vedas. Therefore the {{upanayana|upanayana}} of those who have to study these Vedas should be performed on the weekha|nyagrodha}} respectively for the three. Manu (II. 45) prescribes {{bilva|bilva}} and {{palÄÅ›a|palÄÅ›a}} for {{brÄhmaá¹‡a|brÄhmaá¹‡a}}, {{vaá¹­a|vaá¹­a}} and {{khadira|khadira}} for {{ká¹£atriya|ká¹£atriya}}, {{pÄ«lu|pÄ«lu}} and {{udumbara|udumbara}} for {{vaiÅ›ya|vaiÅ›ya}}, and {{Kulluka|KullÅ«ka}} adds that two staffs should be used by the boy, as two are recommended in a compound.

The staff was required for support, for controlling the cattle of the teacher ( which the student was to tend ), for protection when going out at night and for guidance when entering a river or the like.41

The length of the staff varied according to the {{vará¹‡a|vará¹‡a}} of the boy. Ä€Å›v. gá¹›. I. 19. 13, {{Gaut|Gaut}}. I. 25, {{Vas. Dh. S.|Vas. Dh. S.}} (XI. 55-57), {{PÄr. gá¹›.|PÄr. gá¹›.}} (II. 5), Manu ( II. 46 ) prescribe that in the case of the {{brÄhmaras|naká¹£atras}} with respect to those who follow a particular Veda (which are passed over). One rule is that all {{naká¹£atras|naká¹£atras}} except {{Bharaá¹‡i|Bharaá¹‡i}}, {{Ká¹›ttikÄ|Ká¹›ttikÄ}}, {{MaghÄ|MaghÄ}}, {{ViÅ›ÄkhÄ|ViÅ›ÄkhÄ}}, {{Jyeá¹£á¹­hÄ|Jyeá¹£á¹­hÄ}}, {{ÅšatatÄrakÄ|ÅšatatÄrakÄ}} are good for all. The Moon and Jupiter must be astrologically strong with reference to the boy’s horoscope. The rule about Jupiter probably arose from the fact that Jupiter was supposed to rule over knowledge and happiness and as {{upanayana|upanayana}} was meant to be the entrance for Veda-study, Jupiter’s benevolent aspect was thought to be necessary. If Jupiter and Venus are not to be seen owing to nearness to the sun, {{upanayana|upaná¹‡a|brÄhmaá¹‡a}}, {{ká¹£atriya|ká¹£atriya}} or {{vaiÅ›ya|vaiÅ›ya}} boy respectively the staff should be as high as his head, forehead or the tip of the nose. The {{ÅšÄá¹…. gá¹›.|ÅšÄá¹…. gá¹›.}} ( II. 1. 21-23, S. B. E. vol. 29. p. 260) on the other hand reverses this ( the {{brÄhmaá¹‡a|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} having the shortest staff and the {{vaiÅ›ya|vaiÅ›ya}} the longest ). {{Gaut|Gaut}}. I. 26 says that the staff should be one not eaten by worms, should have the bark attached to it, and should have a curved tip, while Manu II. 47 adds that the staff should be straight, pleasing to look at and should not have come into contact with fire. The {{ÅšÄá¹…. gá¹›.|ÅšÄá¹…. gá¹›.}} (II.ayana}} cannot be performed. Jupiter when in the 2nd, 5th, 7th, 9th, 11th zodiacal sign from the sign of birth (calculation to be made inclusive of the sign of birth) is auspicious, when in the 1st, 3rd, 6th, 10th place from the sign of birth it is auspicious after the performance of a propitiatory {{homa|homa}} and when it is in the 4th, 8th, 12th place from birth, it is inauspicious. The moon is supposed to be malefic when she is in the 2nd, 4th, 5th, 8th, 9th or 12th place from the sign of birth. Four hours from sunrise is the best for {{upanayana|upanayana}}, from that time to noon is middling and afternoon is prohibited. Some said that a person’s {{upanayana|upanayana}} should not be performed in the month in which he was born, others restricted the prohibition to the {{paká¹£a|paká¹£a}} (the half) of the month in which he was born 13. 2-3) prescribes that the {{brahmacÄrÄ«|brahmacÄrÄ«}} should not allow any one to pass between himself and his staff and that if the staff, girdle or the {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} break or rend, he has to undergo a penance (same as the one for the breaking of a chariot at a wedding procession ) and that at the end of the period of brahmacarya, he should sacrifice in water the {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}}, staff, the girdle and the skin42 with a mantra to Varuá¹‡a ( Rg. I. 24. 6) or with the sacred syllable ‘om’. Manu II. 64 and Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S. ( 27. 29 ) say the same thing.

For persons who are entitled to perform the {{upanayana|upanayana}} of a boy, vide note 480.

A {{brahmacÄri|brahmacÄrÄ«}} had to wear two garments, one for the lower part of the body ({{vÄsas|vÄsas}}mauÃ±ji|mauÃ±jÄ«}} girdle also to all. Some of the sÅ«tras (e. g. Baud. gá¹›., Ä€p. Manu ( II. 43 ) says that there may be one knot or three or five (according to family usage, says {{KullÅ«ka|KullÅ«ka}}).43

In order to convey an idea of the rites of upanayana in the days of the gá¹›hya sÅ«tras the ceremony as contained in the Ä€Å›v.gá¹›. sÅ«tra (which is among the shortest ) is set out in full.44 ‘Let him initiate the boy who is decked, whose hair (on the head) is shaved (and arranged), who wears a new garment or an antelope skin if a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a|brÄhmaá¹‡a}}, ruru skin if a {{ká¹£atriya|ká¹£atriya}}, goat’s skin if a {{vaiÅ›ya|vaiÅ›ya}}; if they put on garments they should put on dyed ones, reddish-yellow, red and yellow (for a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a|brÄhmaá¹‡a}}, {{ká¹£atriya|ká¹£atriya}}, {{vaiÅ›ya|vaiÅ›ya}} respectively), they should have girdles and staffs ( as described above). While the boy takes hold of ( the hand of) his teacher, the latter offers ( a homa of clarified butter oblations) in the fire (as{{Ävika|Ävika}}’ (made of wool) for {{vaiÅ›ya|vaiÅ›ya}} instead of ‘{{ajina|ajina}}’. {{Vas. Dh. S.|Vas.Dh.S.}} (XI. 64-67) says that a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} ({{brahmacÄri|brahmacÄrÄ«}}) should wear a (lower) garment which is white and unblemished (or new) and for a {{ká¹£atriya|ká¹£atriya}} or {{vaiÅ›ya|vaiÅ›ya}} it should be the same as in {{Ä€p. The teacher then fills the folded hands of both himself and of the boy with water and with the verse ‘we choose that of Savitá¹› ({{á¹šg|á¹šg}}. V. 82. 1) the teacher drops down the water in his own folded hands on to the water in the folded hands of the boy; having thus poured the water, he should seize with his own hand the boy’s hand together with the thumb (of the boy) with the formula ‘by the urge (or order) of the god Savitá¹›, with the arms of the two AÅ›vins, with the hands of PÅ«á¹£an, I seize thy hand, oh! so and so ‘;45 with the words ‘Savitá¹› has seized thy hand, oh so and so’ a second time ( the teacher seizes the boy’s hand ); with the words ’ mountainous goat ({{kutapa|kutapa}}); other teachers prescribe coloured garments, one coloured with the juice of trees for {{brÄhmaá¹‡as|brÄhmaá¹‡as}} and madder-red and yellow for {{ká¹£atriya|ká¹£atriya}} and {{vaiÅ›ya|vaiÅ›ya}}. {{Ä€Å›v. gá¹›.|Ä€Å›v.gá¹›.}} (I. 19. 8), {{PÄr. gá¹›.|PÄr.gá¹›.}} (II. 5), {{Vas. Who does initiate thee and whom ( does he initiate )? I give9 thee to Ka ( to PrajÄpati)’. With the half verse ({{á¹šg|á¹šg}}. Having wiped the ground round the fire the {{brahmacÄrÄ«|brahmacÄrÄ«}} should put ( on the fire ) a fuel stick silently, since it is known (from Å›ruti) ‘what belongs to PrajÄpati is silently ( done)’, and the {{brahmacÄrÄ«|brahmacÄrÄ«}} belongs to PrajÄpati. Some do this (offering of a fuel stick) with a mantra ’to Agni I have brought a fuel stick, to the great {{JÄtavedasÅ›v.gá¹›.}} (I. 19. 8-9) appears to suggest that the lower and upper garments may be of the same skin or that the lower garment may be white or coloured (as stated above by {{Ä€p. The {{KÄá¹­haka gá¹›.|KÄá¹­haka-gá¹›.}}39 (41. 13) says that the upper garment for the three {{vará¹‡as|vará¹‡as}} should be respectively of the skins of black deer, tiger and ruru deer. A vestige of these rules survives in the modern practice of tying a small piece of deer skin to the {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} of the boy when his {{upanayana|upanayana}} is performed.

That the rules about the lower and upper garments go back to great antiquity is shown by a reference to a {{BrÄhmaá¹‡a|BrÄhmaá¹‡a}} passage in the {{Ä€p. Dh. S.|Ä€p.Dh.S.}}40 I. 1. 3.9 ‘one should wear only deerskin (as lower and upper garments) if one desires the increase of {{Vedlo|Vedic}} lore, only (cotton) garments if one desires the increase of martial valour and both if one desiresÄrÄ«|brahmacÄrÄ«}}50 art thou, sip water, do service, do not sleep by day, depending (completely ) on the teacher learn the Veda’. He (the student) should beg (food) in the evening and the morning ; he should put a fuel stick ( on fire ) in the evening and the morning. That ( which he has received by begging) he should announce to the teacher; he should not sit down (but should be standing) the rest of the day.51

(41. 10) prescribe aftertras}} the ceremony as contained in the {{Ä€Å›v.gá¹›.|Ä€Å›v.gá¹›.}} sÅ«tra (which is among the shortest) is set out in full.44 ‘Let him initiate the boy who is decked, whose hair (on the head) is shaved (and arranged), who wears a new garment or an antelope skin if a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a|brÄhmaá¹‡a}}, ruru skin if a {{ká¹£atriya|ká¹£atriya}}, goat’s skin if a {{vaiÅ›ya|vaiÅ›ya}}; if they put on garments they should put on dyed ones, reddish-yellow, red and yellow (for a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a|brÄhmaá¹‡a}}, {{ká¹£atriya|ká¹£atriya}}, {{vaiÅ›ya|vaiÅ›ya}} respectively), they should have girdles and staffs (as described above). While the boy takes hold of (the hand of homa the tasting of curds thrice after repeating the verse ‘{{DadhikrÄvá¹‡o akÄriá¹£am|DadhikrÄvá¹‡o akÄriá¹£am}}’({{á¹šg|á¹šg}}. IV. 39. 6={{Tai. S.|Tai. S.}} I. 5. 4. 11).

(c) {{PÄr. gá¹›.|PÄr. gá¹›.}} (II.2), {{BhÄr. gá¹›.|BhÄr. gá¹›.}} (I.7), Ä€p. gá¹›. (11.1-4), Ä€p. {{MantrapÄá¹­ha|MantrapÄá¹­ha}} (II. 3. 27-30), Baud. gá¹›. II. 5. 25 (quoting {{ÅšÄá¹­yÄyanaka|ÅšÄá¹­yÄyanaka}}), {{MÄnava|MÄnava}} gá¹›.) his teacher, the latter offers (a {{homa|homa}} of clarified butter oblations) in the fire (as described above) and seats himself to the north of the fire with his face turned to the east, while the other one (the boy) stations himself in front (of the teacher) with his face turned to the west. The teacher then fills the folded hands of both himself and of the boy with water and with the verse ‘we choose that of {{Savitá¹›|Savitá¹›}} ({{á¹šg.|á¹šg.}} V. 82. 1) the teacher drops down the water in his own folded hands on to the water in the folded hands of the boy; having thus poured the water, he should seize with his own hand the boy’s hand together with the thumb (of the boy) with the formula ‘by the urge (or order) of the god {{Savitá¹›|Savitá¹›}}, with the arms of the two {{ I. 22. 4-5, and {{KhÄdira|KhÄdira}} gá¹›. II. 4. 12 refer to the fact that the teacher asks the boy his name and the latter pronounces his name. The teacher also asks whose {{brahmacÄrÄ«|brahmacÄrÄ«}} the boy is.

A person was given (as shown above at pp. 246-247) an {{abhivÄAÅ›vins|AÅ›vins}}, with the hands of {{PÅ«á¹£an|PÅ«á¹£an}}, I seize thy hand, oh! so and so’;45 with the words ‘{{Savitá¹›|Savitá¹›}} has seized thy hand, oh so and so’ a second time (the teacher seizes the boy’s hand); with the words ‘{{Agni|Agni}} is thy teacher, {{ob|oh}} so and so’ a third time. The teacher should cause (the boy) to look at the sun, while the teacher repeats ‘God {{Savitá¹›|Savitá¹›}}! this is thy {{brahmacÄri|brahmacÄrÄ«}}, protect him, may he not die46 and (the teacher should further) say ‘Whose {{brahmacÄrÄ«|brahmacÄrÄ«}} art thou? thou art the {{brahmacÄrÄ«|brahmacÄrÄ«}} of {{PrÄá¹‡a|PrÄá¹‡a}}. Who does initiate thee and whom (does hedanÄ«ya|abhivÄdanÄ«ya}} name either derived from the {{naká¹£atra|naká¹£atra}} of birth or from a deity name or the {{gotra|gotra}} name. This was necessary for several purposes. The teacher had to know that the boy came from a good family, he had also to address him by name (as there might be several pupils). It is wrong to suppose56 from the story of {{SatyakÄma JÄbÄla|SatyakÄma JÄbÄla}} (where the teacher asked him his {{gotra|gotra}}) that only {{brÄhmaá¹‡as|brÄhmaá¹‡as}} were admitted to brahmacarya. All {{smá¹›tis|smá¹›tis}}, even the latest, contemplated that the three castes had the right to learn the Veda. It is one thing to have a right and another to exercise it. Probably very few {{ká¹£atriyas|ká¹£atriyas}} cared to submit their sons to the rigorous discipline of brahmacarya as laid down in the {{smá¹›tis|smá¹›tis}} and cared much less for Veda studies. In the MahÄbhÄrata and in the {{KÄdambarÄ«|KÄdambarÄ«}} it is said that the princes were taught in a special house constructed for the purpose and teachers were paid handsomely and brought there to teach instead of the princes going to the teachers.

It is not possible for want of space to show how the greatest possible confusion prevails as to the order of the various components of the ceremony of upanayana, But a few striking examples may be given. Ä€Å›valÄyana gá¹›. treats of the tying of the girdle and the giving of the staff almost at the end of the ceremony, while Ä€p. gá¹›. puts this after homa and immediately before {{aÃ±jalipÅ«raá¹‡a|aÃ±jalipÅ«raá¹‡a}} (filling the folded hands of both with water); Ä€Å›valÄyana puts {{ÄdityadarÅ›ana|ÄdityadarÅ›ana}} after the boy Oh {{Agni|Agni}} and may we (increase) through brahman (prayer or spiritual lore), {{svÄhÄ|svÄhÄ}}’.47 Having put the fuel stick (on the fire) and having touched the fire, he (the student) thrice wipes off his face with the words ‘I anoint myself with lustre’; it is known (from {{Å›ruti|Å›ruti}}) ‘for he does anoint himself with lustre’. ‘May {{Agni|Agni}} bestow on me, insight, offspring and lustre; on me may {{Indra|Indra}} bestow insight, offspring and vigour ({{indriya|indriya}}); on me may the sun bestow insight, offspring and radiance; what thy lustre is, Oh {{Agni|Agni}}, may I thereby become lustrous; what thy strength is, {{Agni|Agni}}, may I thereby become strong; what thy consuming power is {{Agni|Agni}}, may I thereby acquire consuming power’.48 Having waited upon (worshipped)’s {{band|hand}} is held by the teacher and before {{paridÄna|paridÄna}} (handing the boy {{cover|over}} to the deities), but {{BhÄr|BhÄr}}. (I. 9) puts it after the instruction in the observances of brahmacarya. The {{BhÄr. gá¹›.|BhÄr. gá¹›.}} (I.9) treats of {{ÄdityadarÅ›ana|ÄdityadarÅ›ana}} almost at the end of the rites, while Ä€Å›v. places it among the earlier ones after homa. Besides the same mantras are employed by different sÅ«tras for different purposes; for example, the mantra ‘{{suÅ›rayaá¸¥|suÅ›ravas}} &c.’ is employed by Ä€p. at the time of taking the staff by the boy (XI. 14 and {{MantrapÄá¹­ha|MantrapÄá¹­ha}} II. 5. 1), while Ä€Å›v. gá¹›. (I. 22. 19), {{BhÄr. gá¹›.|BhÄr. gá¹›.}} (I. 10), {{MÄnava|MÄnava}} gá¹›. (I. 23, 17) employ it in ‘{{medhÄjanana|medhÄjanana}}’ (on the 4th day after upanayana) and {{PÄr. gá¹›.|PÄr. gá¹›.}} (II. 4) employs it at the time of putting a fuel stick on fire. Ä€Å›v. employs the verse ‘{{yuvÄ suvÄsÄá¸¥|yuvÄ suvÄsÄá¸¥}}’ ({{á¹šg {{Agni|Agni}} with these formulas, (the student) should bend his knees, embrace (the teacher’s feet) and say to him ‘recite, Sir, recite, Sir, the {{SÄvitrÄ«|SÄvitrÄ«}}’. Seizing the student’s hands with the upper garment (of the student) and his own hands the teacher recites the {{SÄvitrÄ«|SÄvitrÄ«}}, first {{pÄda|pÄda}} by {{pÄda|pÄda}}, then hemistich by hemistich (and lastly) the whole verse. He (the teacher) should make him (the student) recite (the {{SÄvitrÄ«|SÄvitrÄ«}}) as much as he is able. On the place of the student’s heart the teacher lays his hand with the fingers upturned with the formula ‘I place thy heart49 unto duty to me; may thy mind follow my mind; may you attend on my words single-minded; may {{Bá¹›haspati|Bá¹›haspati}} appoint thee unto me. Having tied the girdle round him (the boy) and having given him the staff, the teacher should instruct him in the observances of a {{brahmacÄrÄ«|brahmacÄrÄ«}} with the words ‘a {{brahmacÄrÄ«|bra|á¹šg}}. III. 8. 4) for making the boy turn round (first half) and for touching the region of the boy’s heart (latter half), while the same verse is employed at the time of tying the girdle round the boy’s waist by the {{MÄnava|MÄnava}} gá¹›. (I. 22.8 ) and {{PÄr. gá¹›.|PÄr. gá¹›.}}(II. 2).

Later writers went on adding mantras and details. On the day prior to {{upapayana|upanayana}} the NÄndÄ«Å›rÄddha was performed as said by Haradatta on Ä€p. gá¹›. 10. 5. {{Grahamakha|Grahamakha}} (a sacrifice to the planets) also may be performed the previous day or on any day within 7 or 10 days of the upanayana. Then before the actual upanayana, there is the worship of Gaá¹‡apati and of KuladavatÄs, {{puá¹‡yÄhavÄcana|puá¹‡yÄhavÄcana}}, the worship of MÄtá¹›s and the consecration of the {{maá¹‡á¸apa-devatÄs|maá¹‡á¸apa-devatÄs}}. For the detailed modern procedure (prayoga) of upanayana,hmacÄrÄ«}}50 art thou, sip water, do service, do not sleep by day, depending (completely) on the teacher learn the Veda’. He (the student) should beg (food) in the evening and the morning; he should put a fuel stick (on fire) in the evening and the morning. That (which he has received by begging) he should announce to the teacher; he should not sit down (but should be standing) the rest of the day.51

[^6ta}} will be dealt with separately later on, {{Ä€p. gá¹›.|Ä€p.gá¹›.}} (X.5), {{Baud gá¹›.|Baud.gá¹›.}} (II. 5. 7) and {{PÄr. gá¹›.|PÄr.gá¹›.}} (II. 2) prescribe a dinner to {{brÄhmaá¹‡as|brÄhmaá¹‡as}} before the ceremonies begin and receiving their benedictions. {{Ä€p. gá¹›.|Ä€p.gá¹›.}} (X.5), {{BhÄr. gá¹›.|BhÄr.gá¹›.}} (I. 1), {{Baud gá¹›.|Baud.gá¹›.}}53 (II. 5. 7) say that the boy also is made to take food; according to later works (e. g. {{Saá¹skÄraratnamÄlÄ|Saá¹skÄraratnamÄlÄ}}) the boy takes his meal in the same dish with his mother (for the last time) and other {{brahmacÄrins|brahm75]: >- à¤¤à¤¤à¥‹ à¤¦à¥‡à¤µà¤¾ à¤¯à¤œà¥à¤à¥‹à¤ªà¤µà¥€à¤¤à¤¿à¤¨à¥‹ à¤­à¥‚à¤¤à¥à¤µà¤¾ à¤¦à¤•à¥à¤·à¤¿à¤£à¤‚ à¤œà¤¾à¤¨à¥à¤µà¤¾à¤šà¥à¤¯à¥‹à¤ªà¤¾à¤¸à¥€à¤¦à¤‚à¤¸à¥à¤¤à¤¾à¤¨à¥ à¤…à¤¬à¥à¤°à¤µà¥€à¤¦à¥ à¤¯à¤œà¥à¤à¥‹ à¤µà¥‹à¤½à¤¨à¥à¤¨à¤®à¥ à¤…à¤®à¥ƒà¤¤à¤¤à¥à¤µà¤‚ à¤µ à¤Šà¤°à¥à¤—à¥à¤µà¤ƒ à¤¸à¥‚à¤°à¥à¤¯à¥‹ à¤µà¥‹ à¤œà¥à¤¯à¥‹à¤¤à¤¿à¤°à¥ à¤‡à¤¤à¤¿ à¥¤ à¤…à¤¥à¥ˆà¤¨à¤‚ à¤ªà¤¿à¤¤à¤°à¤ƒ à¤ªà¥à¤°à¤¾à¤šà¥€à¤¨à¤¾à¤µà¥€à¤¤à¤¿à¤¨à¤ƒ à¤¸à¤µà¥à¤¯à¤‚ à¤œà¤¾à¤¨à¥à¤µà¤¾à¤šà¥à¤¯ … … à¤…à¤¥à¥ˆà¤¨à¤‚ à¤®à¤¨à¥à¤·à¥à¤¯à¤¾à¤ƒ à¤ªà¥à¤°à¤¾à¤µà¥ƒà¤¤à¤¾ à¤‰à¤ªà¤¸à¥à¤¥à¤‚ à¤•à¥ƒà¤¤à¥à¤µà¥‹à¤ªà¤¾à¤¸à¥€à¤¦à¤¨à¥â€™ à¤¶à¤¤à¤ªà¤¥ II. 4. 2. 1.

It seems to follow from a passage in the {{Tai. Ä€.|Tai. Ä€.}} (II.1) that a strip of black antelope skin or of clothacÄrins}} (eight in number) are also invited at the same time for meals in the company of the boy. This practice is observed even in modern times. Almost all prescribe that the boy is shaved on this day (as in {{caula|caula}}); this also is done in modern times. But in ancient54 times the shaving was done by the {{ÄcÄrya|ÄcÄrya}} himself as stated by {{SudarÅ›ana|SudarÅ›ana}} on {{Ä€p. gá¹›.|Ä€p.gá¹›.}} 10. 6-8. There are several other matters detailed in {{Ä€p.|Ä€p.}} and others, on which {{Ä€Å›v.|Ä€Å›v.}} and some others are silent. The important ones are mentioned below.

Therefore one should study, sacrifice or officiate at a sacrifice with the {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} on for securing the spreading of sacrifice; having worn an antelope skin or a garment on the right side, he raises the right hand and keeps the left down; this is {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}}; when this position is reversed it is {{prÄcÄ«nÄvÄ«ta|prÄcÄ«nÄvÄ«ta}}; the position called {{saá¹vita|saá¹vÄ«ta}} is for men.’ It is remarkable that here at any rate no cord of threads is meant by {{upavÄ«ta|upavÄ«ta}}, but only a piece of skin or cloth. The first part of the same passage is discussed by Jaimini (III. 4. 1-9) and it is established that the passage enjoins the wearing of {{upavÄ«ta|upavÄ«ta}} in {{DarÅ›a-PÅ«rá¹‡amÄsa|DarÅ›a-PÅ«rá¹‡amÄsa}} which is {{devakarma|devakarma}} and that the reference to {{pracinÄvÄ«ta|prÄcÄ«nÄvÄ«ta}} and {{nivÄ«ta|nivÄ«ta}} is only an {{anuvÄda|anuvÄda}} intended to emphasize the desirability of the wearing of {{upavÄ«ta|upavÄ«ta}} in sacrifices to gods. The TantravÄrtika65 explains that {{nivÄ«ta|nivÄ«ta}} is tying the {{upavÄ«ta|upavÄ«ta}} round the throat like a braid of hair (according to some), while according to others it is tying it round the waist as if for girding up the loins and that the first is not done except in battle, while the latter has to be resorted to in all acts in order to devote sole attention to them. So according to the TantravÄrtika, the {{Tai. S.|Tai. S.}} is not referring (in II. 5. 11. 1) to a cord of threads but to a pieceà¤¯à¤¤à¤¿ à¤‡à¤¤à¥à¤¯à¥-à¤…à¤¤à¥à¤° à¤µà¤šà¤¨-à¤…à¤­à¤¾à¤µà¤¾à¤¤à¥, à¤¤à¤¤à¥-à¤•à¤²à¥à¤ªà¤¨à¤¾à¤¯à¤¾à¤‚ à¤š à¤…à¤¨à¥à¤ªà¤ªà¤¤à¥à¤¤à¥à¤¯à¥-à¤…à¤­à¤¾à¤µà¤¾à¤¤à¥ à¥¤’ à¤¹à¤°à¤¦à¤¤à¥à¤¤ on {{à¤†à¤ª. à¤—à¥ƒ.|Ä€p.gá¹›.}} X. 6 says ‘à¤ªà¥‚à¤°à¥à¤µà¤‚ à¤®à¤¨à¥à¤¤à¥à¤°à¤µà¤¦à¥-à¤µà¤ªà¤¨à¤‚ à¤•à¤°à¥‹à¤¤à¥à¤¯à¥-à¤†à¤šà¤¾à¤°à¥à¤¯à¤ƒ, à¤ªà¤¶à¥à¤šà¤¾à¤¨à¥ à¤¨à¤¾à¤ªà¤¿à¤¤ à¤‡à¤¤à¥à¤¯à¥-à¤‰à¤•à¥à¤¤à¤‚ à¤­à¤µà¤¤à¤¿,’ which is combated by {{à¤¸à¥à¤¦à¤°à¥à¤¶à¤¨|SudarÅ›ana}}.

(a) {{Ä€p. gá¹›.|Ä€p.gá¹›.}} (10.9), {{MÄnava gá¹›.|MÄnava-gá¹›.}} (I. 23. 12), {{Baud. gá¹›.|Baud.gá¹›.}} (II. 5, 10), {{KhÄdira gá¹›.|KhÄdira-gá¹›.}} (II. 4) and {{BhÄr. gá¹›.|BhÄr.gá¹›.}} (I.8) make the boy tread on a stone to the north of the fire with his right foot after {{home|homa}}. (b) {{MÄnava gá¹›.|MÄnava-gá¹›.}} (I.22.3) and {{KÄá¹­haka gá¹›.|KÄá¹­haka-gá¹›.}} (41. of cloth. Some of the {{sÅ«trakÄras|sÅ«trakÄras}} and commentators drop hints that garments were used or could be used as {{upavÄ«ta|upavÄ«ta}}. Ä€p. Dh. S. (II. 2. 4. 22-23 ) says that a householder should always wear an upper garment and then adds’or the sacred thread may serve the purpose of an upper garment.’ This shows that originally {{upavÄ«ta|upavÄ«ta}} meant an upper garment and not merely a cord of threads. In another place the same sÅ«tra says (II. 8. 19.12) ‘one (who partakes of {{Å›rÄddha|Å›rÄddha}} dinner) should eat covered with an upper garment slung over {{tha|the}} left shoulder and passing under the right arm’. Haradatta gives two explanations of this, viz. that one should wear an upper garment (while dining at a {{Å›rÄddha|Å›rÄddha}}) like a {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} i. e. under the right arm and over the left shoulder, that is, a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} cannot rely on Ä€p. Dh. S. II. 2. 4. 23 and wear at {{Å›rÄddha|Å›rÄddha}} repast only the sacred thread (but he must wear the garment in that fashion) and give up the sacred thread for the time.66 While another view is that he must wear the sacred thread and the upper garment both in the fashion of {{upavÄ«ta|upavÄ«ta}}. Ä€p. Dh. S. (I. 2. 6. 18-19) prescribes that when a student wears two garments he should wear one of them (i. e. the upper one) in the {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} mode, but when he wears only one garment then he should wear it round the lower part of the body (and should not cover the upper body with a portion of the garment though it may be long enough for that). Ä€p. Dh. S. I. 5. 15. 1 prescribes that a man must be {{yajÃ±opavÄ«tin|yajÃ±opavÄ«tÄ«}} at the time of waiting upon teachers, elders, guests, at the time of homa, in _{ 10) prescribe after {{homa|homa}} the tasting of curds thrice after repeating the verse ‘{{DadhikrÄvá¹‡o akÄriá¹£am|DadhikrÄvá¹‡o akÄriá¹£am}}’ ({{á¹šg.|á¹šg.}} IV. 39. 6={{Tai. S.|Tai.S.}} I. 5. 4. 11).

(c) {{PÄr. gá¹›.|PÄr.gá¹›.}} (II.2), {{BhÄr. gá¹›.|BhÄr.gá¹›.}} (I.7), {{Ä€p. gá¹›.|Ä€p.gá¹›.}} (11.1-4), {{Ä€p. MantrapÄá¹­ha|Ä€p.MantrapÄá¹­ha}} (II. 3. 27-30), {{Baud. gá¹›.|Baud.gá¹›.}} II. 5. 25 (quoting {{ÅšÄá¹­yÄyanaka|ÅšÄá¹­yÄyanaka}}), {{MÄnava gá¹›.|MÄnava-gá¹›.}} I. 22. 4-5, and {{KhÄdira gá¹›.|KhÄdira-gá¹›.}} II. 4. 12 refer to the fact that the teacher asks the boy his name and the latter pronounces his name. The teacher also asks whose {{brahmacÄrÄ«|brahmacÄrÄ«}} the boy is.

A person was given (as shown above at pp. 246-247) an {{abhivÄdanÄ«ya|abhivÄdanÄ«ya}} name either derived from the {{naká¹£atra|naká¹£atra}} of birth or from a deity name or the {{gotra|gotra}} name. This was necessary for several purposes. The teacher had to know that the boy came from a good family, he had also to address him by name (as there might be several pupils). It is wrong to suppose56 from the story of {{SatyakÄma JÄbÄla|SatyakÄma JÄbÄla}} (where the teacher asked him his {{gotra|gotra}}) that only {{brÄhmaá¹‡as|brÄhmaá¹‡as}} were admitted to {{brahmacarya|brahmacarya}}. All {{smá¹›tis|smá¹›tis}}, even the latest, contemplated that the three castes had the right to learn the Veda. It is one thing to have a right and another to exercise it. Probably very few {{ká¹£atriyas|ká¹£atriyas}} cared to submit their sons to the rigorous discipline of {{brahmacarya|brahmacarya}} as laid down in the {{smá¹›tis|smá¹›tis}} and cared much less for Veda studies. In the {{MahÄbhÄrata|MahÄbhÄrata}} and in{japa|japa}}_ (murmuring prayer), at meals and in taking Äcamana and at the time of daily vedic study. On this Haradatta says’{{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} means a particular mode of wearing the (upper) garment; if one has no upper garment, then there is another (but inferior) mode stated in Ä€p. Dh. S. II. 2. 4. 23 and that at other times it is not necessary to have the {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}}’.

The Gobhila gá¹›. (I. 2. 1) in treating of upanayana[^6 the {{KÄdambarÄ«|KÄdambarÄ«}} it is said that the princes were taught in a special house constructed for the purpose and teachers were paid handsomely and brought there to teach instead of the princes going to the teachers.

  • Vide Keay in ‘Ancient Indian Education’ p. 23 where he says ‘it was still the rule for {{brÄhmaá¹‡as|brÄhmaá¹‡as}} to be received as students.’ Dr. Ghurye in ‘Caste and race in India’ p. 43 merely repeats this {{dictom|dictum}}.

It is not possible for want of space to show how the greatest possible confusion prevails as to the order of the various components of the ceremony of {{upanayana|upanayana}}, But a few striking examples may be given. {{Ä€Å›valÄyana gá¹›.|Ä€Å›valÄyana-gá¹›.}} treats of the tying of the girdle and the giving of the staff almost at the end of the ceremony, while {{Ä€p. gá¹›.|Ä€p.gá¹›.}} puts this after {{homa|homa}} and immediately before {{aÃ±jalipÅ«raá¹‡a|aÃ±jalipÅ«raá¹‡a}} (filling the folded hands of both with water); {{Ä€Å›valÄyana|Ä€Å›valÄyana}} puts {{ÄdityadarÅ›ana|ÄdityadarÅ›ana}} after the boy’s {{band|hand}} is held by the teacher and before {{paridÄna|paridÄna}} (handing the boy {{cover|over}} to the deities), but {{BhÄr.|BhÄr.}} (I. 9) puts it after the instruction in the observances of {{brahmacarya|brahmacarya}}. The {{BhÄr. gá¹›.|BhÄr.gá¹›.}} (I.9) treats of {{ÄdityadarÅ›ana|ÄdityadarÅ›ana}} almost at the end of the rites, while {{Ä€Å›v.|Ä€Å›v.}} places it among the earlier ones after {{homa|homa}}. Besides the same mantras are employed by different {{sÅ«tras|sÅ«tras}} for different purposes; for example, the mantra ‘{{suÅ›rayaá¸¥|suÅ›ravas}} &c.’ is employed by {{Ä€p.|Ä€p.}} at the time of taking the staff by the boy (XI. 14 and {{MantrapÄá¹­ha|MantrapÄá¹­ha}} II. 5. 1), while {{Ä€Å›v. gá¹›.|Ä€Å›v.gá¹›.}} (I. 22. 19), {{BhÄr. gá¹›.|BhÄr.gá¹›.}} (I. 10), {{MÄnava gá¹›.|MÄnava-gá¹›.}} (I. 23, 17) employ it in ‘{{medhÄjanana|medhÄjanana}}’ (on the 4th day after {{upanayana|upanayana}}) and {{PÄr. gá¹›.|PÄr.gá¹›.}} (II. 4) employs it at the time of putting a fuel stick on fire. {{Ä€Å›v.|Ä€Å›v.}} employs the verse ‘{{yuvÄ suvÄsÄá¸¥|yuvÄ suvÄsÄá¸¥}}’ ({{á¹šg.|á¹šg.}} III. 8. 4) for making the boy turn round (first half) and for touching the region of the boy’s heart (latter half), while the same verse is employed at the time of tying the girdle round the boy’s waist by the {{MÄnava gá¹›.|MÄnava-gá¹›.}} (I. 22.8) and {{PÄr. gá¹›.|PÄr.gá¹›.}} (II. 2).

Later writers went on adding mantras and details. On the day prior to {{upapayana|upanayana}} the {{NÄndÄ«Å›rÄddha|NÄndÄ«Å›rÄddha}} was performed as said by {{Haradatta|Haradatta}} on {{Ä€p. gá¹›.|Ä€p.gá¹›.}} 10. 5. {{Grahamakha|Grahamakha}} (a sacrifice to the planets) also may be performed the previous day or on any day within 7 or 10 days of the {{upanayana|81] says ’the student takes as {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} a cord of threads, a garment or a rope of {{kuÅ›a|kuÅ›a}} grass’. This indicates that though a cord of threads was considered in Gobhila’s days as the appropriate {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}}, that was not an invariable rule in his day and that a garment could be employed instead. The commentator being brought up in the latter day tradition explains by saying that if the sÅ«tra was lost in a forest then a garment may be worn like the sacred thread and if even that was lost a rope of {{kuÅ›a|kuÅ›a}}. But this appears to be rather far-fetched as an explanation of Gobhila’s unqualified words. Manu (II. 44) says that the {{upavÄ«ta|upavÄ«ta}} of a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} should be made of cotton, its strands should be twined with the right hand moved over them (or the twist of the strands must be upwards) and it should have three threads’. MedhÄtithi comments on this that ‘{{upavÄ«ta|upavÄ«ta}}’ means a particular mode of wearing a garment or a particular position of it and therefore here by {{upavÄ«ta|upavÄ«ta}} is meant that which can be worn in that mode. The Sm. C. quotes a prose passage from {{á¹šá¹£yaÅ›á¹›á¹…ga|á¹šá¹£yaÅ›á¹›á¹…ga}} ‘or one may carry out all the purposes for which {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} is required by means of a garment and in its absence by a string of three threads’. From the above passages, from the fact that many of the gá¹›hyasÅ«tras are entirely silent about the giving or wearing of the sacred thread in upanayana and from the fact that no mantra67 is cited from the Vedic Literature for the act of giving the {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} (which is now the centre of the upanayana rites), while scores of vedic mantras are cited for the several component parts of the ceremony of upanayana, it is most probable, if not certain, that the sacred thread was not invariably used in the older times as in the times of the later Smá¹›tis and in modern times, that originally the upper garment was {{need|used}} in various positions for certain acts, that it could be laid aside altogether in the most ancient times and that the cord of threads came to be used first as an option and later on exclusively for the upper {{garnent|garment}}.

A few rules about {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} may now be stated here.68 The {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} is to have three threads of nine strands well twisted (for each thread). Vide Baud. Dh.S.I. 5.5., Devala quoted in the Sm. C.69. The nine devatÄs of the nine {{tantus|tantus}} (strands) are given by Devala viz. Oá¹kÄra, Agni, NÄga, Soma, {{Pitá¹›s|Pitá¹›s}}, PrajÄpati, VÄyu, SÅ«rya,70 all gods. MedhÄtithi on Manu II.44 says that in iá¹£á¹­is, animal sacrifices and soma sacrifices, the {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} was to have only one thread of three {{tantus|tantus}}, but it was three-fold in three classes of _{{ahÄ«na, ekÄha,|ahÄ«na, ekÄha,}} and {{sattra|sattra}} sacrifices as they required three fires and in the seven _{{somasaupanayana}}. Then before the actual {{upanayana|upanayana}}, there is the worship of {{Gaá¹‡apati|Gaá¹‡apati}} and of {{KuladavatÄs|KuladevatÄs}}, {{puá¹‡yÄhavÄcana|puá¹‡yÄhavÄcana}}, the worship of {{MÄtá¹›s|MÄtá¹›s}} and the consecration of the {{maá¹‡á¸apa-devatÄs|maá¹‡á¸apa-devatÄs}}. For the detailed modern procedure ({{prayoga|prayoga}}) of {{upanayana|upanayana}}, the S. R. M. (pp. 197-210), which in the main follows the briefer procedure in the {{gá¹›hya sÅ«tras|gá¹›hyasÅ«tras}}, may be consulted. Up to the act of making the boy dine with his mother it is the father who does everything; thereafter it is the {{ÄcÄrya|ÄcÄrya}} who does the rest. The {{saá¹kalpa|saá¹kalpa}} of {{upanayana|upanayana}} is given below57

YajÃ±opavÄ«ta58:â€” A few words must be said on the history of {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} from ancient times. Among the earliest references is one from the {{Tai. S.|Tai.S.}} II. 5. 11.1 ’the {{nivÄ«ta|nivÄ«ta}}59 is (used in actions) for men, the {{prÄcÄ«nÄvÄ«ta|prÄcÄ«nÄvÄ«ta}} (is used in rites) for {{pitá¹›s|pitá¹›s}}, the {{upavÄ«ta|upavÄ«ta}} (in rites) for gods; he wears it in the {{upavÄ«ta|upavÄ«ta}} mode (i.e. slung from the left shoulder), thereby he makes a distinguishing sign of the gods’. In the {{Tai. Br.|Tai.Br.}} (I. 6. 8.) we read ‘wearing in the {{prÄcÄ«nÄvÄ«ta|prÄcÄ«nÄvÄ«ta}} form he offers towards the south; for in the case of {{pitá¹›s|pitá¹›s}}, acts are performed towards the south. Disregarding that one should wear in the {{upavÄ«ta|upavÄ«ta}} form and offer in the north only, since both gods and {{pitá¹›s|pitá¹›s}} are worshipped (in this rite)’.60 The three words {{nivÄ«ta|nivÄ«ta}}, {{pracinÄvÄ«ta|prÄcÄ«nÄvÄ«ta}} and {{upavÄ«ta|upavÄ«ta}} are explained in the {{Gobhila gá¹›.|Gobhila-gá¹›.}} (I. 2. 2-4) which says ‘raising his right arm, putting the head into (the {{upavÄ«ta|upavÄ«ta}}) he suspends (the cord) over his left shoulder in such a way that it hangs down on his right side; thus he becomes {{yajÃ±opavÄ«tin|yajÃ±opavÄ«tÄ«}}. Passing his left arm, putting the head (into the {{upavÄ«ta|upavÄ«ta}}) he suspends it over his right shoulder, so that it hangs down along his left side; in this way he becomes {{prÄcÄ«nÄvÄ«tin|prÄcÄ«nÄvÄ«tÄ«}}; a person becomes {{pracinÄvÄ«tin|prÄcÄ«nÄvÄ«tÄ«}} only in the sacrifice offered to the Manes’61. To the same effect is {{KhÄdira gá¹›.|KhÄdira-gá¹›.}} I. 1. 8-9, {{Manu|Manu}} II. 63, {{Baud. gá¹›.|Baud.gá¹›.}} {{paribhÄá¹£Ä-sÅ«tra|paribhÄá¹£Ä-sÅ«tra}} II. 2. 7 and 10, {{Vaik.|VaikhÄnasa}} I. 5. The {{Baud.|BaudhÄyana}}62 in {{gá¹›. paribhÄá¹£Ä-sÅ«tra|gá¹›hya-paribhÄá¹£Ä-sÅ«tra}} II.2.3 says ‘when it is carried over the neck, both shoulders and the chestá¹sthÄs|somasaá¹sthÄs}}_ seven-fold and five-fold when viewed with reference to the three savanas and two {{saá¹dhyÄs|saá¹dhyÄs}}. The {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} should reach as far as the navel, should not reach beyond the navel, nor should it be above the chest.71 Manu II. 44 and Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S. 27. 19 prescribe that the {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} for {{brÄhmaá¹‡a|brÄhmaá¹‡a}}, {{ká¹£atriya|ká¹£atriya}} and {{vaiÅ›ya|vaiÅ›ya}} should respectively be of cotton, hemp and sheep wool. Baud. Dh. S. (I. 5.5), Gobhila gá¹›. (I.2.1) say that it may be of cotton thread or of {{kuÅ›a|kuÅ›a}} grass and Devala as quoted in Sm. C. says that all twice-born persons should make their {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} of cotton, of {{ká¹£umÄ|ká¹£umÄ}}, hair of cow’s tail, hemp, tree bark or {{kuÅ›a|kuÅ›a}} according to the availability of the material.72 The remarks of the Saá¹skÄramayÅ«kha after quoting Manu II. 44 are interesting ‘we do not know the origin of (or authority for) the practice of present-day {{ká¹£atriyas|ká¹£atriyas}} and {{vaiÅ›yas|vaiÅ›yas}}, viz. of wearing cotton {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}}’. This shows that in the 17th century A. D. many {{ká¹£atriyas|ká¹£atriyas}} and {{vaiÅ›yas|vaiÅ›yas}} put on {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}}.73 KumÄrila also says that wearing {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} and studying Vedas is common to all the three {{vará¹‡as|vará¹‡as}}.

The {{Åšat. Br.|Åšat.Br.}} (II. 4, 2. 1, S. B. E. vol. 12, p. 361) says “the gods being {{yajÃ±opavÄ«tins|yajÃ±opavÄ«tins}} approached near, bending their right knee; he ({{PrajÄpati|PrajÄpati}}) said to them ’the sacrifice is your food, immortality is your strength, the sun is your light’; then the {{pitá¹›s|pitá¹›s}} approached him, being {{prÄcÄ«nÄvÄ«tins|prÄà¤ªà¤¾à¤¸-à¤•à¥à¤·à¥Œà¤®-à¤—à¥‹-à¤¬à¤¾à¤²-à¤¶à¤£-à¤µà¤²à¥à¤•à¤¤à¥ƒà¤£à¥‹à¤¦à¥à¤­à¤µà¤®à¥ à¥¤ à¤¸à¤¦à¤¾ à¤¸à¤‚à¤­à¤µà¤¤à¤ƒ à¤•à¤¾à¤°à¥à¤¯à¤®à¥ à¤‰à¤ªà¤µà¥€à¤¤à¤‚ à¤¦à¥à¤µà¤¿à¤œà¤¾à¤¤à¤¿à¤­à¤¿à¤ƒ à¥¥ à¤¸à¥à¤®à¥ƒà¤¤à¤¿-à¤šà¥° I. p. 32; à¤ªà¤°à¤¾. à¤®à¤¾. I. 2. p. 36; à¤µà¥ƒà¤¦à¥à¤§-à¤¹à¤¾à¤°à¥€à¤¤ (VIII.47-48) has a similar verse.

The number of {{yajÃ±opavÄ«tas|yajÃ±opavÄ«tas}} to be worn differed according to circumstances. A {{brahmacÄri|brahmacÄrÄ«}} was to wear only one {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} and {{saá¹nyÄsins|saá¹nyÄsins}}, when they kept {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} at all, also wore only one. A {{snÄtaka|snÄtaka}}74 (i. e. one who has returned from the teacher’s house after brahmacarya) and a house-{{bolder|holder}} were to wear two while one who desired long life may wear more than two. {{Vas. Dh. S.|Vas. Dh. S.}} XII. 14 {{days|says}} ‘{{snÄtakas|snÄtakas}} should always wear a lower garment and an upper one, two {{yajÃ±opavÄ«tas|yajÃ±opavÄ«tas}}, should have a stick and a pot filled with water’. KaÅ›yapa allowed a householder to wear any number up to ten. Whether {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} as worn in modern times was worn from the most ancient times or not, it is certain that long before the Christian era it had come to be so worn and it had become an inflexible rule that a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} must always wear a {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} and have his top-knot (of hair) always tied up; if he did any act without observing this rule, it was inefficacious.75 Vas.76 and Baud. Dh. S. (II. 2. 1) both say that a man must always wear {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}}. If a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} took his meals without wearing {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}}, he had to undergo {{prÄyaÅ›citta|prÄyaÅ›citta}} viz. to bathe, to mutter prayers and fast; vide Laghu-HÄrita verse 23 quoted by {{AparÄrks|AparÄrka}} pp. 1171, 1173. The Mit, on YÄj. III. 292 prescribes {{prÄyaÅ›citta|prÄyaÅ›citta}} for answering calls of nature without having the {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} placed on the right ear (as YÄj. I, 16 prescribes). Manu IV. 66 forbids the wearing of another’s {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} along with several other things (such as shoes, ornament, garland and {{kamaná¸alu|kamaá¹‡á¸alu}}). In YÄj. (I. 16 and 133) and other {{smá¹›tis|smá¹›tis}} the {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} is called {{brahmasÅ«tra|brahmasÅ«tra}}.

  • à¤¤à¤¤à¥‹ à¤¦à¥‡à¤µà¤¾ à¤¯à¤œà¥à¤à¥‹à¤ªà¤µà¥€à¤¤à¤¿à¤¨à¥‹ à¤­à¥‚à¤¤à¥à¤µà¤¾ à¤¦à¤•à¥à¤·à¤¿à¤£à¤‚ à¤œà¤¾à¤¨à¥à¤µà¥-à¤†à¤šà¥à¤¯ à¤‰à¤ªà¤¾à¤¸à¥€à¤¦à¤‚à¤¸à¥-à¤¤à¤¾à¤¨à¥-à¤…à¤¬à¥à¤°à¤µà¥€à¤¦à¥ â€” à¤¯à¤œà¥à¤à¥‹ à¤µà¥‹à¤½à¤¨à¥à¤¨à¤®à¥, à¤…à¤®à¥ƒà¤¤à¤¤à¥à¤µà¤‚ à¤µ à¤Šà¤°à¥à¤—à¥à¤µà¤ƒ, à¤¸à¥‚à¤°à¥à¤¯à¥‹ à¤µà¥‹ à¤œà¥à¤¯à¥‹à¤¤à¤¿à¤°à¥-à¤‡à¤¤à¤¿ à¥¤ à¤…à¤¥à¥ˆà¤¨à¤‚ à¤ªà¤¿à¤¤à¤°à¤ƒ à¤ªà¥à¤°à¤¾à¤šà¥€à¤¨à¤¾à¤µà¥€à¤¤à¤¿à¤¨à¤ƒ à¤¸à¤µà¥à¤¯à¤‚ à¤œà¤¾à¤¨à¥à¤µà¥-à¤†à¤šà¥à¤¯… à¤…à¤¥à¥ˆà¤¨à¤‚ à¤®à¤¨à¥à¤·à¥à¤¯à¤¾à¤ƒ à¤ªà¥à¤°à¤¾à¤µà¥ƒà¤¤à¤¾ à¤‰à¤ªà¤¸à¥à¤¥à¤‚ à¤•à¥ƒà¤¤à¥à¤µà¥‹à¤ªà¤¾à¤¸à¥€à¤¦à¤¨à¥ à¥¤ â€” {{à¤¶à¤¤à¤ªà¤¥|Åšatapatha-Br.}} II.4.2.1.
  • à¤à¤¤à¤¾à¤µà¤¤à¤¿ à¤¹ à¤—à¥Œà¤¤à¤®à¤ƒ à¤¯à¤œà¥à¤à¥‹à¤ªà¤µà¥€à¤¤à¤‚ à¤•à¥ƒà¤¤à¥à¤µà¤¾ à¤…à¤§à¥‹ à¤¨à¤¿à¤ªà¤ªà¤¾à¤¤ à¤¨à¤®à¥‹ à¤¨à¤® à¤‡à¤¤à¤¿ à¥¤ â€” {{à¤¤à¥ˆ. à¤¬à¥à¤°à¤¾.|Tai.Br.}} III.10.9. à¤¸à¤¾à¤¯à¤£ remarks ‘à¤¸à¥à¤µà¤•à¥€à¤¯à¥‡à¤¨ à¤µà¤¸à¥à¤¤à¥à¤°à¥‡à¤£ à¤¯à¤œà¥à¤à¥‹à¤ªà¤µà¥€à¤¤à¤‚ à¤•à¥ƒà¤¤à¥à¤µà¤¾ à¥¤’.

It seems to follow from a passage in the {{Tai. Ä€.|Tai.Ä€.}} (II.1) that a strip of black antelope skin or of cloth was used in ancient times as {{upavÄ«ta|upavÄ«ta}}63 ’the sacrifice of him who wears the {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} becomes spread out (prosperous, famous), while the sacrifice of him who does not wear it does not spread; whatever a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} studies, wearing a {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}}, he really (therein) performs a sacrifice. Therefore one should study, sacrifice or officiate at a sacrifice with the {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} on for securing the spreading of sacrifice; having worn an antelope skin or a garment on the right side, he raises the right hand and keeps the left down; this is {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}}; when this position is reversed it is {{prÄcÄ«nÄvÄ«ta|prÄcÄ«nÄvÄ«ta}}; the position called {{saá¹vita|saá¹vÄ«ta}} is for men.’ It is remarkable that here at any rate no cord of threads is meant by {{upavÄ«ta|upavÄ«ta}}, but only a piece of skin or cloth. The {{Par. M.|Par.M.}} (I. part 1 p. 173) quotes64 a portion of the above passage and remarks that the {{Tai. Ä€r.|Tai.Ä€r.}} lays down that a man becomes an {{upavÄ«tin|upavÄ«tÄ«}} by wearing one of the two, viz. antelope skin and (cotton) garment. [^6 à¥¥ à¤µà¤¸à¤¿à¤·à¥à¤  XII, 14; à¤µà¤¿à¤·à¥à¤£à¥-à¤§à¤°à¥à¤®à¤¸à¥‚à¤¤à¥à¤° 71. 13-15 has similar rules, the verse of Vasiá¹£á¹­ha is quoted by the Mit. on YÄj I. 133; compare à¤®à¤¨à¥. IV. 36; à¤à¤•à¥ˆà¤•à¤®à¥ à¤‰à¤ªà¤µà¥€à¤¤à¤‚ à¤¤à¥ à¤¯à¤¤à¥€à¤¨à¤¾à¤‚ à¤¬à¥à¤°à¤¹à¥à¤®à¤šà¤¾à¤°à¤¿à¤£à¤¾à¤®à¥ à¥¤ à¤—à¥ƒà¤¹à¤¿à¤£à¤¾à¤‚ à¤š à¤µà¤¨à¤¸à¥à¤¥à¤¾à¤¨à¤¾à¤®à¥ à¤‰à¤ªà¤µà¥€à¤¤-à¤¦à¥à¤µà¤¯à¤‚ à¤¸à¥à¤®à¥ƒà¤¤à¤®à¥ à¥¤ à¤¸à¥‹à¤¤à¥à¤¤à¤°à¥€à¤¯à¤‚ à¤¤à¥à¤°à¤¯à¤‚ à¤µà¤¾à¤ªà¤¿ à¤¬à¤¿à¤­à¥ƒà¤¯à¤¾à¤šà¥ à¤›à¥à¤­à¥à¤°-à¤¤à¤¨à¥à¤¤à¥ à¤µà¤¾ à¥¤ à¤µà¥ƒà¤¦à¥à¤§-à¤¹à¤¾à¤°à¥€à¤¤ VIII. 44-45. Vide à¤¦à¥‡à¤µà¤² quoted in à¤¸à¥à¤®à¥ƒà¤¤à¤¿-à¤š. I. p. 32 and ‘à¤¤à¥à¤°à¥€à¤£à¤¿ à¤šà¤¤à¥à¤µà¤¾à¤°à¤¿ à¤ªà¤à¥à¤šà¤¾à¤·à¥à¤Ÿ à¤—à¥ƒà¤¹à¤¿à¤£à¤ƒ à¤¸à¥à¤¯à¥à¤°à¥ à¤¦à¤¶à¤¾à¤ªà¤¿ à¤µà¤¾ à¥¤ à¤¸à¤°à¥à¤µà¥ˆà¤°à¥à¤µà¤¾ à¤¶à¥à¤šà¤¿à¤­à¤¿à¤°à¥ à¤§à¤¾à¤°à¥à¤¯à¤®à¥ à¤‰à¤ªà¤µà¥€à¤¤à¤‚ à¤¦à¥à¤µà¤¿à¤œà¤¾à¤¤à¤¿à¤­à¤¿à¤ƒ à¥¥’ à¤•à¤¶à¥à¤¯à¤ª quoted in the à¤¸à¤‚à¤¸à¥à¤•à¤¾à¤°-à¤®à¤¯à¥‚à¤–.

An interesting question is whether women ever had upanayana performed or whether they had to wear the {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}}. Several {{smá¹›tis|smá¹›tis}} contain instructive dicta on this point.77 The HÄrÄ«tadharmasÅ«tra as quoted in the Sm. C. and other digests says ’there are two sorts of women, those that are {{brahmavÄdinÄ«s|brahmavÄdinÄ«s}} (i. e. students of sacred lore) and those that are {{sadyovadhÅ«s|sadyovadhÅ«s}} (i. e, who straightway marry). Out of these {{brahmavÄdinÄ«s|brahmavÄdinÄ«s}} have to go through upanayana, keeping fire, vedic study and begging in one’s house (i. e. under the parental roof); but in the case of {{sadyovadhÅ«s|sadyovadhÅ«s}} when their marriage is drawing near, the mere ceremony of upanayana should somehow be performed and then their marriage should be celebrated.’ In the Gobhila gá¹›.78 II. 1. 19 it is said “leading forward towards the sacred fire (from the house) the bride who is wrapped in a robe and wears the sacred thread (slung from her left shoulder, in the {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} mode) he (the husband) should murmur the verse ‘Soma gave her to Gandharva’ ({{á¹šg|á¹šg}}. X. 85. 41)”. It is clear that the girl, according to Gobhila, wore the {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} as a symbol of the rite of upanayana. The commentator to whom this procedure naturally seemed strange explains ‘{{yajÃ±opavÄ«tinÄ«m|yajÃ±opavÄ«tinÄ«m}}’ as meaning ‘whose upper garment is worn in the fashion of the77]:

This passage is quoted as from the KÄá¹­haka in the {{à¤¤à¤¨à¥à¤¤à¥à¤°à¤µà¤¾à¤°à¥à¤¤à¤¿à¤•|TantravÄrtika}} on à¤œà¥ˆà¤®à¤¿à¤¨à¤¿ I.3.7 (p. 201).
  • à¤¤à¤¤à¥à¤°à¥‡à¤¤à¤¿à¤•à¤°à¥à¤¤à¤µà¥à¤¯à¤¤à¤¾-à¤°à¥‚à¤ªà¥‡à¤£ à¤•à¥ƒà¤·à¥à¤£à¤¾à¤œà¤¿à¤¨-à¤µà¤¾à¤¸à¤¸à¥‹à¤°à¥-à¤…à¤¨à¥à¤¯à¤¤à¤°à¥‡à¤£ à¤‰à¤ªà¤µà¥€à¤¤à¤¿à¤¤à¥à¤µà¤‚ à¤¤à¥ˆà¤¤à¥à¤¤à¤¿à¤°à¥€à¤¯à¤•à¥‡ à¤µà¤¿à¤§à¥€à¤¯à¤¤à¥‡ à¥¤ â€” {{à¤ªà¤°à¤¾. à¤®à¤¾.|ParÄ.MÄ.}} I.1.p.173.

In the {{PÅ«rvamÄ«á¹ÄmsÄsÅ«tra|PÅ«rvamÄ«mÄá¹sÄsÅ«tra}} (III. 1. 21) it is established (with reference to the words) in the {{Tai. S.|Tai.S.}} II. 5. 11. 1 ({{upavyayate|upavyayate}} etc. quoted in f. n. 671) that one has to be an {{upavÄ«tin|upavÄ«tÄ«}} throughout all the actions, prescribed in the sections on the {{DarÅ›a-PÅ«rá¹‡amÄsa|DarÅ›a-PÅ«rá¹‡amÄsa}} sacrifice and not only when the {{SÄmidhenÄ«|SÄmidhenÄ«}} verses are recited. The first part of the same passage is discussed by {{Jaimini|Jaimini}} (III. 4. 1-9) and it is established that the passage enjoins the wearing of {{upavÄ«ta|upavÄ«ta}} in {{DarÅ›a-PÅ«rá¹‡amÄsa|DarÅ›a-PÅ«rá¹‡amÄsa}} which is {{devakarma|devakarma}} and that the reference to {{pracinÄvÄ«ta|prÄcÄ«nÄvÄ«ta}} and {{nivÄ«ta|nivÄ«ta}} is only an anuvÄda intended to emphasize the desirability of the wearing of {{upavÄ«ta|upavÄ«ta}} in sacrifices to gods. The {{TantravÄrtika|TantravÄrtika}}65 explains that nivÄ«ta is tying the {{upavÄ«ta|upavÄ«ta}} round the throat like a braid of hair (according to some), while according to others it is tying it round the waist as if for girding up the loins and that the first is not done except in battle, while the latter has to be resorted to in all acts in order to devote sole attention to them. So according to the {{TantravÄrtika|TantravÄrtika}}, the {{Tai. S.|Tai.S.}} is not referring (in II. 5. 11. 1) to a cord sacred thread’. In the {{Saá¹skÄratattvÄ|Saá¹skÄratattva}} of Raghunandana it is stated that HariÅ›armÄ held that according to Gobhila the bride was to wear a {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}}, though Raghunandana himself does not approve of this explanation. In the ceremony of SamÄvartana, Ä€Å›v. gá¹›. III. 8,79 on the subject of applying ointment says ‘after having smeared the two hands with ointment a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} should salve his face first, a {{ká¹£atriya|ká¹£atriya}} his two arms, a {{vaiÅ›ya|vaiÅ›ya}} his belly, a woman her private parts and persons who gain their livelihood by running, their thighs’. It is improper to say, as some do, that as to women this is a general rule interpolated in the treatment of samÄvartana and has nothing to do with the latter. We should rather hold that Ä€Å›v. knew of women undertaking Vedic study and so prescribed what they should do in their samÄvartana. In the MahÄbhÄrata (Vanaparva 305. 20)80 a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} is said to have taught to the mother of the PÄá¹‡á¸ava heroes a number of mantras from the AtharvaÅ›iras. HÄrÄ«ta81 prescribes that in the case of women samÄvartana took place before the appearance of menses. Therefore {{brahmavÄdinÄ«|brahmavÄdinÄ«}} women had upanayana performed in the 8th year from conception, then they studied Vedic lore and finished student-hood at the age of puberty. Yama82 says ‘in former ages, tying of the girdle of {{muÃ±ja|muÃ±ja}} (i. e. upanayana) was desired in the case of maidens, they were taught the vedes and made to recite the {{SÄvitrÄ«|SÄvitrÄ«}} (the sacred {{GÄyatrÄ«|GÄyatrÄ«}} verse); either their father, uncle or brother taught them and not a stranger and begging was prescribed for a maiden in the house itself and she was not to wear deer-skin or bark garment and was not to have matted hair’. Manu seems to have been aware of this usage as prevalent in ancient times, if not his own. Having spoken of the {{saá¹skÄras|saá¹skÄras}} from {{jÄtakarma|jÄtakarma}} to upanayana, Manu winds up (II. 66) ’these ceremonies were to be performed in their entirety for women also, but without mantras’ and adds (II. 67) ’the ceremony of marriage is the only {{saá¹skÄra|saá¹skÄra}} per formed with Vedic mantras in the case of women; (in their case) attendance on the husband amounts to serving a guru (which a student had to do) and performance of domestic duties to worship of fire’ (which the student had to perform by offering a fuel of threads but to a piece of cloth. Some of the {{sÅ«trakÄras|sÅ«trakÄras}} and commentators drop hints that garments were used or could be used as {{upavÄ«ta|upavÄ«ta}}. {{Ä€p. Dh. S.|Ä€p.Dh.S.}} (II. 2. 4. 22-23) says that a householder should always wear an upper garment and then adds ‘or the sacred thread may serve the purpose of an upper garment.’ This shows that originally {{upavÄ«ta|upavÄ«ta}} meant an upper garment and not merely a cord of threads. In another place the same sÅ«tra says (II. 8. 19.12) ‘one (who partakes of {{Å›rÄddha|Å›rÄddha}} dinner) should eat covered with an upper garment slung over {{tha|the}} left shoulder and passing under the right arm’. {{Haradatta|Haradatta}} gives two explanations of this, viz. that one should wear an upper garment (while dining at a {{Å›rÄddha|Å›rÄddha}}) like a {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} i. e. under the right arm and over the left shoulder, that is, a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} cannot rely on {{Ä€p. Dh. S.|Ä€p.Dh.S.}} II. 2. 4. 23 and wear at {{Å›rÄddha|Å›rÄddha}} repast only the sacred thread (but he must wear the garment in that fashion) and give up the sacred thread for the time.66 While another view is that he must wear the sacred thread and the upper garment both in the fashion of {{upavÄ«ta|upavÄ«ta}}. {{Ä€p. Dh. S.|Ä€p.Dh.S.}} (I. 2. 6. 18-19) prescribes that when a student wears two garments he should wear one of them (i. e. the upper one) in the {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} mode, but when he wears only one garment then he should wear it round the lower part of the body (and should not cover the upper body with a portion of the garment though it may be long enough for that). {{Ä€p. Dh. S.|Ä€p.Dh.S.}} I. 5. 15. 1 prescribes that a man must be {{yajÃ±opavÄ«tin|yajÃ±opavÄ«tÄ«}} at the time of waiting upon teachers, elders, guests, at the time of {{homa|homa}}, in japa (murmuring prayer), at meals and in taking {{Äcamana|Äcamana}} and at the time of daily {{vedic|Vedic}} study. On this {{Haradatta|Haradatta}} says ‘{{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} means a particular mode of wearing the (upper) garment; if one has no upper garment, then there is another (but inferior) mode stated in {{Ä€p. Dh. S.|Ä€p.Dh.S.}} II.-stick in the evening every day). This shows that in the day of the Manusmá¹›ti, upanayana for women had gone out of practice, though there were faint glimmerings of its performance for women in former days. Relying on the words ‘in former ages’ occurring in the verses of Yama quoted above medieval digests like the Sm. C., the {{Nirá¹‡ayasindhu|Nirá¹‡ayasindhu}} and others say that this practice belonged to another yuga. In BÄá¹‡abhaá¹­á¹­a’s {{KÄdambarÄ«|KÄdambarÄ«}},83 MahÄÅ›vetÄ (who was practising {{tapas|tapas}}) is described as one whose body was rendered pure by (wearing) a {{brahmasÅ«tra|brahmasÅ«tra}} (i. e. {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}})’. The {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} came to have superhuman virtues attributed to it and so probably even women who were practising {{susterities|austerities}} wore it. The {{Saá¹. Pr.|Saá¹. Pr.}} (p. 419) quotes a verse saying that the Supreme Being is called yajÃ±a and {{yajÃ±opavta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} is so called because it belongs to the Supreme Being (or is used in sacrifices for Him).84

vide {{à¤”à¤¶à¤¨à¤¸à¤¸à¥à¤®à¥ƒà¤¤à¤¿|AuÅ›anasasmá¹›ti}} (JÄ«vÄnanda, part I. p.502) ‘à¤…à¤—à¥à¤¨à¥à¤¯à¥-à¤…à¤—à¤¾à¤°à¥‡ à¤—à¤µà¤¾à¤‚ à¤—à¥‹à¤·à¥à¤ à¥‡, à¤¹à¥‹à¤®à¥‡ à¤œà¤ªà¥à¤¯à¥‡ à¤¤à¤¥à¥ˆà¤µ à¤š à¥¤ à¤¸à¥à¤µà¤¾à¤§à¥à¤¯à¤¾à¤¯à¥‡ à¤­à¥‹à¤œà¤¨à¥‡ à¤¨à¤¿à¤¤à¥à¤¯à¤‚ à¤¬à¥à¤°à¤¾à¤¹à¥à¤®à¤£à¤¾à¤¨à¤¾à¤‚ à¤š à¤¸à¤‚à¤¨à¤¿à¤§à¥Œ à¥¤ à¤‰à¤ªà¤¾à¤¸à¤¨à¥‡ à¤—à¥à¤°à¥‚à¥à¤¯ à¤®à¥à¤–à¤®à¥ à¤…à¤—à¥à¤°à¥‡ à¤¬à¥à¤°à¤¾à¤¹à¥à¤®à¤£à¥‹à¤½à¤¨à¥à¤²à¤¿à¤®à¥à¤ªà¥‡à¤¤à¥ à¥¤ à¤¬à¤¾à¤¹à¥‚ à¤°à¤¾à¤œà¤¨à¥à¤¯à¤ƒ à¥¤ à¤‰à¤¦à¤°à¤‚ à¤µà¥ˆà¤¶à¥à¤¯à¤ƒ à¥¤ à¤‰à¤ªà¤¸à¥à¤¥à¤‚ à¤¸à¥à¤¤à¥à¤°à¥€ à¥¤ à¤Šà¤°à¥‚ à¤¸à¤°à¤£-à¤œà¥€à¤µà¤¿à¤¨à¤ƒ à¥¤ à¤†à¤¶à¥à¤µ. à¤—à¥ƒ. III. 8. 11.

Though {{ká¹£atriyas|ká¹£atriyas}} and {{vaiÅ›yas|vaiÅ›yas}} also were entitled to have the upanayana performed, it appears that they often neglected it or at least neglected the constant wearing of {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}}, so much so that from comparatively early times the {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} came to be regarded as the peculiar indicator of the wearer’s being of the {{brÄhmaá¹‡a|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} caste. For example, KÄlidÄsa in Raghuvaá¹Å›a (XI. 64)85 while describing the irate {{brà¤£à¤¾à¤‚ à¤š à¤¸à¤‚à¤§à¥à¤¯à¤¯à¥‹à¤°à¥-à¤‰à¤­à¤¯à¥‹à¤°à¥-à¤…à¤ªà¤¿ à¥¤ à¤‰à¤ªà¤µà¥€à¤¤à¥€ à¤­à¤µà¥‡à¤¨à¥-à¤¨à¤¿à¤¤à¥à¤¯à¤‚ à¤µà¤¿à¤§à¤¿à¤°à¥-à¤à¤· à¤¸à¤¨à¤¾à¤¤à¤¨à¤ƒ à¥¥’

The {{Gobhila gá¹›.|Gobhila-gá¹›.}} (I. 2. 1) in treating of {{upanayana|upanayana}}86 says ’the student takes as {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} a cord of threads, a garment or a rope of {{kuÅ›a|kuÅ›a}} grass’. This indicates that though a cord of threads was considered in {{Gobhila’s|Gobhila’s}} days as the appropriate {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}}, that was not an invariable rule in his day and that a garment could be employed instead. The commentator being brought up in the latter day tradition explains by saying that if the {{sÅ«tra|sÅ«tra}} was lost in a forest then a garment may be worn like the sacred thread and if even that was lost a rope of {{kuÅ›a|kuÅ›a}}. But this appears to be rather far-fetched as an explanation of {{Gobhila’s|Gobhila’s}} unqualified words. {{Manu|Manu}} (II. 44) says that the {{upavÄ«ta|upavÄ«ta}} of a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} should be made of cotton, its strands should be twined with the right hand moved over them (or the twist of the strands must be upwards) and it should have three threads’. {{MedhÄtithi|MedhÄtithi}} comments on this that ‘{{upavÄ«ta|upavÄ«ta}}’ means a particular mode of wearing a garment or a particular position of it and therefore here by {{upavÄ«ta|upavÄ«ta}} is meant that which can be worn in that mode. The {{Sm. C.|Sm.C.}} quotes a prose passage from {{á¹šá¹£yaÅ›á¹›á¹…ga|á¹šá¹£yaÅ›á¹›á¹…ga}} ‘or one may carry out all the purposes for which {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} is required by means of a garment and in its absence by a string of three threads’. From the above passages, from the fact that many of the {{gá¹›hyasÅ«tras|gá¹›hyasÅ«tras}} are entirelyÄhmaá¹‡a|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} hero ParaÅ›urÄma says ‘wearing the {{upavÄ«ta|upavÄ«ta}}, which was the heritage that came to him from his father, and holding a formidable bow that came to him from the side of his mother (who was a {{ká¹£atriya|ká¹£atriya}} princess)’. If the {{upavÄ«ta|upavÄ«ta}} had been as constantly worn by the {{ká¹£atriyas|ká¹£atriyas}} as by {{brÄhmaá¹‡as|brÄhmaá¹‡as}} in KÄlidÄsa’s day he would not have spoken of it as the peculiar sign of a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a|brÄhmaá¹‡a}}. In the drama {{Veá¹‡Ä«saá¹hÄra|Veá¹‡Ä«saá¹hÄra}} (Act III) when Kará¹‡a resented the attitude of AÅ›vatthÄman, who raised his left foot for kicking Kará¹‡a, and said that he could not cut off his leg as by virtue of his caste (as {{brÄhmaá¹‡a|brÄhmaá¹‡a}}) he could not be punished in that way, AÅ›vatthÄman replied by throwing away his sacred thread with the words ‘here do I give up my caste’.87 That shows that in the days of the {{Veá¹‡Ä«saá¹hÄra|Veá¹‡Ä«saá¹hÄra}} (not later than about 600 A. D.) the {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} had become the peculiar indicator of the caste of {{brÄhmaá¹‡as|brÄhmaá¹‡as}} only.

The BaudhÄyana-sÅ«tra quoted in the {{Saá¹skÄra-ratnamÄlÄ|Saá¹skÄra-ratnamÄlÄ}} (p. 188) says that yarn spun by a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} or his maiden daughter is to be brought, then one is to measure first 96 aá¹…gulas of it with the syllable silent about the giving or wearing of the sacred thread in {{upanayana|upanayana}} and from the fact that no mantra67 is cited from the Vedic Literature for the act of giving the {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} (which is now the centre of the {{upanayana|upanayana}} rites), while scores of {{vedic|Vedic}} mantras are cited for the several component parts of the ceremony of {{upanayana|upanayana}}, it is most probable, if not certain, that the sacred thread was not invariably used in the older times as in the times of the later {{Smá¹›tis|Smá¹›tis}} and in modern times, that originally the upper garment was {{need|used}} in various positions for certain acts, that it could be laid aside altogether in the most ancient times and that the cord of threads came to be used first as an option and later on exclusively for the upper {{garnent|garment}}.

The nine {{devatÄs|devatÄs}} of the nine tantus (strands) are given by {{Devala|Devala}} viz. {{Oá¹kÄra|Oá¹kÄra}}, {{Agni|Agni}}, {{NÄga|NÄga}}, {{Soma|Soma}}, {{Pitá¹›s|Pitá¹›s}}, {{PrajÄpati|PrajÄpati}}, {{VÄyu|VÄyu}}, {{SÅ«rya|SÅ«rya}},70 all gods. {{MedhÄtithi|MedhÄtithi}} on {{Manu|Manu}} II.44 says that in {{iá¹£á¹­is|iá¹£á¹­is}}, animal sacrifices and soma sacrifices, the {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} was to have only one thread of three tantus, but it was three-fold in three classes of ahÄ«na, ekÄha, and sattra sacrifices as they required three fires and in the seven somasaá¹sthÄs seven-fold and five-fold when viewed with reference to the three {{savanas|savanas}} and two {{saá¹dhyÄs|saá¹dhyÄs}}. The {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} should reach as far as the navel, should not reach beyond the navel, nor should it be above the chest.71 {{Manu|Manu}} II. 44 and {{Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. I. 50. 10) and then the {{yajÃ±opayita|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} is to be put on with the verse ‘{{yajÃ±opavÄ«tam|yajÃ±opavÄ«tam}} &o.’ Then there is to be a japa of the {{gÄyatri|gÄyatrÄ«}} verse and then Äcamana. For a brief statement of the mode of putting on a fresh sacred thread vide note below.88

The {{Baudhayana-gphyasesasÅ«tra|BaudhÄyana-gá¹›hyaÅ›eá¹£asÅ«tra}} (II. 8. 1-12 ) gives a few insignificant points of difference as to the upanayana of {{ká¹£atriyas|ká¹£atriyas}}, {{vaisyas|vaiÅ›yas}}, {{ambastha|ambaá¹£á¹­ha}} and {{karaÃ±a|karaá¹‡a}} (son of a {{vaisya|vaiÅ›ya}} from a {{sÅ«dra|Å›Å«dra}} female). It is not necessary to go into these details.

The next important question is as to whether upanayana was performed in the case of the blind, the deaf and dumb, the idiotic &c. Jaimini has established that those who are devoid of a limb89 are not eligible for agnihotra, but this inability arises only when the {{defeot|defect}} is incurable. Similarly the Ä€p. Dh.S. II. 6. 14. 1, Gaut.atriya}} and {{vaiÅ›ya|vaiÅ›ya}} should respectively be of cotton, hemp and sheep wool. {{Baud. Dh. S.|Baud.Dh.S.}} (I. 5.5), {{Gobhila gá¹›.|Gobhila-gá¹›.}} (I.2.1) say that it may be of cotton thread or of {{kuÅ›a|kuÅ›a}} grass and {{Devala|Devala}} as quoted in {{Sm. C.|Sm.C.}} says that all twice-born persons should make their {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} of cotton, of {{ká¹£umÄ|ká¹£umÄ}}, hair of cow’s tail, hemp, tree bark or {{kuÅ›a|kuÅ›a}} according to the availability of the material.72 The remarks of the {{Saá¹skÄramayÅ«kha|Saá¹skÄramayÅ«kha}} after quoting {{Manu|Manu}} II. 44 are interesting ‘we do not know the origin of (or authority for) the practice of present-day {{ká¹£atriyas|ká¹£atriyas}} and {{vaiÅ›yas|vaiÅ›yas}}, viz. of wearing cotton {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}}’. This shows that in the 17th century A. D. many {{ká¹£atriyas|ká¹£atriyas}} and {{vaiÅ›yas|vaiÅ›yas}} put on {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}}. 73 {{KumÄrila|KumÄrila}} also says that wearing {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} and studying Vedas is common to all the three {{vará¹‡as|vará¹‡as}}. [^68 28. 41-42, Vas. 17, 52-54, Manu 9. 201, YÄj. II. 140-141, Viá¹£á¹‡u. Dh. S. 15. 32 ff. lay down that persons who are impotent, {{palita|patita}}, congenitally blind or deaf, cripple and those who suffer from incurable diseases are not entitled to share property on a partition, but are only entitled to be maintained. But they all allow even these to marry. As marriage is not possible for {{dvijatis|dvijÄtis}} unless upanayana precedes it, it appears that the {{core.|ceremony}} of upanayana was gone through as far as it could be carried out in the case of the blind, the deaf and dumb &o. The Baud. gá¹›. {{seá¹£asÅ«tra|Å›eá¹£asÅ«tra}} (II. 9) prescribes a special procedure for the upanayana of the deaf and dumb and idiots. The principal [[298]] points in which their upanayana differs from that of others are that the offering of {{samidh|samidh}}, treading on a stone, putting on a garment, the tying of {{mekhala|mekhalÄ}}, the giving of deer skin and staff are done silently, that the boy does not mention his {{Dame|name}}, it is the {{doÄrya|ÄcÄrya}} himself who makes offerings of cooked food or of clarified3]:

The {{game|same}} purana prescribes that {{kunda|kuá¹‡á¸a}} and {{golaka|golaka}} (the first being the offspring of an adulterous intercourse when the husband of the woman is living and the second when the husband is dead) should have upanayana performed in the same way as for the deaf and dumb, if it is known that the adulterer and the woman were both of the {{brÄhmaá¹‡a|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} caste. There is some difference of opinion as to {{kunda|kuá¹‡á¸a}} and {{golaka|golaka}}. Manu ({{II|III}}. 174) defined them as above. Ap. Dh. S, II. 6. 13. 1, Manu (10.5) and Yaj. (I. 90, 92 ) expressly say that a person born of a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} from a brahmana woman belongs to the same caste only if he is born in lawful wedlook and that even the anuloma sons must be born in wedlock.
