The number of {{yajÃ±opavÄ«tas|yajÃ±opavÄ«tas}} to be worn differed according to circumstances. A {{brahmacÄri|brahmacÄrÄ«}} was to wear only one {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} and {{saá¹nyÄsins|saá¹nyÄsins}}, when they kept {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} at all, also wore only one. A {{snÄtaka|snÄtaka}}74 (i {{kunda|kuá¹‡á¸a}} and {{golaka|golaka}} being the offspring of adultery [[299]] would not really be {{brÄhmaá¹‡as|brÄhmaá¹‡as}} even though both parents were brahmanas. Thus, though these two are not strictly {{brÄhmaá¹‡as|brÄhmaá¹‡as}}, upanayana of a secondary kind is allowed to them. Manu (III. 156 ) included the {{kunda|kuá¹‡á¸a}} and {{golaka|golaka}} among {{brÄhmaá¹‡as|brÄhmaá¹‡as}} not to be invited at {{Å›rÄddha|Å›rÄddha}}. Elaborate discussions have been held why they were so specially mentioned, when they were not on Manu’s own dictum {{brÄhmaá¹‡as|brÄhmaá¹‡as}}. Some like the author of the Samgraha held that the {{kunda|kuá¹‡á¸a}} and {{golaka|golaka}} forbidden by Manu in sraddha were those born under the ancient practice of {{ni. e. one who has returned from the teacher’s house after {{brahmacarya|brahmacarya}}) and a house-bolder were to wear two while one who desired long life may wear more than two. {{Vas. Dh. S.|Vas.Dh.S.}} XII. 14 {{days|says}} ‘{{snÄtakas|snÄtakas}} should always wear a lower garment and an upper one, two {{yajÃ±opavÄ«tas|yajÃ±opavÄ«tas}}, should have a stick and a pot filled with water’. {{KaÅ›yapa|KaÅ›yapa}} allowed a householder to wear any number up to ten. Whether {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} as worn in modern times was worn from the most ancient times or not, it is certain that long before the Christian era it had come to be so worn and it had become an inflexible rule that a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} must always wear a {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«yoga|niyoga}}; while others repudiated the idea that those born under the practice of {{niyoga|niyoga}} had any taint attaching to them.90

It is not necessary to go into the question of the upanayana of mixed castes. Manu ( X. 41 ) says that the six {{anulomas|anulomas}} are eligible for the rites of {{drijas|dvijas}} (and so for upanayana ) and the Mit. on Yaj. I. 92 and 95 says that the {{anulomas|anulomas}} have upanayana performed according to the rules of the {{oaste|caste}} of the mother and further mixed castes arising from the six anuloma castes in the anuloma order are also eligible for upanayana. The Baud. gá¹›. {{seá¹£asÅ«tra|Å›eá¹£asÅ«tra}} II. 8 gives rules for the {{upana yana|upanayana}} of {{ká¹£atriyas|ká¹£atriyas}}, {{vaisyas|vaita}} and have his top-knot (of hair) always tied up; if he did any act without observing this rule, it was inefficacious.75 {{Vas.|Vasiá¹£á¹­ha}}76 and {{Baud. Dh. S.|Baud.Dh.S.}} (II. 2. 1) both say that a man must always wear {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}}. If a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} took his meals without wearing {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}}, he had to undergo {{prÄyaÅ›citta|prÄyaÅ›citta}} viz. to bathe, to mutter prayers and fast; vide {{Laghu-HÄrita|Laghu-HÄrÄ«ta}} verse 23 quoted by {{AparÄrks|AparÄrka}} pp. 1171, 1173. The {{Mit.|Mit.}} on {{YÄj.|YÄj.}} III. 292 prescribes {{Å›yas}} and of mixed castes like {{rathakara|rathakÄra}}, {{ambaá¹£tba|ambaá¹£á¹­ha}} &c. Gaut. IV. 21 made an exception in the case of the son of a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} from a {{sÅ«dra|Å›Å«dra}} woman. All {{pratilomas|pratilomas}} were like {{sÅ«dra|Å›Å«dra}} according to Manu (IV. 41 ) and the offspring of a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} from a {{sÅ«dra|Å›Å«dra}} woman, though anuloma, was like {{pratilomas|pratilomas}}. A {{Å›Å«dra|Å›Å«dra}} is only {{ekajÄti|ekajÄti}} and not {{dvijÄti|dvijÄti}} (Gaut. X. 51) and for the {{pratilomas|pratilomas}} ( as well as for the gÅ«dra ) there was no upanayana.

Upanayana wasprÄyaÅ›citta|prÄyaÅ›citta}} for answering calls of nature without having the {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} placed on the right ear (as {{YÄj.|YÄj.}} I, 16 prescribes). {{Manu|Manu}} IV. 66 forbids the wearing of another’s {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} along with several other things (such as shoes, ornament, garland and {{kamaná¸alu|kamaá¹‡á¸alu}}). In {{YÄj.|YÄj.}} (I. 16 and 133) and other {{smá¹›tis|smá¹›tis}} the {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} is called {{brahmasÅ«tra|brahmasÅ«tra}}.

Savitryupadesa - It will be seen from the passage of the {{Sat. Br.|Åšat. Br.}} quoted above (in note 625) that the sacred Gayatri verse was imparted in very {{apoient|ancient}} times to the student by the teacher a year, or six months, 24, 12 or 3 days after upanayana and that the {{Sat. This ancient rule was probably due to the fact that students in those far-off times when first pÄda is quoted in the {{à¤¤à¤¨à¥à¤¤à¥à¤°à¤µà¤¾à¤°à¥à¤¤à¤¿à¤•|TantravÄrtika}} p. 896.

An interesting question is whether women ever had {{upanayana|upanayana}} performed or whether they had to wear the {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}}. Several {{smá¹›tis|smá¹›tis}} contain instructive dicta on this point.77 The {{HÄrÄ«tadharmasÅ«tra|HÄrÄ«tadharmasÅ«tra}} as quoted in the {{Sm. C.|Sm.C.}} and other digests says ’there are two sorts of women, those that are {{brahmavÄdinÄ«s|brahmavÄdinÄ«s}} (i. e. they came to the teacher at the age of seven or eight had hardly any previous instruction and so must have found it difficult to pronounce properly and {{correotly|correctly}} the sacred verse immediately on initiation. It is for this reason that so modern a work as the S. R. M. (p. 194) says that such mantras as ‘{{San-no devirabhiá¹£taye|Åšaá¹ no devÄ«r abhiá¹£á¹­aye}}’ ({{á¹šg|á¹šg}}. X. 9. 4) which have to be repeated by the {{brahmacÄrl|brahmacÄrÄ«}} in the rites of upanayana should be taught students of sacred lore) and those that are {{sadyovadhÅ«s|sadyovadhÅ«s}} (i. e. who straightway marry). Out of these {{brahmavÄdinÄ«s|brahmavÄdinÄ«s}} have to go through {{upanayana|upanayana}}, keeping fire, {{vedic|Vedic}} study and begging in one’s house (i. e. under the parental roof); but in the case of {{sadyovadhÅ«s|sadyovadhÅ«s}} when their marriage is drawing near, the mere ceremony of {{upanayana|upanayana}} should somehow be performed and then their marriage should be celebrated.’ In the {{Gobhila gá¹›.|Gobhila-gá¹›.}}78 II. 1. 19 it is said “leading forward towards the sacred fire (from the house) the bride who is wrapped in a robe and wears the sacred thread (slung from her to him even before upanayana, just as the wife (who being a woman had no upanayana performed ) was taught the Vedic mantras accompanying such acts as that of examining {{ajya|Äjya}} in sacrifices. The same rule of postponing the {{instruc tion|instruction}} in Gayatri is stated in {{SÄn. gá¹›.|ÅšÄn. gá¹›.}} II. 5, {{MÄnava|MÄnava}} gá¹›. I. 22. 15, {{BhÄr. gá¹›.|BhÄr. gá¹›.}} I. 9, {{PÄr. gá¹›.|PÄr. gá¹›.}} II. 3. The general practice however seems to have been to impart the Gayatri that very day. According to most of the sÅ«tras the teacher sits to the north of the fire facing the east left shoulder, in the {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} mode) he (the husband) should murmur the verse ‘{{Soma|Soma}} gave her to {{Gandharva|Gandharva}}’ ({{á¹šg.|á¹šg.}} X. 85. 41)”. It is clear that the girl, according to {{Gobhila|Gobhila}}, wore the {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} as a symbol of the rite of {{upanayana|upanayana}}. The commentator to whom this procedure naturally seemed strange explains ‘{{yajÃ±opavÄ«tinÄ«m|yajÃ±opavÄ«tinÄ«m}}’ as meaning ‘whose upper garment is worn in the fashion of the sacred thread’. In the {{Saá¹skÄratattvÄ|Saá¹ and in front of him the student sits facing the west and then the student requests the teacher to recite to him the Verse sacred to Savitr and the {{toacher|teacher}} imparts to him first one {{pÄda|pÄda}} at a time, then two pÄdas and then the whole. According [[301]] to Baud. gá¹›. II. 5. 34-37 the student places on the fire four fuel sticks of the {{palÄÅ›a|palÄÅ›a}} or other {{saorificial|sacrificial}} tree anointed with clarified butter and repeats mantras to Agni, VÄyu, Aditya and the lord of {{vratas|vratas}} with ‘{{svÄhÄ|svÄhÄ}}’ when offering {{til|to}} them. Various intricate modes occur in the sskÄratattva}} of {{Raghunandana|Raghunandana}} it is stated that {{HariÅ›armÄ|HariÅ›armÄ}} held that according to {{Gobhila|Gobhila}} the bride was to wear a {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}}, though {{Raghunandana|Raghunandana}} himself does not approve of this explanation. In the ceremony of {{SamÄvartana|SamÄvartana}}, {{Ä€Å›v. gá¹›.|Ä€Å›v.gá¹›.}} III. 8,79 on the subject of applying ointment says ‘after having smeared the two hands with ointment a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a|brÅ«tras and the commentaries thereon about how this first instruction in {{GÄyatri|GÄyatrÄ«}} is to be carried out. The differences are due to the place assigned to the {{vyÄbrtis|vyÄhá¹›tis}} ({{bbÅ«á¸¥|bhÅ«á¸¥}}, {{bhuvaá¸¥|bhuvaá¸¥}}, {{svaá¸¥|svaá¸¥}}). Two illustrations are set out below from {{SudarÅ›ana|SudarÅ›ana}} on Ap. gá¹›. II. 11. The syllable ‘om ’ has been looked upon as very sacred from ancient times and is a symbol of the Supreme Being. The {{Tai. Br.|Tai. Br.}} II. 11 contains {{Ähmaá¹‡a}} should salve his face first, a {{ká¹£atriya|ká¹£atriya}} his two arms, a {{vaiÅ›ya|vaiÅ›ya}} his belly, a woman her private parts and persons who gain their livelihood by running, their thighs’. It is improper to say, as some do, that as to women this is a general rule interpolated in the treatment of {{samÄvartana|samÄvartana}} and has nothing to do with the latter. We should rather hold that {{Ä€Å›v.|Ä€Å›v.}} knew of women undertaking Vedic study and so prescribed what they should do in their {{samÄvartana|samÄvartana}}. In the {{MahÄbhÄrata|MahÄbh&|a}} eulogy of ‘{{omkara|oá¹kÄra}}’ and quotes {{á¹šg|á¹šg}}. I. 164.39 in support {{rco aksare parame|á¹›co aká¹£are parame}} &c.’ taking the word ‘{{aksara|aká¹£ara}}’ to mean the ‘{{omkara|oá¹kÄra}}’. The {{Tai. Up.|Tai. Thereforesyllable) om is Brahma; om is all this (universe). A {{brahmana|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} about to begin the teaching (of the Veda) says ‘om’ with the idea that he may reach {{pear|near}} brahma. ‘Om’ is called pranava. Ap. Dh. S. I. 4. 13. 6 says ‘{{omkara|oá¹kÄra}} is the door to [[302]] heaven {{brahmavÄdinÄ«|brahmavÄdinÄ«}} women had {{upanayana|upanayana}} performed in the 8th year from conception, then they studied Vedic lore and finished student-hood at the age of puberty. {{Yama|Yama}}82 says ‘in former ages, tying of the girdle of {{muÃ±ja|muÃ±ja}} (i. e. {{upanayana|upanayana}}) was desired in the case; therefore one who is about to study the Veda, should start his study by first repeating om.’ Manu (II. 74 ) prescribes the repetition of pranava at the beginning and at the end of daily Vedic study and further on (II. 76 ) says that the three letters ‘a’, ‘u’,’m’(in om) and the three {{vyÄbftis|vyÄhá¹›tis}} were distilled from the three vedas by PrajÄpati as the essence. of maidens, they were taught the {{vedes|Vedas}} and made to recite the {{SÄvitrÄ«|SÄvitrÄ«}} (the sacred {{GÄyatrÄ«|GÄyatrÄ«}} verse); either their father, uncle or brother taught them and not a stranger and begging was prescribed for a maiden in the house itself and she was not to wear deer-skin or bark garment and was not to have matted hair’. {{Manu|Manu}} seems to have been aware of this usage as prevalent in ancient times, if not his own. Having spoken of the {{saá¹skÄ {{MedbÄtitbi|MedhÄtithi}} on Manu (II. 74 ) says that ‘om must be uttered at the {{begin.|beginning}} of the daily Vedic study by the student or by the house holder ( when performing {{brahmayajÃ±a|brahmayajÃ±a}}), but it is not necessary in {{japa|japa}}’ &o. In the {{MÄrkandeya-purÄá¹‡a|MÄrkaá¹‡á¸eya-purÄá¹‡a}} chap. 42, Vayu chap. 20, {{ras|saá¹skÄras}} from {{jÄtakarma|jÄtakarma}} to {{upanayana|upanayana}}, {{Manu|Manu}} winds up (II. 66) ’these ceremonies were to be performed in their entirety for women also, but without mantras’ and adds (II. 67) ’the ceremony of marriage is the only {{saá¹skÄra|saá¹skÄra}} performed with Vedic mantras in the case of women; (in their case) attendance on the husband amounts to serving a guru (which a student had to do) and performance of domestic duties toVá¹­ddha-Harsta-smrti|Vá¹›ddha-HÄrÄ«ta-smá¹›ti}} VI. 59-62 and in numerous other places there are hyperbolical or esoterio identifications of the three letters of om with Viá¹£á¹‡u, Laká¹£mi and the jive, with the three Vedas, with the three worlds, with three fires &o. In the {{Kathopaniá¹£ad|Kathopaniá¹£ad}} I. 2. 1 worship of fire’ (which the student had to perform by offering a fuel-stick in the evening every day). This shows that in the day of the {{Manusmá¹›ti|Manusmá¹›ti}}, {{upanayana|upanayana}} for women had gone out of practice, though there were faint glimmerings of its performance for women in former days. Relying on the words ‘in former ages’ occurring in the verses of {{Yama|Yama}} quoted above medieval digests like the {{5-17 om is said to be the end of all Vedas, the source of the knowledge of brahma and also its symbol.

The sacred Gayatri verse is {{á¹šg|á¹šg}}. III. 62. 10 and {{oocurs|occurs}} in the other Vedas also. It is addressed to Savitr (the sun) and may also be interpreted as a prayer to the Source and Inspirer of everything. It literally means ‘we contemplate that esteemed (longed for)Sm. C.|Sm.C.}}, the {{Nirá¹‡ayasindhu|Nirá¹‡ayasindhu}} and others say that this practice belonged to another {{yuga|yuga}}. In {{BÄá¹‡abhaá¹­á¹­a’s KÄdambari|BÄá¹‡abhaá¹­á¹­a’s KÄdambarÄ«}},83 {{MahÄÅ›vetÄ|MahÄÅ›vetÄ}} (who was practising tapas) is described as one refulgence ( glory ) of the divine Savitr who may inspire our intellects (or actions)’. Some gá¹›hya sÅ«tras prescribe the same {{vorge|verse}} for all students, whether {{brÄhmaá¹‡as|brÄhmaá¹‡as}}, {{ká¹£atriyas|ká¹£atriyas}} or {{vaisyas|vaiÅ›yas}}. But others say that for {{brÄhmaá¹‡a|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} the {{SÄvitrl|SÄvit whose body was rendered pure by (wearing) a {{brahmasÅ«tra|brahmasÅ«tra}} (i. e. {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}})’. The {{yajÃ±opavÄ«ta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} came to have superhuman virtues attributed to it and so probably even women who were practising {{susterities|austerities}} wore it. The {{Saá¹. Pr.|Saá¹.Pr.}} (p.rÄ«}} verse is the {{GÄyatri|GÄyatrÄ«}}, but for {{ká¹£atriyas|ká¹£atriyas}} and {{vaisyas|vaiÅ›yas}}, a {{sÄvitri|sÄvitrÄ«}} ( verse addressed to Savitr) in the {{Triá¹£Ã§ubh|Triá¹£á¹­ubh}} (having 11 syllables in each quarter 419) quotes a verse saying that the Supreme Being is called {{yajÃ±a|yajÃ±a}} and {{yajÃ±opavta|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} is so called because it belongs to the Supreme Being (or is used in sacrifices for Him).84

On the 4th day after upanayana a rite was performed called {{MedbÄjangna|MedhÄjanana}} ( generation of intelligence) by virtue of which [[306]] it was supposed that the student’s intellect was made capable of mastering Vedic lore. {{A$v|Ä€Å›v}}. gá¹›. As thou art the preserver of the treasure of sacrifice for the gods, thus may I become the preserver of the treasure of Veda among men’. The {{BhÄr. gá¹›}} should repeat it. {{Sandhya|Saá¹dhyÄ}}:-On the day of upanayana there is no morning {{saidhyÄ|saá¹dhyÄ}}. Jaimini says ‘as long as there is no imparting of the Gayatri there is no {{sandhya|saá¹dhyÄ}}.’ So the student begins his {{samdbya|saá¹dhyÄ}} in the noon of the day of upanayana. As however on that day he knows no Vedic text except the {{GÄyatri|GÄyatrÄ«}}, his whole {{samdhyÄ|saá¹dhy-à¤¤à¤¤à¥-à¤¸à¤µà¤¿à¤¤à¥à¤°à¥-à¤µà¤°à¥‡à¤£à¥à¤¯à¤®à¥ à¥¤ à¤“à¤‚ à¤­à¥à¤µà¤ƒ à¤­à¤°à¥à¤—à¥‹ à¤¦à¥‡à¤µà¤¸à¥à¤¯ à¤§à¥€à¤®à¤¹à¤¿ à¥¤ à¤“à¤‚ à¤¸à¥à¤µà¤ƒ à¤§à¤¿à¤¯à¥‹ à¤¯à¥‹ à¤¨à¤ƒ à¤ªà¥à¤°à¤šà¥‹à¤¦à¤¯à¤¾à¤¤à¥ à¥¤ à¤“à¤‚ à¤­à¥‚à¤¸à¥-à¤¤à¤¤à¥-à¤¸à¤µà¤¿à¤¤à¥à¤°à¥-à¤µà¤°à¥‡à¤£à¥à¤¯à¤‚ à¤­à¤°à¥à¤—à¥‹ à¤¦à¥‡à¤µà¤¸à¥à¤¯ à¤§à¥€à¤®à¤¹à¤¿ à¥¤ à¤“à¤‚ à¤­à¥à¤µà¤ƒ à¤§à¤¿à¤¯à¥‹ à¤¯à¥‹ à¤¨à¤ƒ à¤ªà¥à¤°à¤šà¥‹à¤¦à¤¯à¤¾à¤¤à¥ à¥¤ à¤“à¤‚ à¤¸à¥à¤µà¤ƒ à¤¤à¤¤à¥-à¤¸à¤µà¤¿à¤¤à¥à¤°à¥-à¤µà¤°à¥‡à¤£à¥à¤¯à¤‚ à¤­à¤°à¥à¤—à¥‹ à¤¦à¥‡à¤µà¤¸à¥à¤¯ à¤§à¥€à¤®à¤¹à¤¿ à¤§à¤¿à¤¯à¥‹ à¤¯à¥‹ à¤¨à¤ƒ à¤ªà¥à¤°à¤šà¥‹à¤¦à¤¯à¤¾à¤¤à¥ à¥¤’ this is one method; another is to put the {{vyahrtis|vyÄhá¹›tis}} at the end e.g. ‘à¤“à¤‚ à¤¤à¤¤à¥-à¤¸à¤µà¤¿à¤¤à¥à¤°à¥-à¤µà¤°à¥‡à¤£à¥à¤¯à¤‚ à¤­à¥‚à¤ƒ à¥¤ à¤“à¤‚ à¤­à¤°à¥à¤—à¥‹ à¤¦à¥‡à¤µà¤¸à¥à¤¯ à¤§à¥€à¤®à¤¹à¤¿ à¤­à¥à¤µà¤ƒ à¥¤ à¤“à¤‚ à¤§à¤¿à¤¯à¥‹ à¤¯à¥‹ à¤¨à¤ƒ à¤ªà¥à¤°à¤šà¥‹à¤¦à¤¯à¤¾à¤¤à¥ à¤¸à¥à¤µà¤ƒ à¥¤ à¤“à¤‚ à¤¤à¤¤à¥-à¤¸à¤µà¤¿à¤¤à¥à¤°à¥… à¤§à¥€à¤®à¤¹à¤¿ à¤­à¥‚à¤ƒ à¥¤ à¤“à¤‚ à¤§à¤¿à¤¯à¥‹ à¤¯à¥‹Ä}} worship consists of the {{GÄyatri|GÄyatrÄ«}},

The word ‘{{samdhya|saá¹dhyÄ}}’ literally means ’twilight’, but also indicates the action of prayer performed in the morning and evening twilight. This act is generally styled ‘{{samdhyopÄsana|saá¹dhyopÄsana}}’ or ‘{{samdhyÄvandana|saá¹dhyÄvandana}}’ or simply ‘{{sandhya|saá¹dhyÄ}}.’ This act of adoration is sometimes prescribed as necessary thrice a day viz. at day-break, in the noon and at sun-set e.g. Atri says’& twice born person possessed of the knowledge of the Self should perform three {{gamdhy&|saá¹dhyÄ}} adorations.’ These are respectively named {{GÄyatri|GÄyatrÄ«}} ( morning one), {{Savitri|SÄvitrÄ«}} ( noon) and Sarasvati [[313]] ( evening one) by Yoga-yÄjÄavalkya. à¤¨à¤ƒ à¤ªà¥à¤°à¤šà¥‹à¤¦à¤¯à¤¾à¤¤à¥ à¤­à¥à¤µà¤ƒ à¥¤ à¤“à¤‚ à¤¤à¤¤à¥-à¤¸à¤µà¤¿à¤¤à¥… à¤ªà¤¾à¤¤à¥ à¤¸à¥à¤µà¤ƒ à¥¥’; compare Bhar.gá¹›. I. 9. {{à¤ªà¥‹. à¤—.|Baud.gá¹›.}} II.5.40 is the same as {{à¤†à¤ª. à¤—.|Ä€p.gá¹›.}} 11. 11. {{à¤¸à¥à¤µ:|svaá¸¥}} is often pronounced as {{à¤¹à¤µà¤¾|suvaá¸¥}}. à¤“à¤®à¥ à¤‡à¤¤à¤¿ à¤¬à¥à¤°à¤¹à¥à¤® à¥¤ à¤“à¤®à¥ à¤‡à¤¤à¥€à¤¦à¤‚ à¤¸à¤°à¥à¤µà¤®à¥ à¥¤ … à¤“à¤®à¥ à¤‡à¤¤à¤¿ à¤¬à¥à¤°à¤¾à¤¹à¥à¤®à¤£à¤ƒ à¤ªà¥à¤°à¤µà¤•à¥à¤·à¥à¤¯à¤¨à¥à¤¨à¥-à¤†à¤¹ â€” à¤¬à¥à¤°à¤¹à¥à¤® à¤‰à¤ªà¤¾à¤ªà¥à¤¨à¤µà¤¾à¤¨à¤¿ à¤‡à¤¤à¤¿ à¥¤ à¤‰à¤ªà¤¾à¤ªà¥à¤¨à¥‹à¤¤à¤¿ à¤à¤µ à¤¬à¥à¤°à¤¹à¥à¤® à¥¤ â€” {{à¤¤à¥‡. à¤‰.|Tai.Up.}} I.8. {{à¤¯à¥‹à¤—à¤ªà¤¤à¥à¤°|YogasÅ«tra}} I. 27 declares ‘à¤¤à¤¸à¥à¤¯ à¤µà¤¾à¤šà¤•à¤ƒ à¤ªà¥à¤°à¤£à¤µà¤ƒ’. à¤“à¤™à¥à¤•à¤¾à¤°à¤ƒ à¤¸à¥à¤µà¤°à¥à¤—-à¤¦à¥à¤µà¤¾à¤°à¤‚ à¤¤à¤¸à¥à¤®à¤¾à¤¦à¥-à¤¬à¥à¤°à¤¹à¥à¤® à¤…à¤§à¥à¤¯à¥‡à¤·à¥à¤¯à¤®à¤¾à¤£ à¤à¤¤à¤¦à¥-à¤†à¤¦à¤¿ à¤ªà¥à¤°à¤¤à¤¿à¤ªà¤¦à¥à¤¯à¥‡à¤¤ à¥¤ â€” {{à¤†à¤ª. à¤§. à¤¸à¥‚.|Ä€p.Dh.S.}} I.4 Generally however the {{sandhya|saá¹dhyÄ}} prayer is prescribed twice ( AÅ›v. gá¹›. III. 7, Ap. Dh. S. I. 11. 30. 8, Gaut. II. 17, Manu II. 101, Yaj. I. 24-25 &o).

All prescribe that the morning prayer is to be begun before sunrise and should be carried on till the disc of the sun is seen on the horizon and the evening prayer begins when the {{diso|disc}} of the sun is about to set and goes on up to the appearance of stars. This is the most proper time; but a secondary time was allowed up to three {{ghaá¹­ikÄs|ghaá¹­ikÄs}} after sunrise and sun-set. The duration of the prayer each time was to be one {{muhÅ«rta|muhÅ«rta}} ( i. e. two {{ghaá¹­ikÄs|ghaá¹­ikÄs}}, according to Yoga-yÄjÄavalkya ) whatever the length of the day may be. Manu (IV. 93-94) however recommends the prayer to extend as long as one could afford, since the ancient sages {{seoured|secured}} long life, intelligence, glory, fame and spiritual eminence by long {{samdhyÄ|saá¹dhyÄ}} prayers.

According to most writers japa of Gayatri and other sacred.13.6. {{à¤®à¥‡à¤§à¤¾.|MedhÄ.}} on à¤®à¤¨à¥ II.74 ‘à¤¸à¤°à¥à¤µà¤¦à¤¾-à¤®à¤¹à¤£à¤‚ {{à¤®à¤¾à¤ªà¤¯à¤¨à¤µà¤¿à¤§à¤¿à¤®à¤¾à¤¤à¥à¤°à¤§à¤°à¥à¤®à¥‹à¤‚|à¤ªà¥à¤°à¤¾à¤¯à¤£-à¤µà¤¿à¤§à¤¿-à¤®à¤¾à¤¤à¥à¤°-à¤§à¤°à¥à¤®à¥‹}} à¤¯à¤¥à¤¾ à¤¸à¥à¤¯à¤¾à¤¤à¥ à¥¤ … à¤…à¤¤à¥‹ à¤¹à¥‹à¤®-à¤®à¤¨à¥à¤¤à¥à¤°-à¤œà¤ª-à¤¶à¤¾à¤–à¤¾à¤¨à¥à¤µà¤šà¤¨-à¤¯à¤œà¥à¤à¤¾à¤¦à¥€à¤¨à¤¾à¤®à¥ à¤†à¤°à¤®à¥à¤­à¥‡ à¤¨à¤¾à¤¸à¥à¤¤à¤¿ à¤ªà¥à¤°à¤£à¤µà¥‹à¤½à¤¨à¥à¤¯à¤¤à¥à¤°à¤¾à¤ªà¤¿ à¤‰à¤¦à¤¾à¤¹à¤°à¤£à¤¾à¤¦à¤¿à¤•-à¤µà¤¾à¤•à¥à¤¯-à¤µà¥à¤¯à¤¾à¤¹à¤¾à¤°à¥‡.’ In the {{à¤®à¤¾à¤£à¥à¤¡à¤•à¥à¤¯à¥‹à¤ªà¤¨à¤¿à¤·|MÄá¹‡á¸Å«kyopaniá¹£ad}} (12) and in {{à¤—à¥Œà¤°à¤ªà¤¾à¤µ’ à¤•à¤¾à¤°à¤¿à¤•à¤¾|Gauá¸apÄda’s KÄrikÄ}} (I. 24-29) à¤“à¤™à¥à¤•à¤¾à¤° is identified with the supreme Brahma.

[[300]] {{Manu|Manu}} (II. 74) prescribes the repetition of {{pranava|praá¹‡ava}} at the beginning and at the end of daily Vedic study and further on (II. 76) says that the three letters ‘a’, ‘u’, ’m’ (in {{om|oá¹}}) and the three {{vyÄbftis|vyÄhá¹›tis}} were distilled from the three {{vedas|Vedas}} by {{PrajÄpati|PrajÄpati}} as the essence. {{MedbÄtitbi|MedhÄtithi}} on {{Manu|Manu}} (II. 74 mantras is the principal thing in {{sandhya|saá¹dhyÄ}} and other things such as {{mÄrjana|mÄrjana}} are merely subsidiary, but MedhÄtithi on Manu II. 101 ( where the words are ‘{{japanstiá¹£thet|japaá¹s tiá¹£á¹­het}}’ and not ‘* {{tiá¹£than japet|tiá¹£á¹­han japet}}’) says that japa is subsidiary or secondary and the place of the prayer and the posture of the praying person are the principal items. When it is said ‘one should perform the {{sandhya|saá¹dhyÄ}}’ what is meant is that one should contemplate the deity called Aditya represented by the orb of the sun and should also contemplate on the fact that the same Intelligence dwells in his heart. The proper place for {{samdhyÄ|saá¹dhyÄ}} prayer is outside the village ( Ap. Dh. 8. I. 11. 30. 8, Gaut. II. 16, {{Manava|MÄnava}} gá¹›. I. 2. 2), in a lonely place ( {{San. gá¹›.|ÅšÄn. gá¹›.}} II 9. 1 ‘araá¹‡ye’) or on a river or other sacred spot (Baud. gá¹›. II. 4. 1). But this does not apply to the {{agnihotrin|agnihotrÄ«}}, who has to perform {{vedio|vedic}} rites and repeat homa [[314]] mantras at sun-rise and who therefore may perform {{samdhyÄ|saá¹dhy) says that ‘{{om|oá¹}} must be uttered at the {{begin.|beginning}} of the daily Vedic study by the student or by the house holder (when performing {{brahmayajÃ±a|brahmayajÃ±a}}), but it is not necessary in {{japa|japa}}’ &c. In the {{MÄrkandeya-purÄá¹‡a|MÄrkaá¹‡á¸eya-purÄá¹‡a}} chap. 40, {{Vayu|VÄyu}} chap. 20, {{Vá¹­ddha-Harsta-smrti|Vá¹›ddha-HÄrÄ«ta-smá¹›ti}} VI. 59-62 and in numerous other places there are hyperbolical or esoteric identifications of the three letters of {{om|oá¹}} with {{Viá¹£á¹‡u|Viá¹£á¹‡u}}, {{Laká¹£mi|Laká¹£mÄ«}} and the {{jive|jÄ«va}}, with the three Vedas, with the three worlds, with three fires &c. In the {{Kathopaniá¹£ad|Kaá¹­hopaniá¹£ad}} I. 2. 15-17 {{om|oá¹}} is said to be the end of all Vedas, the source of the knowledge of brahma and also its symbol.

The sacred {{Gayatri|GÄyatrÄ«}} verse is {{Rg.|á¹šg.}} III. 62. 10 and {{oocurs|occurs}} in the other Vedas also. It is addressed to {{Savitr|Savitá¹›}} (the sun) and may also be interpreted as a prayer to the Source and Inspirer of everything. It literally means ‘we contemplate that esteemed (longed for) refulgence (glory) of the divine {{Savitr|Savitá¹›}} who may inspire our intellects (or actions)’. Some {{gá¹­hya sÅ«tras|gá¹›hyasÅ«Ä}} adoration in his house. Vasiá¹£tha quoted by AparÄrka says that {{samdhyÄ|saá¹dhyÄ}} performed in a cowpen or on a river or near the shrine of Viá¹£á¹‡u ( or other deity ) respectively is ten times, 100000 of times or numberless times better than {{sandhyÄvandana|saá¹dhyÄvandana}} in the house. All {{presoribe|prescribe}} that the morning {{samdhya|saá¹dhyÄ}} is to be performed standing and the evening one in a sitting posture ( Ä€Å›v. gá¹›, III. 7. 6, ÅšÄn. gá¹›. II. 9. 1 and 3, Manu II. 102 ) and the morning {{samdhyÄ|saá¹dhyÄ}} is to be performed facing the east and the evening one facing the northwest ( Ä€Å›v. gá¹›. III. 7. 4, {{SÄn. gá¹›.|ÅšÄn. gá¹›.}} II. 9. 1). He is to bathe, to sit in a pure spot on a seat of kusa grass, should have the sacred cord in the usual position and restrain his speech ( i, o, should be silent and not talk in the midst of {{samdhyÄ|saá¹dhyÄ}}).

The principal constituents of {{samdhyopasana|saá¹dhyopÄsana}} are these : Äcamanas (sipping of water ), {{prÄá¹‡ÄyÄma|prÄá¹‡ÄyÄma}}, {{mÄrjana|mÄrjana}} thrice ( sprinkling himself with water to the accompaniment of several mantras ), {{aghamará¹£ana|aghamará¹£aá¹‡a}}, offering of water to the sun (arghya ), japa of {{Gayatrl|GÄyatrÄ«}}, and {{upasthÄns|upasthÄnas}} ( reciting mantras by way of worship of the sun in the morning and generally of Varuá¹‡a in the evening). Among the earliest references to {{SamdhyopÄsana|Saá¹dhyopÄsana}} is the one in the {{Tai. Ar.|Tai. Ä€r.}} II. 2, where it is said thattras}} prescribe the same {{vorge|verse}} for all students, whether {{brÄhmaá¹‡as|brÄhmaá¹‡as}}, {{ká¹£atriyas|ká¹£atriyas}} or {{vaisyas|vaiÅ›yas}}. But others say that for {{brÄhmaá¹‡a|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} the {{SÄvitrl|SÄvitrÄ«}} verse is the {{GÄyatri|GÄyatrÄ«}}, but for {{ká¹£atriyas|ká¹£atriyas}} and {{vaisyas|vaiÅ›yas}}, a {{sÄvitri|sÄvitrÄ«}} (verse addressed to {{Savitr|Savitá¹›}}) in the {{Triá¹£Ã§ubh|Triá¹£á¹­ubh}} (having 11 syllables in each quarter) or {{Jagati|JagatÄ«}} (12 syllables in each quarter) should be the proper one. Here again there is difference. According to the commentators on the {{Kathaka gá¹›.|KÄá¹­haka-gá¹›.}} (41.20), the {{verge|verse}} ‘{{adabdhebhiá¸¥ savita|Ädabdhebhiá¸¥ savitaá¸¥}}’ ({{Kathakam|KÄá¹­hakam}} IV. 10) and the {{verge|verse}} ‘{{ViÅ›vÄ rÅ«pÄpi|viÅ›vÄ rÅ«pÄá¹‡i}}’ ({{Kathakam|KÄá¹­hakam}} XVI. 8) are cited as the {{Savitri|SÄvitrÄ«}} for a {{ká¹£atriya|ká¹£atriya}} and a {{vaisya|vaiÅ›ya}} respectively; while the commentator on {{SÄn. gá¹›.|ÅšÄá¹….gá¹›.}} (II. 5. 4-6) says that the {{Trisá¹­ubh|Triá¹£á¹­ubh}} which is to be taught as the {{SÄvitri|SÄvitrÄ«}} to the {{ká¹£atriya|ká¹£atriya}} students is ‘{{& ká¹›á¹£pena rajasÄ|Ä ká¹›á¹£á¹‡ena rajasÄ}}’ ({{Rg.|á¹šg.}} I. 35. 2) and the {{Jagati Savitri|JagatÄ« SÄvitrÄ«}} for the {{valáº¥ya|vaiÅ›ya}} is ‘{{Hiranya pÄá¹‡iá¸¥ savita|hiraá¹‡yapÄá¹‡iá¸¥ savitÄ}}’ ({{Rg.|á¹šg.}} I. 35. 9) when {{brahmavÄdins|brahmavÄdins}} facing the east throw up water consecrated by the Gayatri, the evil spirits that fight with the sun are sent tumbling into the country (called) Mandeha Aruá¹‡a (of the evil spirits). This shows that in ancient times {{samdhyÄ|saá¹dhyÄ}} consisted principally of offering water (arghya ) to the sun in worship and japa of Gayatri. Ä€Å›v. gá¹›. III.7.3-6, {{San. gá¹›.|ÅšÄn. gá¹›.}} II. 9,1-3 and others refer only to the japa of the GÄyatri mantra in [[315]] {{SamdhyopÄsana|Saá¹dhyopÄsana}}. {{Manava|MÄnava}} gá¹›. Modern writers went on adding details e. g. it is now the practice in the Deccan to repeat the 24 names of Viá¹£á¹‡u at the very beginning of the {{samdbyopÄsana|saá¹dhyopÄsana}}, but this is hardly any where prescribed by any smrti or early commentator. Similarly elaborate rules are laid down about mystic {{nyÄsas|nyÄsas}} with the sixteen verses of the Puruá¹£asukta ( vide Apararka p. 140 ), of the {{nyÄsa|nyÄsa}} of the NÄrÄyapamantra of 25 letters on 25 parts of the body ({{Vá¹›ddha-HÄrita|Vá¹›ddha-HÄrÄ«ta}} VI. 16-19 ) and of the letters of the {{GÄyatri|GÄyatrÄ«}} (Bá¹›hatparÄÅ›ara chap. In modern times Äcamana {{ia|is}} performed with the three names of Viá¹£á¹‡u, viz. {{KoÅ›ava|KeÅ›ava}}, Narayana and Madhava in the form ‘om {{KesavÄya namaá¸¥|KeÅ›avÄya namaá¸¥}}.’ The 24 names are given below.

A few words on each of the or ‘{{hamsaá¸¥ Å›uciá¹£ad|haá¹saá¸¥ Å›uciá¹£ad}}’ ({{Rg.|á¹šg.}} IV. 40. 5). According to the {{VÄrabagÄ¼hya|VÄrÄha-gá¹›hya}} (5) ‘{{devo yÄti savita|devo yÄti savitÄ}}’ and ‘{{yuÃ±jate manaá¸¥|yuÃ±jate manaá¸¥}}’ ({{Rg.|á¹šg.}} V. 81.1) are the {{Triá¹£tubh|Triá¹£á¹­ubh}} and {{Jagat|JagatÄ«}} meant as {{Savitri|SÄvitrÄ«}} for the {{ká¹£atriya|ká¹£atriya}} and {{vaisya|vaiÅ›ya}} respectively. According to {{Satatapa|ÅšÄtÄtapa}} quoted in the {{MadanapÄrijÄta|MadanapÄrijÄta}} (p. 23) the verse ‘{{Deva savitaá¸¥|deva savitaá¸¥}}’ ({{Tai. According to {{MedbÄtithi|MedhÄtithi}} on {{Manu|Manu}} II. 38: ‘{{& krsÃ±ena|Ä ká¹›á¹£á¹‡ena}}’ ({{Rg.|á¹šg.}} I. 35. 2) and ‘{{visva rÅ«pÄá¹‡i|viÅ›vÄ rÅ«pÄá¹‡i}}’ ({{Kathakam|KÄá¹­hakam}} XVI. 8) are the two {{SÄvitris|SÄvitrÄ«s}} respectively for {{ksatriya|ká¹£atriya}} and {{vaibya|vaiÅ›ya}}.

[[301]] That all these rules about the {{SÄvitrÄ«|SÄvitrÄ«}} being in the {{GÄyatrÄ«|GÄyatrÄ«}}, {{Tristubh|Triá¹£á¹­ubh}} and {{JagatÄ«|JagatÄ«}} metres for the three {{vará¹‡as|vará¹‡as}} respectively are probably very ancient follows from the text ‘{{gÄyatrgÄ brÄhmaá¹‡amassajata triá¹£tubbÄ rÄjanyam|gÄyatryÄ brÄhmaá¹‡am asá¹›jata triá¹£á¹­ubhÄ rÄjanyam}} &c.’ (quoted in note 356 above). The {{Ä€Å›v. gá¹›.|Ä€Å›v.gá¹›.}}, {{Ä€p. gá¹›.|Ä€p.gá¹›.}}, and some other {{sÅ«tras|sÅ«tras}} are entirely silent on the point, while {{PÄr, gá¹›.|PÄr.gá¹›.}} II. 3 allows an option viz. all {{vará¹‡as|vará¹‡as}} may learn the {{GÄyatri|GÄyatrÄ«}} or the {{Savitri|SÄvitrÄ«}} verses in the {{Gayatri|GÄyatrÄ«}}, {{Tristubh|Triá¹£á¹­ubh}} and {{JagatÄ«|JagatÄ«}} respectively.98 Why the {{GÄyatri|GÄyatrÄ«}} verse ({{Rg.|á¹šg.}} III. principal components of {{sandhyÄ|saá¹dhyÄ}} may be said here. Elaborate rules about acamana are laid down in several {{smptis|smá¹›tis}} ; vide Gaut. I. 35-40, Ap. Dh. S. I. 5. 15. 2-11 and 16. 1-16, Manu II. 58-62, Yaj. I. 18-21. Such rules must have been elaborated from very ancient times. The {{Tai. Br.|Tai. Br.}} (I. 5. 10) prescribes that one should not perform Äcamana with water that is in a cleft of the earth. Ap. Dh. S. (1. 5. 15. 5) says the same thing. One should perform [[316]] acamana in a sitting posture ( and not standing nor bent) in a pure spot, facing the north or east, one should sip water {{thriloo|thrice}} with water that is not hot and that is free from foam or bubbles, one should after sipping water wipe the lips twice (thrice according to Ä€p.)and should touch with the wet right hand one’s eyes, ears, nose, heart and head. The water for {{doamana|Äcamana}} should be as much as would penetrate (or reach) to the heart in the case of {{brahmaá¹‡as|brÄhmaá¹‡as}}, to the throat in the case of {{ká¹£atriyas|ká¹£atriyas}}, to the palate in the case of {{vaisyas|vaiÅ›yas}}; women and {{sÅ«dras|Å›Å«dras}} should sip on occasions of {{donmana|Äcamana}} only once as much water as would reach the palate, Manu (II. 18) and YÄj. (I. 18) say that water should be sipped by the {{brÄhmatirtha|brÄhmatÄ«rtha}} (i. e, from the root of the thumb). The further elaborate rules laid down in such {{smstis|smá¹›tis}} as Gobhila gá¹›. (L 2.5-6), Saá¹…khasmrti 10 are not set out here. The occasions for acamana are many. According to {{Gobbila|Gobhila}} gá¹›. I. 1.2 one must do every gá¹›hya rite with {{yajÃ±opevita|yajÃ±opavÄ«ta}} worn in the usual way and after {{doamana|Äcamana}}. Haradatta on Ap. Dh. S. I. 5. 15. 1 says that acamana is a subsidiary matter in all religious acts. There are several occasions when the sipping of water twice is necessary, the principal being before and after bhojana ( meals ); vide Gautama I, 40, Vas. 3. 38, Yaj. I. 196, Sm. O. I. p. 100, MadanapÄrijÄta p. 57, Par. M. I. part 1. pp. 241-243. Both the {{Bá¹›. Up.|Bá¹›. Up.}} (VI. 1. 14 ) and {{Chan. Up.|ChÄn. Up.}} V. 2. 2 refer to the {{praotice|practice}} of sipping water before and after bhojana and the VedantasÅ«tra III. 3. 18 is based on these upaniá¹£ad passages and says that water is looked upon as the garment of prÄá¹‡a. Numerous occasions when acamana is necessary are stated in Ap. Dh. S. I. 5. 16. 15-16, Manu V. 138 and 145, Yaj. I. 196 62. 10) came to be famous it is difficult to say. Its fame was probably due to its grand simplicity and to its adaptability to an idealistic conception of the world as emanating from an all-pervading Intelligence. The {{Gopatha BrÄbmana|Gopatha-BrÄhmaá¹‡a}} (I. 32-33) explains the {{GÄyatri|GÄyatrÄ«}} in various ways. In the {{Tai. Ä€r.|Tai.Ä€r.}} (II. 11) it is stated that the {{mystio|mystic}} words ‘{{bhÅ«á¸¥|bhÅ«á¸¥}}. {{bhuvah|bhuvaá¸¥}}, {{svaá¸¥|svaá¸¥}}’ are the truth (essence) of speech and that {{Savita|SavitÄ}} in the {{Gayatri|GÄyatrÄ«}} means ‘one who {{en. genders|engenders}} glory’.99 {{Atharvaveda|Atharvaveda}} 19. 71. 1 calls it ‘{{vedamata|vedamÄtÄ}}’ and prays that it may {{confor|confer}} long life, glory, children, cattle &c, on the singer.100 The {{Br. Up.|Bá¹›.Up.}} V, 14. 1-6101 contains a sublime eulogy of {{GÄyatri|GÄyatrÄ«}} which word is there derived from ‘{{gaya|gaya}}’ meaning ‘{{prÄá¹‡a|prÄá¹‡a}}’, and the root ‘{{trai|trai}}’ (to save) and it is said that when the teacher repeats the {{GÄyatri|GÄyatrÄ«}} for the benefit of the young student {{be|he}} thereby saves the boy’s life (from ignorance and the effects of sin). The {{Ä€p. Dh. S.|Ä€p.Dh.S.}} I. 1. 1. 10 {{montions|mentions}} a {{brahmana|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} text to the effect that the {{Savitri|SÄvitrÄ«}} is recited for all the Vedas (i.e. by its recital all Vedas become recited as it is their essence). {{Manu|Manu}} II. 77 (={{Vispu Dh. S.|Viá¹£á¹‡u-Dh.S.}} 55. 14) says that each {{pada|pÄda}} of the {{Savitri|SÄvitrÄ«}} was as if extracted from each of the three Vedas and that (II. 81={{Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S.|Viá¹£á¹‡u-Dh.S.}} 55, 15) the {{Savitri|SÄvitrÄ«}} together with {{om|oá¹}} and the three mystic syllables ({{bhÅ«á¸¥|bhÅ«á¸¥}} &c.) is the mouth of Vedic lore ({{sinoe|since}} Vedic study starts with it, or ‘{{brabna|brahma}}’ may mean ’the Supreme Being’). {{Manu|Manu}} II. 77-83, {{Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. 8.|Viá¹£á¹‡u-Dh.S.}} 55. 11-17, {{Saá¹…khasmrti|Saá¹…kha-smá¹›ti}} XII, {{Saá¹varta|Saá¹varta}} (verses 216-223), {{Bphat-ParÄÅ›ara|Bá¹›hat-ParÄÅ›ara}} chap. V (where the mystical significance of each of the 24 letters of the {{GÄyatri|GÄyatrÄ«}} is explained) and numerous102 other works contain hyperbolical eulogies of {{Gayatri|GÄyatrÄ«}}. {{ParÄÅ›ara|ParÄÅ›ara}} V.1 calls it ‘{{Vedamata|VedamÄtÄ}}’. The words ‘{{Apo jyoti rasonrtam brahina bhur-bhuvah svarom|Ä€po jyotÄ« raso’má¹›taá¹ brahma bhÅ«r-bhuvaá¸¥ svar-oá¹}}’ are said to be the {{tiras|Å›iras}} (head) of the {{Gaya tri|GÄyatrÄ«}} (vide {{Saá¹…khasmrti|Saá¹…kha-smá¹›ti}} XII. 12). {{Manu|Manu}} (II, KÅ«rmapurÄá¹‡a I. 2. 13. 1-8 &c.

[[317]] {{PrÄnÄyÄma|PrÄá¹‡ÄyÄma}} (restraint of breath ) is defined by the Yoga sÅ«tra (II 49 ) as the regulation of inhalation and exhalation of air. Gaut. I. 50 prescribes three {{prÄá¹‡ÄyÄmas|prÄá¹‡ÄyÄmas}}, each of which lasts for 15 matras (moras). Baud. Dh. S. IV. 1. 30 ( = Vas. Dh. 8. 25. 13={{Saá¹…khasmrti|Saá¹…khasmá¹›ti}} VII. 14 ) and Yaj. I. 23 say that the Åširas of {{GÄyatri|GÄyatrÄ«}}, the three {{vyÄbftis|vyÄhá¹›tis}} each preceded by ‘om’ and the Gayatri verse are to be rehearsed mentally during the time of {{prÄá¹‡ÄyÄma|prÄá¹‡ÄyÄma}}. According to Yoga-yÄjÃ±avalkya, one should first revolve in the mind the seven {{vyabftis|vyÄhá¹›tis}}, each preceded by ‘om,’ then the Gayatri and then the {{diras|Å›iras}} of {{GÄyatri|GÄyatrÄ«}}, {{PrÄnÄyima|PrÄá¹‡ÄyÄma}} has three components, {{pÅ«raka|pÅ«raka}} ( inhaling the outside air ), {{kumbhaka|kumbhaka}} ( keeping in the inhaled air i. e, neither taking in air nor giving it out) and {{reoaka|recaka}} (exhaling air from the lungs ). Manu VI. 70-71 highly praises the utility of {{prÄá¹‡ÄyÄma|prÄá¹‡ÄyÄma}} in purifying the mind of sin.

{{MÄrjana|MÄrjana}} is performed by means of kusa grass dipped in water kept in a vessel of copper or udumbara wood or earthen. ware and while doing so one is to repeat ‘om,’ the {{vyÄbrtis|vyÄhá¹›tis}}, Gayatri and the three verses ‘{{apo hi á¹£thÄ|Äpo hi á¹£á¹­hÄ}}’ ({{á¹šg|á¹šg}}. X, 9,1-3). Baud. Dh. S. II. 4. 2 adds more Vedic mantras for {{mÄrjana|mÄrjana}}. {{MÄnava|MÄnava}} gá¹›. I. 1.24, YÄj. I. 22 and others prescribe {{mÄrjana|mÄrjana}} only with the three verses {{apo bi á¹£tha|Äpo hi á¹£á¹­hÄ}}’({{á¹šg|á¹šg}}. X, 9, 1-3).

{{Aghamará¹£ana|Aghamará¹£aá¹‡a}} (driving out sin) consists in taking water in the right hand formed in the shape of a cow’s ear, holding it {{noar|near}} one’s nose, breathing out from the nose on the water (with the idea of driving away sin from oneself) to the {{accom paniment|accompaniment}} of the three verses’ {{ftam ca|á¹›taá¹ ca}}’ ({{á¹šg|á¹šg}}. X. 190.1-3) and then casting the water away to one’s left on the ground.

Arghya ( offering water out of {{respeot|respect}} to the sun ) {{conaists|consists}} in taking water in one’s joined hands, repeating the Gayatri verse over it and standing {{faoing|facing}} the sun and casting [[318]] it up thrice. If a man cannot have water ( being at the time of {{SamdhyÄ|Saá¹dhyÄ}} on a road or in jail &o.) he could use dust for water. The {{Tai. Ä€r.|Tai. Ä€r.}} II. 2 says that a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} contemplating the rising and setting sun and doing obeisance to it by going from left to right attains all bliss, since this Aditya is Brahma,

As to japa of Gayatri, vide above under {{Savitryupadega|SÄvitryupadeÅ›a}} (p. 304). There is an extensive literature about the greatness of the japa of {{GÄyatri|GÄyatrÄ«}} and of other holy vedic mantras which are passed over bere and for which reference may be made to A parÄrka pp. 46â€“48, Sm, C. I. pp. 143–152, gá¹›. R. pp. 241-250, {{AhnikaprakÄÅ›a|Ä€hnikaprakÄÅ›a}} pp. 311-316. A few details will be given under {{Ähnika|Ähnika}},

{{AstoupasthÄna|UpasthÄna}} vide above(.104) and others prescribe that every day a {{brahmacÄri|brahmacÄrÄ«}} (as well as others) must perform {{japa|japa}} of {{Gayatrl|GÄyatrÄ«}}, {{Baud. Dh. S.|Baud.Dh.S.}} II 4. 7-9 prescribes that in the evening {{SandhyÄ|SandhyÄ}} adoration one should murmur the {{GÄyatri|GÄyatrÄ«}} a thousand times, or a hundred times with {{prÄá¹‡ÄyÄma|prÄá¹‡ÄyÄma}} each time or ten times with ‘{{om|oá¹}}’ and the seven {{vyahrtis|vyÄhá¹›tis}}.103 {{Vas. Dh. S.|Vas.Dh.S.}} 26. 15 {{proscribes|prescribes}} that a man desirous of purifying himself from sin should repeat the divine {{Gayatri|GÄyatrÄ«}} 1000 times (daily) as the maximum or 100 times (as the middle way) or at least ten times. There are mantras for invoking the {{GÄyatri|GÄyatrÄ«}} and for taking leave of it.104

  • The {{à¤†à¤µà¤¾à¤¹à¤¨à¤®à¤¨à¥à¤¤à¥à¤°|ÄvÄhanamantra}} acc. to à¤—à¥‹à¤­à¤¿à¤² is ‘à¤†à¤¯à¤¾à¤¹à¤¿ à¤µà¤°à¤¦à¥‡ à¤¦à¥‡à¤µà¤¿ à¤…à¤•à¥à¤·à¤°à¥‡ à¤¬à¥à¤°à¤¹à¥à¤®-à¤µà¤¾à¤¦à¤¿à¤¨à¤¿ à¥¤ à¤—à¤¾à¤¯à¤¤à¥à¤°à¥€ à¤›à¤¨à¥à¤¦à¤¸à¤¾à¤‚ à¤®à¤¾à¤¤à¤°à¥-à¤¬à¥à¤°à¤¹à¥à¤®-à¤¯à¥‹à¤¨à¥‡ à¤¨à¤®à¥‹à¤½à¤¸à¥à¤¤à¥ à¤¤à¥‡ à¥¥’ quoted in {{à¤¸à¥à¤®à¥ƒà¤¤à¤¿à¤š|Smá¹›ticandrikÄ}}, (p.315, note 748). According to Baud. the worship of the sun is done with the verses’{{udvayam|udvayaá¹}}’ ({{á¹šg|á¹šg}}. I. 50.10), ‘{{Udu tyam|Udu tyaá¹}}’ ({{á¹šg|á¹šg}}. I. 50.1),’{{citram|citraá¹}}’({{á¹šg|á¹šg}}. I. 115.1), ‘{{tao-caká¹£ur|taccaká¹£ur}}’ ({{á¹šg|á¹šg}}. {{VIL|VII}}. 66. 16), ‘{{ya udagat|ya udagÄt}}’ ({{Tai. Ä€r.|Tai. Ä€r.}} IV. 42, 5). Manu II. 103 {{presoribes|prescribes}} that he who does not perform the {{SardhyopÄsana|Saá¹dhyopÄsana}} in the morning and evening should be excluded from all actions meant for the benefit or honour of dvijas. Gobhila smá¹›ti II. 1 says the same and adds that {{brÄhmanya|brÄhmaá¹‡ya}} resides in the three {{sathdhyÄs|saá¹dhyÄs}} and that he who has no concern for {{samdhyopasana|saá¹dhyopÄsana}} is not a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a|brÄhmaá¹‡a}}, Baud. Dh. 8. II. 4. 20 calls upon the religious king to make {{brÄbmapas|brÄhmaá¹‡as}}, who do not engage in {{samdhyopasana|saá¹dhyopÄsana}} thrice a day, perform the work peculiar to {{sadras|Å›Å«dras}}. The {{KÅ«rma-purÄá¹‡a|KÅ«rma-purÄá¹‡a}} goes so far as to say that even if a person engages in other actions which are religious but gives up the performance of {{samdhyopÄsana|saá¹dhyopÄsana}}, in so doing he falls into numerous hells. Manu declares (II. 102) that sins committed at night through ignorance ( or oversight) are removed by the performance of [[319]] morning {{samdhyÄ|saá¹dhyÄ}} and the sins committed in the day are removed by the evening {{sandhyÄ|saá¹dhyÄ}}. Baud. Dh. 8. II. 4. 25-28, Yaj. III, 307 are to the same effect. When a person is impure owing to mourning or birth in the family, he is to perform {{sandhyd|saá¹dhyÄ}} only up to arghya to the sun but not japa nor {{upasthÄna|upasthÄna}}.

In modern times the {{samdhyopasana|saá¹dhyopÄsana}} has become a lengthy business by the addition of materials from purÄá¹‡as and the tantras. But as observed by the {{SamskararatnamÄlÄ|Saá¹skÄraratnamÄlÄ}} rituals like {{nyÄsa|nyÄsa}} are non-Vedic and many do not perform them. For various {{nyÄsas|nyÄsas}} and {{mudrÄs|mudrÄs}} (postures of the fingers, hands eto.) one may consult the {{Smá¹›ti-muktÄphala|Smá¹›ti-muktÄphala}} ( Ähnika pp. 328-333 ), Sm, C. I. pp. 146â€“148.

{{NyÄsa|NyÄsa}} means ‘mentally invoking god and holy texts to come to {{ocoupy|occupy}} certain parts of the body to render it a pure and fit receptacle for worship and contemplation. The sixteen verses of the {{PurusasÅ«kta|Puruá¹£asÅ«kta}} ({{á¹šg|á¹šg}}. X. 90) are respectively invoked to reside in the left and right hands, the left foot, the right foot, the left and right knees, the left and right sides, the navel, the heart, the throat, the left and right arms, the mouth, the eyes and the head ( vide Apararka p. 140 ). The Bhagavata (VI. 8. 4-5) recommends that one should perform {{nyÄsa|nyÄsa}} on the hands and limbs of one’s own body with the two mantras of Narayana and thus make NÄrÄyaá¹‡a one’s armour when some danger arises and that one should perform {{nyÄsa|nyÄsa}} with ‘om’ and other syllables on one’s feet, knees, thighs, belly, heart, chest, mouth and head. The Sm. C. I. p. 144 quotes verses from VyÄsa and BrahmÄ as to the {{nyÄsa|nyÄsa}} of the letters of the Gayatri with ‘om’ and {{namaá¸¥|namaá¸¥}}’ on the several parts of the [[320]] body. {{VÅ›ddha-HÄrita|Vá¹›ddha-HÄrÄ«ta}} VI. 16-19 speaks of the {{nyÄge|nyÄsa}} of the twenty-five letters of the mantra about {{NÄrdyana|NÄrÄyaá¹‡a}} on the twenty-five parts of the body. The {{Nityaofrapaddhati|NityÄcÄrapaddhati}} (pp. 578-579 ) describes the {{nyÄga|nyÄsa}} of the letters of the alphabet (51 in all ) from ‘Om {{KeÅ›avÄya namaá¸¥|KeÅ›avÄya namaá¸¥}}’ to ‘{{ká¹£am Ná¹›simbaya namaá¸¥|ká¹£aá¹ Ná¹›siá¹hÄya namaá¸¥}}.’ One well-known mode is to assign Govinda, {{Mabidhara|MahÄ«dhara}}, {{Há¹‡Å›ikeÅ›a|Há¹›á¹£Ä«keÅ›a}}, Trivikrama, Viá¹£á¹‡u, Madhava respectively on the tips of the thumb, the index finger, the middle finger, the ring finger, the small finger and the middle of the palm, Manu II. 60 enjoining the touching of the limbs and head with water appears to contain the germ of this practice of {{nyÄsa|nyÄsa}}.

The Sm. O. I. (pp. 146â€“148 ) quotes long passages about the {{mudrÅ«s|mudrÄs}} (hand poses) to be made in the {{samI. p. 143) and the {{à¤µà¤¿à¤¸à¤°à¥à¤œà¤¨à¤®à¤¾à¤¤à¥à¤°|visarjanamantra}} is ‘à¤‰à¤¤à¥à¤¤à¤°à¥‡ à¤¶à¤¿à¤–à¤°à¥‡ à¤œà¤¾à¤¤à¤¾ à¤­à¥‚à¤®à¥à¤¯à¤¾à¤‚ à¤ªà¤°à¥à¤µà¤¤-à¤µà¤¾à¤¸à¤¿à¤¨à¤¿ à¥¤ à¤¬à¥à¤°à¤¹à¥à¤®à¤£à¤¾ à¤¸à¤®à¤¨à¥à¤œà¥à¤à¤¾à¤¤à¥‡ à¤—à¤šà¥à¤› à¤¦à¥‡à¤µà¤¿ à¤¯à¤¥à¤¾-à¤¸à¥à¤–à¤®à¥ ’ quoted in the {{à¤—à¥ƒà¤¹à¤¸à¥à¤¥à¤°à¤°à¤®à¤¾à¤•à¤°|Gá¹›hastharatnÄkara}} p. 241. The {{à¤¸à¥à¤®à¥ƒà¤¤à¤¿à¤¥.|Smá¹›tic.}} (I. p. 151) quotes from à¤—à¥‹à¤­à¤¿à¤² ‘à¤®à¤¹à¥‡à¤¶à¥à¤µà¤°-à¤®à¥à¤–à¥‹à¤¤à¥à¤ªà¤¨à¥à¤¨à¤¾ à¤µà¤¿à¤·à¥à¤£à¥‹à¤°à¥-à¤¹à¥ƒà¤¦à¤¯-à¤¸à¤‚à¤­à¤µà¤¾ à¤¬à¥à¤°à¤¹à¥à¤®à¤£à¤¾… à¤¯à¤¥à¥‡à¤šà¥à¤›à¤¯à¤¾’ and also ‘à¤‰à¤¤à¥à¤¤à¤®à¥‡ à¤¶à¤¿à¤–à¤°à¥‡ à¤œà¤¾à¤¤à¥‡ &c.’ These are recited in the modern Sandhya also.

{{BrahmacÄri-dharmÄá¸¥|BrahmacÄri-dharmÄá¸¥}}â€”Certain rules and observances are prescribed for all {{brahmacÄrins|brahmacÄrins}}105. They are of two kinds, some are {{pregoribed|prescribed}} for a very short time and some have to be observed for all the years of studenthood. The {{Ä€Å›v.gá¹›.|Ä€Å›v.gá¹›.}} I. 22.17106 says “for three nights, or twelve nights or a year after {{upanayana|upanayana}} the {{brahmacÄri|brahmacÄrÄ«}} should not eat ‘{{ká¹£Ära|ká¹£Ära}}’ and ‘{{lavana|lavaá¹‡a}}’ and should sleep on the ground”. The same rule is stated by {{Baud. gá¹›.|Baud.gá¹›.}} II. 5. 55 (adding the maintenance of the fire kindled at the {{upanayana|upanayana}} for three days), {{BhÄr. gá¹›.|BhÄr.gá¹›.}} I. 10, {{PÄr. gá¹›.|PÄr.gá¹›.}} II. 5, {{KbÅ«dira gá¹›.|KhÄdira-gá¹›.}} II. 4. 33 (adding avoidance of milk also for three days). {{Hir. gá¹›.|Hir.gá¹›.}} (I. 8. 2) specially prescribes for the first three days the avoidance of {{ká¹£Ära|ká¹£Ära}}, of {{lavana|lavaá¹‡a}} and vegetables, and the duty of sleeping on the ground, of not drinking out of an earthen vessel, of not giving the remnants of his food to {{Å›Å«dras|Å›Å«dras}} and several other observances which he has to continue throughout the period of student-hood. These latter are briefly indicated in {{Manu|Manu}} II. 108 and 176 viz. offering {{samidhs|samidhs}} into fire, begging for food, not using a cot, working for the teacher, daily bath, {{tarpana|tarpaá¹‡a}} of gods, sages and {{pitá¹›s|pitá¹›s}} &c.

The observances that last throughout the period of student hood are first seen in the passage of the {{Sat. Br.|Åšat.Br.}} (quoted above in note 625). The same are laid down in {{AÅ›v, gá¹›,|Ä€Å›v.gá¹›.}} I. 22.2 (quoted on p. 283 above), {{PÄr. gá¹›.|PÄr.gá¹›.}} II. 3, {{Ä€p. MantrapÄtha|Ä€p.MantrapÄá¹­ha}} (II. 6. 14), {{Kathaka gá¹›.|KÄá¹­haka-gá¹›.}} (41. 17) and other texts. These are principally {{acamana|Äcamana}}, {{guruÅ›usrÅ«á¹£Ä|guruÅ›uÅ›rÅ«á¹£Ä}}, {{vÄksaiyama|vÄksaá¹yama}} (silence), {{samidÄdhÄna|samidhÄdhÄna}}. When the teacher says in the {{upanayana|upanayana}} ‘drink water, do work &c’, the student is to reply with the word ‘yes’ (’{{bÄdham|bÄá¸ham}}’ or {{om|oá¹}}). But the {{sÅ«tras|sÅ«tras}} and {{emptis|smá¹›tis}} lay down many rules about these and other observances; vide {{Gaut.|Gaut.}} II, 10-40, {{SÄn. gá¹›.|ÅšÄdhyÄ|saá¹dhyÄ}} adoration. The Samgraha quoted in the PÅ«jÄprakÄÅ›a (p. 123 ) states that the {{mudrÄs|mudrÄs}} are to be made in worship, at the time of japa, {{dhyÄna|dhyÄna}} (contemplation) and when starting on {{kÄmya|kÄmya}} rites ( performed for securing some desired object) and that they tend to bring the deity worshipped near to the worshipper. The names and number of {{mudrÄs|mudrÄs}} differ considerably. For example, the Sm. O. and {{Smr. Mu.|Smá¹›. Mu.}} (abnika pp. 331-332 ) quote passages defining the following {{mudrÄs|mudrÄs}} viz. {{sammukha|sammukha}}, {{sampuá¹­a|sampuá¹­a}}, {{vitata|vitata}}, {{vistirna|vistÄ«rá¹‡a}}, {{dvimukha|dvimukha}}, {{trimukha|trimukha}}, {{adhomukba|adhomukha}}, {{vyÄpakÄÃ±jalika|vyÄpakÄÃ±jalika}}, {{yamapasa|yamapÄÅ›a}}, {{grathita|grathita}}, {{sammukhonmukha|sammukhonmukha}}, {{vilamba|vilamba}}, {{muÈ™tika|muá¹£á¹­ika}}, {{mina|mÄ«na}}, {{kÅ«rma|kÅ«rma}}, {{varÄha|varÄha}}, {{simbÄkrÄnta|siá¹hÄkrÄnta}}, {{mahÄkrÄnta|mahÄkrÄnta}}, {{mudgara|mudgara}} and {{pallava|pallava}}, The {{NityÄcÄrapaddhati|NityÄcÄrapaddhati}} p. 533 derives the word {{mudrÄ|mudrÄ}} from ‘mud’ (joy) and the root ‘rÄ’ (to give ) or ‘{{drÄvay|drÄvayati}}’ (causal of dru, to put to flight) and says that “{{nudra|mudrÄ}}” is 80 called because it gives delight to the gods and also puts to flight asuras (evil beings). That work and the PÅ«jÄ-prakasa (pp. 123-126 ) give the names of {{mudrÄs|mudrÄs}}. They are {{dvahani|ÄvÄhanÄ«}}, {{sthÄpini|sthÄpinÄ«}}, {{samnidhÄpani|sannidhÄpanÄ«}}, {{samrodhini|saá¹rodhinÄ«}}, {{prasÄdamudrÄ|prasÄdamudrÄ}}, {{avagun. thana-mudrÄ|avagunthana-mudrÄ}}, {{sammukba|sammukha}}, {{prarthana|prÄrthanÄ}}, {{sankha|Å›aá¹…kha}}, {{cakra|cakra}}, {{gadÄ|gadÄ}}, {{abja|abja}}, (or {{padma|padma}}), {{musala|musala}}, {{khadga|khaá¸ga}}, {{dhanus|dhanus}}, {{bÄpa|bÄá¹‡a}}, {{nÄraca|nÄrÄca}}, {{kumbha|kumbha}}, {{vighna|vighna}} (for VighneÅ›vara ), {{saura|saura}}, {{pustaka|pustaka}}, {{laká¹£ml|laká¹£mÄ«}}, {{saptajihva|saptajihva}} ( for Agni VaiÅ›vÄnara ), {{durgÄ|durgÄ}}, {{namaskara|namaskÄra}} (bringing together both hands from the wrist to the tips of the fingers ), {{aÃ±jali|aÃ±jali}}, {{samhara|saá¹hÄra}} (in all 32). The {{NitydoÄrapaddhati|NityÄcÄrapaddhati}} (p. 536 ) says that {{sankha|Å›aá¹…kha}}, {{cakra|cakra}}, {{gada|gadÄ}}, {{padma|padma}}, {{musala|musala}}, {{khadga|khaá¸ga}}, srivatsa and kaustubha are the eight {{mudrÄs|mudrÄs}} of Viá¹£á¹‡u. The Sm. C. quotes a [[321]] work {{oalled|called}} Mahasamhita that the {{mudrÄs|mudrÄs}} are not to be performed in the presence of a crowd and if so performed the deities become angry and the {{mudrÄs|mudrÄs}} become fruitless. The SÄradatilaka (23. 106 ) states that all deities are gladdened by the {{mudrÄs|mudrÄs}} and in verses 107-114 describes the following {{mudrÄs|mudrÄs}}, viz. {{dvahani|ÄvÄhanÄ«}}, {{stbÄpani|sthÄpanÄ«}}, {{samnidhÄpani|sannidhÄpanÄ«}}, {{saá¹rodbini|saá¹rodhinÄ«}}, {{sammukha|sammukha}}, {{Bakala|Sakala}}, {{avagunthana|avagunthana}}, {{dhenu|dhenu}}, {{mabÄmudra|mahÄmudrÄ}}. The Acara-dinakara of Vardhamana-sÅ«ri composed in samvat 1468 ( 1411-12 A. D.) for Jainas enumerates 42 {{mudrÄs|mudrÄs}} and defines them ( 1923, part II. pp. 385-386 ).

The influence of these {{mudrÄs|mudrÄs}} spread outside India and they are still practised in the island of Bali. Miss Tyra de Kleen has brought out a very interesting book on the ‘mudrÄs (the hand poses ) practised by Buddhist and Åšaiva priests’ (called pedandas ) in Bali, with 60 full page drawings (1924, New York ).

Study af the Veda :–A detailed examination of the educa tional system from {{anoient|ancient}} times onwards, together with its methods, courses of study and kindred topics will require a volume by itself. The works mentioned in the note below may be read for that purpose. Here a few salient features {{alono|alone}} can be set out.

The pivot of the whole educational system of ancient India was the teacher (variously called {{acÄrya|ÄcÄrya}}, guru, u pÄdhyÄya). The instruction was oral. {{á¹šg|á¹šg}}. VII. 103.5 ( speaking of frogs ) says ‘when one of these frogs follows another in making noise just as a learner repeats the words of the teacher’. Vide the quotations from the Atharvaveda and the {{Sat. Br.|Åšat. Br.}} cited above (f. n. 622 and 625 ). In the beginning the father alone may have taught his son. The story narrated in the {{Br. Up.|Bá¹›. Up.}} V. 2. 1 and the story of Svetaketu {{Aruá¹‡eya|Ä€ruá¹‡eya}} who was taught by his father everything he knew ({{Br. Up.|Bá¹›. Up.}} VI. 2. 1 and 4 ) illustrate this (vide f. n. 633 ). But even from very ancient times the practice [[322]] of sending boys to learn from an {{acÄrya|ÄcÄrya}} had become usual. The {{Chan. Up.|ChÄn. Up.}} itself says in one passage (VI. 1) that {{Svetaketus Ä€ruÃ±eya|Åšvetaketu Ä€ruá¹‡eya}} was placed by his father for twelve years as a student with a teacher. The saine upaniá¹£ad (III. 11. 5 ) says that the father should impart the ‘madhuvidya’ to his eldest son or to a wortby pupil only. As the boy stayed with the teacher in the latter’s house and all instruction was oral, the teacher’s position assumed the greatest importanceá¹….gá¹›.}} II. 6.8, {{Gobhila gá¹›.|Gobhila-gá¹›.}} III. 1.27, {{KhÄdira gá¹›.|KhÄdira-gá¹›.}} II. 5. 10-16, {{Hir. gá¹›.|Hir.gá¹›.}} 8.1-7, {{Ap. Dh. S,|Ä€p.Dh.S.}} I. 1. 3. 11-I. 2.7. 30, {{Baud. Dh. S.|Baud.Dh.S.}} I. 2, {{Manu|Manu}} II. 49-249, {{YÄj.|YÄj.}} I. 16-32 &c. The rules centre principally round {{agnipa ricaryÄ|agniparicaryÄ}} (worshipping fire), {{bhiká¹£Ä|bhiká¹£Ä}} (begging for food), {{samdhyopÄsana|saá¹dhyopÄsana}}, study of Veda and its methods and duration, avoidance of certain foods and drinks and other matters like singing &c., {{guruÅ›usrÅ«á¹£a|guruÅ›uÅ›rÅ«á¹£Ä}} (including honouring him and his family and other elders), and the special {{vratas|vratas}} of the {{brabma cÄri|brahmacÄrÄ«}}. These principal topics will be dealt with in some detail now. Before doing so some other matters will have to be briefly disposed of.

On the 4th day after {{upanayana|upanayana}} a rite was performed called {{MedbÄjangna|MedhÄjanana}}107 (generation of intelligence) by virtue of which it was supposed that the student’s intellect was made capable of mastering Vedic lore.

[[302]] {{A$v. gá¹›.|Ä€Å›v.gá¹›.}} I. 22. 18-19 deals with this. The teacher makes the student sprinkle water in an unobjectionable direction thrice from the left to the right with a pot of water round about a {{palÄÅ›a|palÄÅ›a}} tree that has one root, or round a bunch of {{kuÅ›a|kuÅ›a}} grass if there is no {{palÄÅ›a|palÄÅ›a}}, and makes him repeat the mantra ‘Oh glorious one, thou art glorious. As thou, glorious one, are glorious, thus, glorious one, make me full of glory. As thou art the preserver of the treasure of sacrifice for the gods, thus may I become the preserver of the treasure of Veda among men’.108 The {{BhÄr. gá¹›.|BhÄr.gá¹›.}} (1. 10) also prescribes this rite on the fourth day after {{upanayana|upanayana}} and says that a {{palÄÅ›a|palÄÅ›a}} tree with one root is to be anointed with {{Äjya|Äjya}} and the mantra ‘{{suÅ›ravaá¸¥|suÅ›ravas}}’ (the same as in {{Ä€Å›v.|Ä€Å›v.}}) is to be muttered. {{Kathaka gá¹›.|KÄá¹­haka-gá¹›.}} 41. 18, {{MÄnava gá¹›.|MÄnava-gá¹›.}} I. 22. 17 also refer to this. The {{Sam. Pr.|Saá¹.Pr.}} (pp. 444-446) gives a more elaborate description. In addition to what is stated in {{Ä€Å›v. gá¹›.|Ä€Å›v.gá¹›.}} it quotes from {{Saunaka|Åšaunaka}} and adds a few more details, viz. the student deposits at the root of the {{palÄÅ›a|palÄÅ›a}} his garments already worn, the staff and the girdle and then wears new ones and then when the boy returns to the house, a stream of water is poured before him. The teacher takes the garments &c. left by the boy. The {{Ä€p. gá¹›.|Ä€p.gá¹›.}} 11. 24 says that on the fourth day the teacher takes the garments worn on the day of {{upanayang|upanayana}} by the student and the boy wears new ones. {{Sudarsana|SudarÅ›ana}} on {{Ap.|Ä€p.}} 11. 24 speaks of {{palÄÅ›a-karms|palÄÅ›a-karma}} on the fourth day. The boy goes out with his {{ÄcÄrya|ÄcÄrya}} to the east or north and three {{sthapdilas|sthaá¹‡á¸ilas}} are prepared to the north or east of a {{palÄsa|palÄÅ›a}} tree and on these three {{pranava|praá¹‡ava}} ({{om|oá¹}}), {{sraddhÄ|Å›raddhÄ}} and {{medha|medhÄ}} are invoked, are worshipped with unguents, flowers, lamp &c. (as in the regular worship of an image), then the {{pranava|praá¹‡ava}} is worshipped with the formula ‘{{yas-chandasÄm|yaÅ›-chandasÄm}}’ to ‘{{Å›rutam me gopaya|Å›rutaá¹ me gopÄya}}’ ({{Tai. Up.|Tai.Up.}} I.4.1), {{sraddha|Å›raddhÄ}} with the hymn ‘{{sraddhayÄgniá¸¥|Å›raddhayÄgniá¸¥}}’ {{Rg.|á¹šg.}} X. 151 and {{MedhÄ|MedhÄ}} with the {{anuvÄka|anuvÄka}} ‘{{MedhÄ devi|MedhÄ devÄ«}}’ ({{Tai. Ä€r.|Tai.Ä€r.}} X. 39). Then the staff is deposited at the foot of the {{palÄÅ›a|palÄÅ›a}}, another staff is taken and the student returns to the house with the {{acÄrya|ÄcÄrya}}. In modern times in the Deccan a similar ceremony is gone through under the (Marathi) name ‘Palasula’.

  • {{ay: bas two meaning:|SuÅ›ravas has two meanings:}} (1) ‘glorious,’ (2) ‘who hears well’ (i. e. who learns the Veda well by hearing it from the teacher). This occurs in {{94. . Tr.|Ä€p.Dh.S.}} II. 5.1 also (but in {{179. q. XI. 14|Ä€p.gá¹›. 11.14}} it is the {{free|rite}} for taking the staff).

We have seen above that the student has to offer {{samidh|samidh}} (fuel stick) into the fire on the day of {{upanayana|upanayana}}. The fire kindled at the time of {{upanayana|upanayana}} was to be kept up for three days and the fuel-sticks were offered in that fire during those days. Afterwards {{samidh|samidh}} was to be offered in the ordinary fire (vide {{Baud.. Satyakama JÄbÄla says to his teacher (in {{Chan|ChÄn}}. IV. 9.3) ‘I have heard from persons like your revered self that knowledge when learnt from an {{acÄrya|ÄcÄrya}} reaches the highest excellence. The {{SvetÄÅ›va. taropaniá¹£ad|ÅšvetÄÅ›vataropaniá¹£ad}} ( VI. 23 ) places the guru almost on a level with God and inculcates the highest devotion to him. The Ap. Dh. S. I. 2. 6. 13 says the pupil should wait upon the {{acÄrya|ÄcÄrya}} as if he were “God.’ The story of Ekalavya, whom Droá¹‡a refused to take as a pupil because he was a {{niá¹£Äda|niá¹£Äda}} and who by worshipping the image of {{Dropa|Droá¹‡a}} is alleged to have become an adept in archery, illustrates two points viz. the prevailing notion of the greatness of a guru and the necessity of single minded devotion to him for attaining {{proficienoy|proficiency}} (vide Adiparva 132 for the story and also Droá¹‡aparva 181. 17). The MahÄbhÄrata condemns him who learns the Vedas at home and says that Raibhya became superior to Yavakrita because the former learnt from a guru, while the other did not do {{8o|so}}. In Manu and other {{smstis|smá¹›tis}} there is some divergence about the [[323]] greatness of the {{ÄdÄrya|ÄcÄrya}}. Manu II. 146 ( = {{Viá¹£á¹‡u|Viá¹£á¹‡u}} Dh. 8. 30. 44 ) says that both the father ({{jangka|janaka}}) and the teacher are {{oalled|called}} father ({{pilÄ|pitÄ}}) but the father (i, e. {{ÄcÄrya|ÄcÄrya}}) who imparts the sacred Veda is superior to the father that gives (physical) birth, since the birth in spiritual learning is for a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} of eternal benefit here and hereafter. But in II. 145 Manu says that an {{ÄcÄrya|ÄcÄrya}} is ten times superior to an {{upÄdhyÄya|upÄdhyÄya}}, the father is superior to a hundred {{ÄcÄryas|ÄcÄryas}}, while the mother is thousand-fold superior to the father. Gaut. II. 56 declares that the {{ÄcÄrya|ÄcÄrya}} is the highest among all gurus while according to some the mother {{js|is}} the highest. YÄj. I. 35 also places the mother higher than the {{ÄcÄrya|ÄcÄrya}}. Gaut. I. 10-11, Vas. Dh. S. III. 21, Manu II. 140, Yaj. I. 34 define the {{ÄcÄrya|ÄcÄrya}} as one who performs the {{upana yana|upanayana}} of the student and im parts the whole Veda to him. The Nirukta ( I. 4) derives acÄrya as follows: ‘he makes the student understand the proper course of conduct, or he collects wealth (i. e. fee) from the student (or gathers together the meanings of words ), or he increases the intelligence ( of the student)’. Ä€p. Dh. S. I. 1.1. 14 says ‘The {{ÄcÄrya|ÄcÄrya}} is so called since the student gathers his duties from him.’ Manu II. 69 says that the teacher, after performing {{upa nayana|upanayana}}, teaches his pupil the rules about {{sauca|Å›auca}} (bodily purity), {{ÄcÄra|ÄcÄra}} (rules of conduct in every day life), the offering (of fuel-stick) in fire and {{sandhya|saá¹dhyÄ}} adoration. YÄj. I. 15 is to the same effect. Though the words {{ÄcÄrya|ÄcÄrya}}, guru and {{upÄdhyÄya|upÄdhyÄya}} are very often used as synonyms, {{anoient|ancient}} writers made a distinction between them. According to Manu II. 141 and 142, an {{upadhyÄya|upÄdhyÄya}} is one who teaches to a student a {{por tion|portion}} of the Veda or the VedÄngas (subsidiary lores of the [[324]] Veda ) as a means of his own livelihood and a guru is one who performs the {{samgkÄras|saá¹skÄras}} and who maintains the child. This latter definition shows that guru means the father here. Vas. Dh. 8. (III. 22-23), Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S. 29. 2, and YÄj. I. 35 define {{upÄdhyÄya|upÄdhyÄya}} in the same way as Manu. {{Aooording|According}} to YÄj. I. 34 the guru is one who performs the {{samskÄras|saá¹skÄras}} and imparts the Voda. This corroborates the statement made above that originally the father himself taught the Veda to his son. The word guru is often used in the sense of any elderly person, male or female, who is entitled to respect. {{Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh.|Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh.}} S. ( 32.1-2 ) {{asys|says}} that the father, the mother and the {{acÄrga|ÄcÄrya}} are the three highest gurus of a person and Manu II. 227-237 contain the most sublime glorification of these three, Devala says that among gurus five deserve special honour, viz. father, mother, {{acarya|ÄcÄrya}}, eldest brother and husband ( in the case of women)’. Manu (II. 149 ) says that whoever confers on another the benefit of knowledge, whether great or small, is the latter’s guru.

Upanayana and qualifications of the ÄcÄrya

devoted to the {{Veda|Veda}}, who knows dharma, is born of a good family, who is pure, is a Å›rotriya that has studied his Vedic {{sÄkha|Å›ÄkhÄ}} and who is not lazy. Åšrotriya has been defined above (f. n. 290). Ä€p. Dh. S. II. 3. 6. 4 and Baudh. gá¹›. I. 7. 3 define a Å›rotriya as one who has studied one {{Å›ÄlchÄ|Å›ÄkhÄ}} of a Veda. Vide {{VÄyupurias|VÄyupurÄá¹‡a}} vol. I. 59, 29 also.109 The {{ÄoÄrya|ÄcÄrya}} in {{upanayang|upanayana}} must be a brÄhmaá¹‡a; as to the study of veda one should ordinarily learn the Veda from a brÄhmaá¹‡a teacher; in times of difficulty (i. e. when a brÄhmaá¹‡a is not available) one may learn the Veda from a {{ksatriya|ká¹£atriya}} or {{vaibya|vaiÅ›ya}} teacher; but in such circumstances the only service that a {{brÄbmaá¹‡a|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} student rendered to the guru would be following after the non-brÄhmaá¹‡a teacher; he had not to render bodily service (such as shampooing or washing the feet &c). Vide Ä€p. Dh. S. II. 2. 4. 25-28 (quoted above in note 299), Gaut. 7.1-3, Baudh. Dh. S. I. 2. 40-42, Manu II. 241. Manu II. 238 allows even a {{brÄlmaá¹‡a|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} to learn {{subhÄ|Å›ubhÄ}} vidyÄ (visibly beneficial knowledge) even from a {{sÅ«dra|Å›Å«dra}}, {{Santiparva|ÅšÄntiparva}} 165.31 does the same.110 The Mit. on YÄj. I. 118 remarks that a ká¹£atriya or vaiÅ›ya should teach a {{brahmana|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} only when urged by him and not at his sweet will. AparÄrka (p. 160) says that Manu II. 241 allows only teaching to a ká¹£atriya, but does not allow him to make it a means of his livelihood.111

We saw above (p. 321) that the instruction was entirely oral. The first thing that was taught to the boy was the praá¹‡ava and the {{vyÄbá¹›tis|vyÄhá¹›tis}} and the GÄyatrÄ«. Then the boy was to be taught other parts of the Veda. It is desirable to get out briefly the method of teaching the Veda followed in ancient times. The {{SÄn.|ÅšÄá¹…kh.}} gá¹›. (IV. 8) describes the method as follows: the teacher sits facing the east or north, while the other (i. e. the student) sits to his right facing the north or two students may sit in that way; but if there are more than two they should sit as the available space will allow.112 The student should not sit on a high {{soat|seat}} [[326]]

before the teacher nor on the same seat with him; he should not {{stretoh|stretch}} out his feet, nor should he sit seizing his knees with his arm, nor should he lean against a support, nor should he place his feet on his lap nor should he hold his feet like an axe. After the student utters “Recite, Sir,”113 the teacher should urge him to pronounce ‘oá¹ƒ’; the other (i. e. the student) should reply ‘oá¹ƒ’. Thereafter he (the student) should recite continuously. After reciting he should clasp the teacher’s feet and say ‘we have finished, Sir’ and go away to his business. Some teachers say that the teacher should say ‘Leave, let us stop’. In the {{Rk PrÄtisakhya|á¹šk-prÄtiÅ›Äkhya}},114 15th paá¹­ala, there is a description of the method of teaching the Veda, which closely agrees with the above quotation from the ÅšÄá¹…kh. gá¹›. It adds that the teacher may also sit facing the north-east. When the teacher recites two words or more, the first pupil (to the right of the teacher) repeats the first of the two or more words and the other pupils repeat the rest afterwards. The teacher recites one word if it is a compound, two words if they are un-compounded; the teacher also clearly explains how to recite the words if there is any difficulty; in this way the whole praÅ›na is finished and all the pupils repeat again the whole of it. A praÅ›na generally has three mantras and each {{adhydya|adhyÄya}} has about sixty praÅ›nas. Manu (II. 70-74) also prescribes certain rules: the student should sip water (Äcamana) when about to begin Vedic study, should face the north, should fold both hands115 together (and place them on his knee), should wear light (pure) {{olothes|clothes}}, should at the beginning and end of Vedic study clasp the feet of the teacher with crossed hands

[[327]]

Upanayana and method of teaching

and should touch the right foot of the teacher with his own right hand and left foot with the left hand, should repeat ‘oá¹ƒ’ at the beginning and at the end of Vedic study. The teacher should say to the pupil ‘repeat’ and should stop from teaching with the words ’let there be a pause’. Gaut. (I. 49-58) gives similar rules. The Gopatha Br. I. 31 uses the expression ‘{{Barve veda mukhato gÄ¼hitÄá¸¥|Sarve vedÄ mukhato gá¹›hÄ«tÄá¸¥}}’, which is current even in modern times (it means all Vedas were committed to memory by word of mouth).

The study of the Veda was the first duty of every twice born person (dvijÄti). Vedic Literature had grown to vast proportions even in the times of the Tai. Br. (III. 10.11), as the story of Indra and BharadvÄja cited above (p. 271) shows. The ideal was set up by Manu II. 165, viz. that the whole Veda together with secret doctrines (Upaniá¹£ads) was to be learnt by every dvijÄti. The Åšat. Br. XI. 5. 7 {{oontains|contains}} a eulogy of Veda study (svÄdhyÄya) and the injunction ‘svÄdhyÄyo ‘dhyetavyaá¸¥’ (one must study the Veda) occurs there very frequently. The Ä€p. Dh. S. (I. 4. 12. 1 and 3) quotes116 the Taitt. Ä€. II. 14. 3 that the study of the Veda (svÄdhyÄya) is austerities and also the {{Sat. Br. XI. 5. 6. 8.|Åšat. Br. XI. 5. 6. 3}}. The MahÄbhÄá¹£ya (vol. I. p. 1) quotes a Vedic text ‘a {{brÄhmans|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} should study and understand without any purpose (or desire of reward) dharma and the Veda with its six {{angas|aá¹…gas}}’.117 The MahÄbhÄrata says that a brÄhmaá¹‡a may be deemed to have completely accomplished his duty by the study of the Veda.118 YÄj. I. 40 says that it is Veda alone that confers the highest bliss upon dvijÄtis by enabling them to understand and perform sacrifices, austerities and auspicious {{sots|acts}} (like saá¹ƒskÄras). The MahÄbhÄá¹£ya (vol. I. p. 9) contains the traditional extent of the four Vedas, viz. that there were 101 {{Å›ÄkhÄg|Å›ÄkhÄs}} of the Yajurveda, 1000 of the SÄmaveda, 21 of the {{Rgvoda|á¹šgveda}} and nine of the Atharvaveda.119

[[328]]

Concessions had to be made to the shortness of human life and the weakness of the human mind. Therefore Gaut. II. 51, Vas. Dh. S. VII. 3, Manu III. 2, YÄj. I. 52 and others allowed a person to study only one Veda. After a man studied his own Veda, he could if so minded study another Å›ÄkhÄ of another Veda or other Vedas. The rule laid down by many smá¹›tis is that one should study the {{sakhÄ|Å›ÄkhÄ}} of the Veda which his ancestors studied and should perform religious rites with mantras derived from that {{sÄkha|Å›ÄkhÄ}}. Vide MedhÄtithi on Manu III. 2 and ViÅ›varÅ«pa on YÄj. I. 51.120 That person who does not study a Vedic ÅšÄkhÄ studied by his ancestors and studies another Å›ÄkhÄ altogether was called ‘{{Å›akhÄraá¹‡á¸a|Å›ÄkhÄraá¹‡á¸a}}’. Whatever religious rites a man did with the procedure and {{inantras|mantras}} of another Å›ÄkhÄ giving up his own {{sakha|Å›ÄkhÄ}} becomes fruitless. But an exception was made to the effect that if some121 religious rite was omitted in one’s Å›ÄkhÄ, but was dealt with in another Å›ÄkhÄ and was not opposed to the teaching of one’s Å›ÄkhÄ, it may be performed as in the case of Agnihotra (which is not dealt with in all Å›ÄkhÄs, but is to be performed by all).

Teachers mostly confined themselves to one place. But we find that even in ancient times there were teachers who wandered from one country to another. In the Kauá¹£. Br. Up. IV. 1 we find that the famous BÄlÄki GÄrgya moved about in the countries of {{Usinara|UÅ›Ä«nara}}, Matsya, {{Kuru-PancÄla|Kuru-PaÃ±cÄla}} and {{KÄÅ›ivi deba|KÄÅ›i-Videha}}. In the {{Bs.|Bá¹›.}} Up. III. 3. 1 Bhujyu LÄhyÄyani tells {{Yajnavalkya|YÄjÃ±avalkya}} that he and others wandered about in the country of Madra for study. Students generally stuck to one teacher; but it appears that they sometimes flocked to renowned teachers as waters flow down a slope122 (Tai. Up. I. 4. 3). There were also students who wandered from teacher to teacher and were therefore derisively called ‘{{tirthakaka|tÄ«rthakÄka}} (crows at a sacred place),’ as the {{Mahabhaá¹£ya|MahÄbhÄá¹£ya}} states.123

As the study of the Veda was a duty enjoined upon a {{brahmana|brÄhmaá¹‡a}}, so teaching Veda to another was a duty. MedhÄtithi on Manu (II. 113) quotes a Vedic text124 ‘He who having studied the Veda would not teach one who requests him to do so would be one who destroys his own good acts (i.e. would lose the benefit thereof), would shut the door leading to happiness; therefore he should teach; it leads to great glory’. When SatyakÄma JÄbÄla did not teach his pupil Upakosala anything for twelve years, though the latter served {{asaiduously|assiduously}} by attending to the sacred fires of the teacher, the teacher’s wife remonstrated with the husband by saying ’this student has worked hard and attended the fires, may the fires not {{congure|censure}} you and order you to teach him the vidyÄ he desires’ (ChÄn. Up. IV. 10. 1-2). The PraÅ›na Up.125 VI. 1 gives expression to the view that if a teacher keeps back anything he knows he dries up entirely. The Ä€p. Dh. S. I. 4. 14. 2-3 expressly prescribes ’the teacher whom a student asks for instruction should not refuse him, if he finds no defect in the student.’126 Ä€p. Dh. S. (I. 2. 8. 25-28) lays down certain excellent rules for the teacher “the teacher, anxious for the welfare of the student as if he were his son, should attentively impart learning to the student without hiding anything from him in all matters of duty; nor should the teacher restrain the student for his own work in such a way as to cause obstacles in his study except in seasons of distress. A teacher becomes no teacher if he avoids giving instruction’ (i. e. he may be abandoned). The {{Dronaparva|Droá¹‡aparva}} (50.21) says that a pupil comes only after the son according to the idea of those [[329]]

Upanayana and duty to teach Veda

who know dharma. If a teacher does not teach a pupil any thing even after his pupil has stayed with him for a year, the former receives all the sins of the pupil. A teacher127 who did not teach or was sinful was to be abandoned. Similarly a teacher, who became puffed up, did not care for what should or should not be done and took to a sinful path was to be abandoned.128 Ä€p. I. 1. 1. 13 lays down that a student must stay with his teacher who performs his upanayana till he completes his study, unless the teacher himself swerved from the path of dharma and became a sinner and (I. 2. 7. 26) that if the teacher cannot teach the subject, the pupil may resort to another teacher.

The smá¹›tis lay down rules about the qualifications of a student who deserves to be taught. In the VidyÄsÅ«kta quoted in the Nirukta129 (II. 4) we see that the teacher was not to impart vidyÄ (knowledge) to one who was jealous (or who treated vidyÄ with contempt), was crooked and was not self-restrained and that learning was to be imparted to one who was pure, attentive, intelligent and endowed with brahmacarya (celibacy), who would never prove false (to his teacher) and who would guard what he learnt as a treasure. Manu (II. 109 and 112 also) says that ten persons deserve to be taught viz. the son of the teacher, a student who serves his guru, one who gives some knowledge in exchange, one who knows dharma or who is pure (in body and mind), who is truthful, who is able to study and retain it, who gives money (for teaching), who is well-disposed and who is one’s near relative (agnate). YÄj. I. 28 mentions all these and adds that the student must be grateful, not {{inolined|inclined}} to hate or prove false to the teacher, healthy and not disposed to find fault. The student should always be dependent on [[330]] History of {{Dharmalasira|DharmaÅ›Ästra}}

and under the control of the teacher (as Ä€p. Dh. S.130 I. 1. 2. 19 and NÄrada say) and should stay with no one but the teacher. We saw above (p. 274) that from ancient times the student had to serve the teacher by tending his cattle (ChÄndogya IV. 4. 5), had to beg for food and announce it to the teacher (ibid. IV. 3. 5) and to look after his sacred fires and to learn the Veda only in the time that would be left after doing work for the guru.131 Besides these, the rules concerning his conduct towards the teacher, the teacher’s wife and son, concerning the method of salutation and showing respect, the food, drinks, and actions allowed or prohibited to students are too numerous to be set out in detail. A few important ones from Gautama, Ä€p. Dh. S., Manu II and YÄj. I. 33 are stated below. Gaut. (II. 13, 14, 18, 19, 22, 23, 25) says that the student should speak the truth, bathe everyday, should not look at the sun; should avoid honey, flesh, perfumes, the wearing of flowers, sleeping by day, rubbing oil on the body, putting collyrium in the eyes, going in a cart,132 wearing shoes and holding an umbrella, love affairs, anger, covetousness, infatuation, vain discussions, playing on musical instruments, luxurious baths with hot water, {{metioulous|meticulous}} cleansing of the teeth, {{eostatic|ecstatic}} states of mind, dancing, singing, calumny of others, dangerous places, gazing at women or touching young women, gambling, serving a low person (or doing very low work), injury to animals, obscene or {{barsh|harsh}} talk, wine. Manu (II. 198 and 180-181) prescribes that he should not sleep on a cot and should observe complete celibacy, but if he suffers from night emissions he should bathe, worship the sun and repeat thrice the mantra ‘punar mÄm…’ (Taitt. Ä€r. I. 30). The Ä€p. Dh. S. (I. 1. 2. 21-30, I. 1. 3. 11-24) contains similar rules of conduct. Ä€p. says (I. 1. 2. 28-30) that the student should not wash his limbs with hot water (generally), but he may do so if they are smeared with dirty and impure matter provided he does it out of the sight of the teacher and that he should not bathe in water in a sportive manner, but

[[331]]

Upanayana-qualifications of a good student

133 should not laugh, but if he could not help laughing he should do so covering his face with his hands (says Ä€p.).

Gautama and Baudh. Dh. S. (I. 2. 34 and 37) say that the student is to serve his teacher by following after him when he goes anywhere, he should help the teacher in his toilet and bath and should shampoo his body and take food left by him (ucchiá¹£á¹­a);134 {{lio|he}} should be diligent in doing work that would be pleasing and beneficial to the teacher; he was to study when the {{toacher|teacher}} called him, he was not to cover his throat with a piece of cloth, or was not to sit in the presence of his teacher with his feet on his lap, was not to stretch his feet, he was not to clear loudly his {{tbroat|throat}}, nor to laugh, yawn or crack the knuckles; he was when called by the teacher to reply at once leaving his seat or bed and was to approach the teacher even when he called from a distance; he should always occupy a seat lower than that of his teacher and should go to sleep after his teacher and rise before him (Gautama II. 20-21, 30-32). Manu II. 194-198 and Ä€p. Dh. S. I. 2. 5. 26 and I. 2. 6. 1-12 contain similar rules. Manu (II. 199) says that a pupil should

not mimic the gait, the manner of speech and the actions of the teacher. Manu (II. 200-201) calls upon the pupil to close his ears (with his hands or fingers) or to leave the place where somebody indulges in calumnies about the teacher or points out the faults in him, and states that if the pupil himself finds fault with his teacher or calumniates him, the pupil (in the next life) is born as an ass or a dog. Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S.135 28. 26 says the same.

Some rules are laid down as to how the brahmacÄrin is to deal with the hair on his head. Even the {{Rg.|á¹šgveda}} speaks of boys with several {{sikbÄs|Å›ikhÄs}} (topknot). Vide note 598 above. Gaut. One of the rules for the student was that he was not to pronounce the name of his teacher even when the teacher was not present without prefixing or affixing an {{honorifio|honorific}} addition (such as {{srl|Å›rÄ«}}, {{bhatta|bhaá¹­á¹­a}}, {{McÄrya|ÄcÄrya}}). Gaut. ordains that the student should not speak of his teacher, the teacher’s son or wife or of a man who has been initiated for a srauta sacrifice by their bare names138 and then says that when it is absolutely necessary

Rules of conduct for a student

to refer to {{thege|these}} by name the student should not pronounce the name and gotra of his teacher as they are, but by means of a synonym (e. g. if the teacher’s name is Haradatta or {{Deves. vara|DeveÅ›vara}} the pupil should respectively say DevarÄta or SureÅ›vara). Ä€p. Dh. S. I. 2. 8. 15 says that even after returning home a snÄtaka should avoid touching his former teacher with his fingers (to call his attention), frequent muttering of something in his {{care|ears}}, laughing into his face, calling him out loudly, taking his name, ordering him about.
